# A Field Guide to Wild Edible Plants, Trees, and Fungi of the United States
<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### By Region — Identification, Harvest, Preparation, and Safety

*Published by Seedwarden*

---

*The land was a pharmacy and a pantry long before it was a real estate holding. This guide covers every significant edible plant, tree, shrub, and fungus across all nine US regions. Use it as the encyclopedia it is — but always verify before you eat.*

---

## Important Notice

Misidentifying a wild plant can kill you. That is not an exaggeration, and this guide does not change that fact. Use this as a reference and a starting point, but never eat anything you are not 100% certain you have correctly identified. Photographs and written descriptions are not a substitute for learning from an experienced forager in the field, hands on the actual plant. Cross-reference every identification with at least one additional source before consuming anything. If there is any doubt at all, leave it alone.

---

## Before You Begin: Please Read This

This guide is a starting point, not a certification of safety. Before eating anything:

**Verify with multiple sources.** Cross-reference every identification with at least one additional regional field guide or an experienced forager who can confirm in person.

**Learn the deadly lookalikes first.** Every region has plants that look edible and are not. This guide calls them out explicitly for every species.

**Forage where it is legal.** National parks prohibit all harvesting. Many state and county lands have restrictions. Private land requires permission.

**Harvest sustainably.** Take no more than 10–25% of any wild population. Leave roots intact unless the root is the harvest target.

**When in doubt, leave it.** No wild plant is worth a hospital visit.

---

## How to Use This Guide

Plants are organized by US geographic region. Within each region they are divided into three sections: **PLANTS** (herbs, forbs, vines, and aquatic species), **TREES AND SHRUBS**, and **FUNGI**. Species that appear across multiple regions are listed in full in each region with region-specific notes.

Each entry follows this format:
- **Identification** — key features, what to look for, dangerous lookalikes
- **Where to Find It** — habitat, elevation, associated vegetation
- **Season** — when to harvest
- **Edible Parts** — what is edible and what is not
- **Preparation** — how to process and cook
- **Safety** — critical warnings

---

## A Note on "Native" vs. "Naturalized" Plants

This guide includes both native and naturalized species. Dandelion, garlic mustard, and autumn olive are not native — but they are widespread, abundant, and edible. The practical standard here: if it grows wild, is safe, and is sustainable to harvest, it belongs in this guide.

---

---

# REGION 1: NORTHEAST
*Maine, New Hampshire, Vermont, Massachusetts, Rhode Island, Connecticut, New York, New Jersey, Pennsylvania, Maryland, Delaware, Washington D.C.*

The Northeast is defined by its hardwood forests, river bottomlands, cold streams, and four distinct seasons. Spring is the most productive foraging season — a flush of tender greens and morels emerges before the canopy closes. Fall brings nuts, berries, and mushrooms. Winter's frozen ground hides edible roots for the prepared forager.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ramps / Wild Leeks
*Allium tricoccum*

![Ramps (Allium tricoccum) — broad leaves emerging in moist hardwood forest](images/native-plants/allium-tricoccum.jpg)

**Identification**
Broad, smooth-edged, lance-shaped leaves emerge in early spring from a slender reddish-pink stem. Leaves are 4–10 inches long, 1–3 inches wide, growing in clumps of 2–3 from a thin white bulb. The single most reliable ID feature: crush a leaf and it releases an unmistakable pungent onion-garlic scent. No onion smell = not ramps.

**Where to Find It**
Moist, rich hardwood forest under sugar maple, yellow birch, beech, and basswood. Often colonizes entire hillsides near streams. Rich, loamy, well-drained but moist soil.

**Season**
Mid-April through mid-May. Window is 3–4 weeks. Flowers appear after leaves die back (June–July); the white umbel flower confirms identity for marking future harvests.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: bulbs, stems, and leaves. Flowers also edible.

**Preparation**
Raw leaves in salads, pesto, or as wraps. Bulbs pickled in white wine vinegar with salt and sugar. Sautéed whole in butter — flavor mellows significantly with heat. Grilled whole over open fire. Ramp kimchi.

**Safety**
*Lily of the Valley (Convallaria majalis)* — TOXIC — grows in similar habitat with similar broad leaves. Lacks any onion smell. Crush the leaf: if it smells like flowers or nothing, put it down immediately. The smell test is absolute.

**Notes**
Ramps are slow-growing and over-harvested in popular areas. Harvest only one leaf per plant and leave the bulb, or take no more than 10% of any patch's bulbs. A patch takes 7+ years to recover from heavy harvest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fiddlehead Fern (Ostrich Fern)
*Matteuccia struthiopteris*

![Fiddlehead Fern (Matteuccia struthiopteris) — tightly coiled spring shoots](images/native-plants/matteuccia-struthiopteris.jpg)

**Identification**
Tightly coiled, bright emerald-green fronds emerging from a central crown in early spring. Two structural features confirm *Matteuccia* specifically: (1) a **deep U-shaped groove** (not V-shaped) on the smooth green inner face of the stem; (2) **papery brown sheaths** that wrap the coil loosely and rub off cleanly between your fingers. Stems are smooth and hairless.

**Where to Find It**
Floodplains, stream banks, moist low-lying woodlands. Forms large colonies in river bottomlands. Look where seasonal flooding occurs.

**Season**
2–4 weeks in early-to-mid spring (April–May depending on latitude and elevation). Once coils begin to unfurl, they toughen and become less palatable.

**Edible Parts**
Young tightly-coiled fronds only. Harvest when 4–16 inches tall and still fully coiled.

**Preparation**
Must be cooked — raw fiddleheads cause gastrointestinal upset even when correctly identified. Boil or steam 5–10 minutes. Sauté with butter, garlic, lemon. Add to pasta or stir-fry. Pickle in white wine vinegar brine. Flavor: asparagus meets green bean with an earthy note.

**Safety**
**Cinnamon Fern (*Osmunda cinnamomea*)** produces fiddleheads in similar habitat but has woolly brown sheaths that do not rub off cleanly. Not considered safe to eat. Always confirm the U-shaped groove and clean-rubbing papery sheath. Cook before eating — do not eat raw.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — opposite toothed leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 2–5 feet tall with opposite, coarsely toothed, pointed leaves on a square stem. Identification is immediate and painful: hollow stinging hairs covering all surfaces inject formic acid and histamine on contact. Drooping clusters of inconspicuous green flowers appear from leaf axils. Harvest young shoots in early spring.

**Where to Find It**
Nitrogen-rich disturbed soil — forest edges, stream banks, old farmyards, compost piles, roadsides. Often an indicator of historically rich or disturbed soil. Grows in large patches.

**Season**
Harvest young shoots and top 4–6 inches of growth in early spring (March–May) **before the plant flowers**. Post-flowering nettles develop kidney-irritating alkaloids and should not be eaten.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and tender leaves only, before flowering. Seeds also edible (nutritious, but rarely harvested).

**Preparation**
Wear thick gloves to harvest. Cooking for 5 minutes (boiling, steaming, or blanching) completely neutralizes the sting — cooked plant is safe to handle and eat. Rich flavor, similar to spinach but earthier. Nettle soup with potato and onion. Blanched and pressed into pasta dough. Dried and brewed as tea. Substitute for cooked spinach in any recipe.

**Safety**
**Do not eat after the plant flowers** (late May onward in most of the Northeast). Pre-flower tender shoots are safe and highly nutritious. Wear thick gloves — thin rubber gloves do not stop the hairs.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — basal rosette and flower](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the most recognizable plants in North America. Basal rosette of deeply lobed, toothed leaves arising from a central crown at ground level — no stem leaves, ever. A single hollow leafless stem from the crown bears one yellow flower head, which matures into the familiar spherical seed ball. White milky latex bleeds from any broken stem or leaf. Taproot is long, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, fields, disturbed areas, gardens. Virtually everywhere.

**Season**
Year-round where ground doesn't freeze hard. Young spring leaves are least bitter. Flowers spring through fall. Roots most concentrated in fall.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: leaves, flowers, roots, even flower buds. Young spring leaves least bitter; older leaves more so.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads with vinaigrette (fat cuts bitterness). Older leaves: blanch 2 minutes, sauté with garlic and olive oil. Flowers battered and fried, steeped in sugar syrup for cordial, or made into wine. Flower buds pickled like capers. Roots roasted at 350°F until dark and fragrant, ground as caffeine-free coffee substitute — nutty, slightly bitter, excellent.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. Cats ear (*Hypochaeris radicata*) is similar but has branching stems — also edible. Dandelion is one of the safest and most identifiable plants for beginners. Avoid plants from lawns treated with herbicides.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Forms dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small (1–3 cm), opposite, oval, pointed, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 petals each so deeply notched they appear to be 10. The single most reliable feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node. Visible with a hand lens.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, under shrubs, in disturbed areas, at lawn edges. Common in partially shaded spots with good moisture.

**Season**
Late winter through spring. Often the first edible green of the season — appearing while frost is still possible. Dies back in summer heat; may return in fall.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant. Young plants are most tender; older stems get stringy.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy flavor. Tossed into smoothies. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green combined with more flavorful foraged greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Asparagus
*Asparagus officinalis*

![Wild Asparagus (Asparagus officinalis) — spears emerging along sunny fence line](images/native-plants/asparagus-officinalis.jpg)

**Identification**
Escaped cultivated asparagus — the same species, naturalized for centuries. Spring spears are smooth, green, and identical to supermarket asparagus but often thinner. In late summer and fall, mature plants grow to 3–5 feet as feathery, fern-like masses of fine needle-like foliage that turn golden in fall — this is when to locate and mark patches for the following spring.

**Where to Find It**
Full-sun locations: roadsides, fence lines, field edges, ditches, abandoned homesteads. Once established, a patch persists for decades.

**Season**
Harvest in early spring (March–May) when spears are 4–8 inches tall and before tips open. Scout in fall, return the following spring.

**Edible Parts**
Young spears only. Snap at the natural breaking point where tender meets tough.

**Preparation**
Steam, roast, grill, or sauté. Raw when very young and thin. Wild asparagus flavor is often more intense than cultivated.

**Safety**
Distinctive enough in both spear and mature form that misidentification is unlikely. Smooth green scaling spears with no dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis stricta*

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped leaflets and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Small, low-growing plant with three heart-shaped leaflets arranged like a clover, each with a distinct fold down the center. Immediately distinguished from clover by heart-shaped (not oval) leaflets. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers. The entire plant has a distinctive sharp, lemony-sour taste from oxalic acid — tasting a small leaf is a reliable identification aid.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, gardens, sidewalk cracks, disturbed ground, forest edges. Very common.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Most tender and abundant in spring.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods. Use as seasoning or snack, not as a primary food source.

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail snack or salad ingredient. The sour flavor functions as a lemon substitute — add to salads, grain dishes, cold drinks. Brew in water for a tart wild "lemonade."

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid. Safe in moderate amounts. **Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, arthritis, or kidney disease.** Do not eat large amounts daily. Same caution as spinach and chard.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Milkweed
*Asclepias syriaca*

![Common Milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) — large leaves and spherical flower clusters](images/native-plants/asclepias-syriaca.jpg)

**Identification**
Stout, upright plant 2–5 feet tall with large, opposite, oval leaves 4–8 inches long. Stems and leaves exude white milky latex when broken. Rounded clusters of pink-to-lavender flowers in midsummer develop into distinctive warty, boat-shaped seed pods 3–5 inches long that split open in fall releasing silky-tailed seeds.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, roadsides, disturbed open ground, meadow edges. Full sun, dry to moist soil.

**Season**
Young shoots: spring (May–June). Flower buds and flowers: early summer. Young pods: midsummer when pods are 1–2 inches and still firm inside. Seeds: fall (inner silk edible but mostly caloric curiosity).

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (4–6 inches), flower buds, flowers, very young pods (before silk develops). Do not eat old pods or excessive raw quantities.

**Preparation**
All parts require boiling — change water once to reduce bitterness. Young shoots boiled 5 minutes taste like asparagus. Flower buds boiled briefly, tossed in butter. Young pods boiled, similar to okra. Raw milkweed is mildly toxic — always cook.

**Safety**
**Dogbane (*Apocynum cannabinum*)** — TOXIC — looks similar with opposite oval leaves and milky sap. Dogbane stems are smooth and often reddish; milkweed stems are hairy. Dogbane has narrow tubular white flowers; milkweed has globe-shaped pink clusters. Do not eat dogbane. When in doubt, smell the broken stem — milkweed has a distinctive sweet-bitter smell; dogbane is more acrid.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Garlic Mustard
*Alliaria petiolata*

![Garlic Mustard (Alliaria petiolata) — triangular-toothed leaves and white four-petaled flowers](images/native-plants/alliaria-petiolata.jpg)

**Identification**
A biennial that forms low rosettes of kidney-shaped, scallop-edged leaves in its first year, then sends up a 2–4 foot flower stalk in spring of its second year. Second-year leaves are triangular, sharply toothed. Small white four-petaled flowers. Crush any part of the plant: the distinctive garlicky-mustard odor is the reliable ID feature. Seed pods (siliques) are long and narrow.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, disturbed woodland, stream banks, roadsides, yards. An aggressive invasive throughout the Northeast — foraging it is encouraged.

**Season**
First-year rosette leaves: fall through early spring. Second-year leaves and flower shoots: early spring. Flowers: May. Seeds: late spring–summer.

**Edible Parts**
First-year rosette leaves, second-year young leaves and shoots, flowers, green seed pods, and seeds (use like mustard seed).

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads or as pesto (blended with olive oil, parmesan, lemon, and pine nuts — a classic use). Flowers as garnish. Seeds ground as mustard. Older leaves can be bitter — blanch first.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes when the garlic smell is present. The four white petals and garlic-mustard smell are diagnostic.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Jerusalem Artichoke / Sunchoke
*Helianthus tuberosus*

![Jerusalem Artichoke (Helianthus tuberosus) — tall sunflower plant with knobby tubers](images/native-plants/helianthus-tuberosus.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall, coarse-stemmed sunflower, 5–10 feet tall with rough, alternate leaves and bright yellow sunflower-like flowers 2–3 inches across in late summer. The key feature for the forager is underground: irregularly shaped, knobby tubers connected in clusters, brown-skinned with white flesh, resembling ginger root. Taste is nutty, sweet, mild.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, stream banks, old fields, disturbed ground. Often persists at old homestead sites and garden escapes.

**Season**
Tubers best in fall (after frost sweetens them) through early spring. Dig after the plant dies back — the dead stalks mark the location.

**Edible Parts**
Tubers only. Raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Eat raw (crisp, sweet). Roast whole in coals or oven. Slice thin and pan-fry in fat until golden. Mash. Soup. Pickle. **Warning:** the inulin in Jerusalem artichoke causes significant gas and digestive discomfort in many people, especially when eaten in large quantities raw. Start with small amounts.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes once the tubers are found underground and the above-ground plant confirmed as a sunflower. The gas issue is real — earned the nickname "fartichoke."

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems are hollow and float. The flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Clear springs, spring-fed streams, cold running water. Prefers clean water — **harvest only from verified clean water sources** (upstream of farms, roads, and septic systems).

**Season**
Best in cool months (spring and fall). Available year-round where springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk. No dangerous plant lookalikes when in flowing water, but always verify the habitat is clean.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Burdock
*Arctium minus / Arctium lappa*

![Burdock (Arctium minus) — large dock-like leaves and burr-covered seed heads](images/native-plants/arctium-minus.jpg)

**Identification**
A large biennial with enormous leaves (up to 2 feet across in the first year) — heart-shaped, woolly-white on the undersides, on long hollow petioles. The stout taproot is 1–3 feet long, brown-skinned, white-fleshed. Second year: tall branching stalk (3–6 feet) with smaller leaves and the distinctive round burr-covered seed heads (which gave Velcro its inspiration).

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, roadsides, forest edges, waste places, old fields. Widespread and common.

**Season**
First-year root: fall through early spring. Leaf petioles and young leaves: spring. Young flower stalks of second-year plants: spring, before they elongate.

**Edible Parts**
Root (primary food), leaf petioles (inner core), young second-year flower stalks (peeled).

**Preparation**
Root (gobo in Japanese cuisine): scrub, peel off rough skin, slice thin. Sauté with sesame oil, sake, and soy (kinpira gobo). Add to stews, soups, stir-fries. Mild, earthy, slightly sweet. Leaf petioles: peel the outer stringy skin, boil 10 minutes, use like celery. Flower stalks: peel, boil briefly, eat like asparagus.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once the plant is confirmed with the distinctive burrs (second year) or enormous leaves. First-year plants without burrs — confirm with white woolly leaf undersides and hollow petioles.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicory
*Cichorium intybus*

![Chicory (Cichorium intybus) — bright blue daisy-like flowers and branching stems](images/native-plants/cichorium-intybus.jpg)

**Identification**
A stout perennial with basal rosette of toothed leaves (similar to dandelion but with clasping upper stem leaves). The most distinctive feature: bright blue daisy-like ray flowers, 1–2 inches across, that open only in morning sunlight and close by afternoon. Stems are branching, hairy, angular, with small clasping leaves. Thick taproot similar to dandelion but larger. White milky sap in the root.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, fields, disturbed areas, meadows. Very common throughout the Northeast.

**Season**
Young basal rosette leaves: spring and fall. Roots: fall (most concentrated). Flowers: summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (raw or cooked), roots (roasted coffee substitute), flowers (edible garnish).

**Preparation**
Young spring leaves raw in salads (bitter — combine with milder greens). Blanch older leaves to reduce bitterness. Root coffee: dig fall roots, wash, chop into small pieces, roast at 350°F until very dark, grind, brew — earthy, rich, caffeine-free coffee substitute. This is the same root used commercially as a coffee additive.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. The distinctive blue flowers and angular branching stem make chicory recognizable from a distance in bloom. Basal rosette without flowers may be confused with dandelion — both are edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. The diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder. Stems are grooved and often striped in red, green, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides, compost piles. One of the most abundant edible weeds in North America.

**Season**
Late spring through fall. Young leaves and shoot tips are best in early to midsummer before the plant becomes large and woody.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds (tedious to harvest but nutritious).

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Cooking reduces oxalic acid. Add to soups, omelets, pasta, anywhere cooked greens work. Nutritionally superior to spinach in protein, iron, and vitamin B2.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating, do not eat in very large quantities daily. Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, or kidney disease. Same caution as spinach.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips. The entire plant has a slightly mucilaginous quality when cut.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, dry, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, driveways, fields. Common in summer heat. Thrives where other plants struggle.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Peaks in hot weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (mucilaginous quality thickens dressings slightly). Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. Seeds ground into flour (tedious but nutritious). In Mediterranean and Middle Eastern cuisines, purslane is a mainstream salad ingredient.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants. Worldwide it is eaten as a common vegetable — only North America treats it as a weed. Exceptional in omega-3 fatty acid content for a land plant.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Blue Violet
*Viola sororia*

![Common Blue Violet (Viola sororia) — heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers](images/native-plants/viola-sororia.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with heart-shaped, scallop-edged basal leaves 1–3 inches across. Flowers are deep violet-blue (sometimes white) with five petals, the lower three with darker veins. Grows from a short, thick rhizome.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, woodland edges, moist disturbed ground. Throughout the Northeast.

**Season**
Leaves: spring through early summer (most tender in spring). Flowers: April–May.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves and flowers both edible. Leaves are high in vitamin C and vitamin A.

**Preparation**
Leaves raw in salads or cooked as greens. Flowers as colorful garnish on salads and desserts. Flowers candied (dipped in egg white, coated in sugar, dried) as an elegant edible decoration. Leaves can be used as a thickener in soups — they have a slightly mucilaginous quality.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for violets with the characteristic heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated. Leaves have prominently toothed edges with the central tooth at the tip smaller than or equal to the adjacent teeth (distinguishing from mock strawberry).

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, old fields, forest edges, roadsides. Full sun to partial shade.

**Season**
Flowers in May. Fruit ripens June–July. Leaves available spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea — high in vitamin C).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, baked goods, or dried. The flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries — one wild strawberry packs more flavor than a dozen cultivated ones. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
**Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica* / *Duchesnea indica*)** — the small red berry on a plant with similar leaves is insipid but NOT toxic. Distinguished by its upward-facing yellow flowers (wild strawberry has white flowers) and berries that point upward rather than drooping. No dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Blackberry
*Rubus allegheniensis*

![Wild Blackberry (Rubus allegheniensis) — ripe black berries on thorny canes](images/native-plants/rubus-allegheniensis.jpg)

**Identification**
Thorny, arching canes 3–6 feet tall with compound leaves of 3–5 toothed leaflets. White five-petaled flowers in spring clusters. Fruit is a blackberry — an aggregate of drupelets ripening from green to red to glossy black. Canes are distinctly thorny (not just prickled like some related species).

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, forest edges, roadsides, clearings, powerline cuts. Abundant in disturbed open areas.

**Season**
Flowers May–June. Fruit ripens July–August. Fully ripe fruit pulls free easily with no resistance.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit, young spring shoots (peeled, eaten raw or cooked), and leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, in jam, pie, cordial, wine. Young spring shoots: peel the outer skin, eat the pale tender inner shoot raw. Leaf tea.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes among blackberries in the Northeast. Pokeweed berries (TOXIC) grow on a distinctly different plant — large herbaceous stalk with magenta stems, no thorns, different leaf shape.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Japanese Knotweed
*Fallopia japonica*

![Japanese Knotweed (Fallopia japonica) — hollow segmented stems and shovel-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/fallopia-japonica.jpg)

**Identification**
An aggressive invasive reaching 6–12 feet by midsummer. Hollow, bamboo-like stems with distinct nodes and shovel-shaped leaves with a flat or slightly notched base (not heart-shaped). Young spring shoots emerge as reddish-green asparagus-like spears. In fall, small white-cream flowers appear in clusters from leaf axils.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, stream banks, disturbed areas, edges of properties. Often grows in dense monoculture colonies.

**Season**
Young shoots in early spring (April–May) when less than 8 inches tall. Older stems are too fibrous.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots only. Hollow stems at 4–8 inches, before leaves fully expand.

**Preparation**
Flavor similar to rhubarb — sour and tangy from oxalic acid. Chop and cook in pies, crumbles, chutneys, or sauces exactly as you would rhubarb. Can be juiced and fermented. Do not eat large quantities raw.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — same cautions as rhubarb. Do not eat in large raw quantities. Foraging it is ecologically encouraged (invasive species).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ground Ivy
*Glechoma hederacea*

![Ground Ivy (Glechoma hederacea) — scalloped kidney-shaped leaves and small purple flowers](images/native-plants/glechoma-hederacea.jpg)

**Identification**
Low creeping perennial with small, rounded, kidney-shaped leaves with scalloped edges on square stems. Small purple-blue tubular flowers appear in spring. The leaves are strongly aromatic when crushed — a distinctive sharp, minty-musty smell.

**Where to Find It**
Shaded lawns, garden edges, woodland edges, moist disturbed areas. Very common throughout the Northeast.

**Season**
Year-round in the Northeast. Most tender in spring.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots.

**Preparation**
Use fresh as a tea herb — steep in hot water for a pleasant mild tea with a hint of mint and resin. Add small amounts to salads. Use as a flavoring herb in soups and cooked dishes. In historical use it was used to clarify ale ("alehoof").

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. Use in moderation — the volatile oils in large quantities may cause digestive irritation in some people.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Ginger
*Asarum canadense*

![Wild Ginger (Asarum canadense) — heart-shaped leaves and single maroon flower at base](images/native-plants/asarum-canadense.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, slow-spreading ground cover with large, paired, heart-shaped, velvety leaves 3–6 inches across, growing close to the ground in colonies. A single small maroon-brown, three-lobed, urn-shaped flower hides at ground level under the leaf pair in early spring. The roots (rhizomes) smell strongly of ginger when crushed — the most reliable ID feature.

**Where to Find It**
Rich, moist deciduous forest with well-drained, loamy soil. Often associated with trillium and bloodroot.

**Season**
Rhizomes: spring through fall. The plant is slow-growing — harvest sparingly.

**Edible Parts**
Root/rhizome only (as a spice/flavoring). Used as a ginger substitute.

**Preparation**
Dig small sections of rhizome, wash, peel, and use fresh like ginger — in teas, marinades, baking. Dry and grind as a spice powder. Use in very small quantities as flavoring, not as a primary food.

**Safety**
Wild ginger contains aristolochic acid, a compound associated with kidney damage in large quantities over long periods. Use as a flavoring spice only, in small amounts. Do not consume in medicinal doses daily.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia*

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — sword-like leaves and brown cylindrical seed spike in wetland](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: flat, sword-like leaves up to 9 feet tall growing from wetland, topped by the distinctive brown cylindrical "hot dog on a stick" seed spike. *T. latifolia* (broadleaf) has male and female flower spikes that nearly touch; *T. angustifolia* (narrowleaf) has a gap between them. Both are fully edible.

**Where to Find It**
Freshwater wetlands, marshes, swamps, ponds, slow-moving streams, ditches. One of the most common wetland plants in North America.

**Season**
Multiple harvest windows throughout the year:
- **Spring:** Young shoots at 4–18 inches (peel to white core)
- **Early summer:** Green female flower spikes before pollen develops
- **Summer:** Yellow pollen from the male spike
- **Fall–Winter:** Starchy rhizomes

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots, green flower spikes, pollen, and rhizomes. The male spike pollen is particularly valuable as a flour supplement.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel to pale inner core; eat raw (cucumber-like) or steamed. Green flower spikes: boil 10 minutes; eat like corn on the cob. Pollen: shake male spike into a bag; mix into pancakes or bread as 25% flour replacement. Rhizomes: pound in water, settle starch, drain, dry — produces a high-starch flour.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in its wetland habitat. One of the most forager-friendly plants — the "supermarket of the swamp."

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Queen Anne's Lace / Wild Carrot
*Daucus carota*

![Queen Anne's Lace (Daucus carota) — flat white flower umbel with single purple flower at center](images/native-plants/daucus-carota.jpg)

**Identification**
A biennial with a flat-topped white flower cluster (compound umbel) with a characteristic single dark purple-red flower at the exact center. The leaves are finely divided, carrot-like. Crush a leaf or the root — it smells distinctly of carrot. First-year plants form only a rosette; the thin taproot smells of carrot. Second year plants flower. Mature seed clusters curl inward forming a "bird's nest" shape.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, fields, old meadows. Very common.

**Season**
First-year root: fall. Young second-year shoots before flowering.

**Edible Parts**
First-year root (when small and carrot-scented). Second-year shoots before flowering.

**Preparation**
First-year root: peel and eat raw or cooked like a small, tough carrot. Older roots become very fibrous and woody — less palatable.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE WARNING.** *Poison Hemlock (Conium maculatum)* and *Water Hemlock (Cicuta douglasii)* — BOTH POTENTIALLY FATAL — grow in similar habitats and have similar white flower umbels and finely divided leaves. Differences: Poison Hemlock has purple-spotted stems (never seen on Wild Carrot), no carrot smell, and lacks the central purple flower. Water Hemlock grows in wet areas. The carrot smell of Wild Carrot is the key differentiator — **if it doesn't smell like carrot, do not eat it.** The Apiaceae (carrot family) contains some of the deadliest plants in North America. Beginners should avoid all members until experienced.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Lotus
*Nelumbo lutea*

![American Lotus (Nelumbo lutea) — large round floating leaves and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/nelumbo-lutea.jpg)

**Identification**
Large aquatic perennial with enormous round leaves (1–2 feet across) that rise above the water surface on long petioles, shedding water like a water-resistant coating. Large, pale yellow flowers 4–8 inches across in summer. Distinctive seed head: a flat-topped structure resembling a shower head with oval seeds embedded in holes. Rhizomes are thick, chambered, resembling water chestnuts, buried in pond mud.

**Where to Find It**
Ponds, slow-moving rivers, lakes, backwaters. Scattered throughout the Northeast, more common in the mid-Atlantic south.

**Season**
Young leaves emerging: spring. Seeds in the seed head: fall. Rhizomes: fall through winter.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked), seeds (raw or roasted), seed head (as a vessel/container when dry), rhizomes (cooked), and young leaf stalks.

**Preparation**
Seeds: remove from seed head, shell, eat raw (starchy, mild) or roast. Rhizomes: dig from mud, wash, slice into rounds, sauté, or add to soups — similar to lotus root used in Asian cuisine, with characteristic holes in cross-section. Young leaves: steam and use as wraps or cooked greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive round leaves and yellow flowers. Wade carefully in pond mud.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Black Walnut
*Juglans nigra*

![Black Walnut (Juglans nigra) — compound leaves and round green-hulled nuts](images/native-plants/juglans-nigra.jpg)

**Identification**
A large tree (50–100 feet) with alternate, pinnately compound leaves (15–23 leaflets). The bark of mature trees is deeply ridged and dark gray to nearly black. The fruit is a round nut (1–3 inches diameter) enclosed in a thick green husk that turns black when ripe. The inner nut shell is very hard and corrugated. Crush any part of the husk — a strong, distinctive, pungent walnut smell.

**Where to Find It**
Moist, rich, well-drained soils — forest edges, stream banks, valleys, old homesteads. Often a solitary tree rather than groves. Widely planted as a shade tree.

**Season**
Nuts ripen and fall in September–October. Collect from the ground after they fall.

**Edible Parts**
The nutmeat inside the hard inner shell. The green hull is not eaten but makes a brown dye.

**Preparation**
**WEAR GLOVES** — the green hull contains juglone, which permanently stains skin dark brown. To process: wear rubber gloves, remove the green husk (stomp on it or use gloves), rinse nuts, cure in a single layer in a cool, dry spot for 2–3 weeks. Crack the very hard shell with a hammer or nutcracker specifically designed for black walnuts. Pick out the nutmeat. The flavor is distinctly more intense and complex than English walnut. Use in baked goods, ice cream, salads.

**Safety**
Juglone from the hull stains skin (harmless but persistent) and is toxic to some plants — do not compost hulls near tomatoes or other juglone-sensitive plants. The nut itself is safe and nutritious.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Shagbark Hickory
*Carya ovata*

![Shagbark Hickory (Carya ovata) — distinctive shaggy peeling bark and compound leaves](images/native-plants/carya-ovata.jpg)

**Identification**
A large tree (60–100 feet) whose name is its best identification: the gray bark peels in long, distinctive shaggy plates curling away from the trunk at both ends like a shaggy coat — unmistakable. Compound leaves with 5 leaflets (the terminal leaflet largest). Nuts enclosed in a thick, four-valved green husk that turns brown and splits at maturity, revealing a tan to white shell.

**Where to Find It**
Dry to moist upland forests, forest edges, mixed hardwood stands with oaks.

**Season**
Nuts ripen and fall September–October.

**Edible Parts**
Nutmeat inside the shell.

**Preparation**
Crack with a hammer. Nutmeat is sweet and excellent — among the best-tasting of all North American native nuts. Use raw, in baked goods, or infuse into cream for ice cream and desserts. Traditional hickory nut cake is a classic.

**Safety**
Distinguish from bitternut hickory (*Carya cordiformis*) which has yellow-tipped buds, thinner husks, and bitter inedible nuts. Shagbark's unique peeling bark distinguishes it from all other trees.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Hazelnut
*Corylus americana*

![American Hazelnut (Corylus americana) — clusters of nuts in leafy husks](images/native-plants/corylus-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (3–10 feet) with rounded, finely toothed, alternate leaves with a heart-shaped base. The nuts grow in clusters of 2–4, each enclosed in ragged, leafy husks (involucres) that are longer than the nut. The round nut has a brown shell and resembles a small cultivated hazelnut.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, thickets, roadsides, stream banks, open woodlands. Tolerates partial shade. Very common in the Northeast.

**Season**
Nuts ripen August–September. Harvest before squirrels, which find them first. A productive hazel patch should be checked daily once nuts are visible.

**Edible Parts**
Nutmeat inside the shell.

**Preparation**
Crack and eat fresh (the best way — sweet, mild, rich). Roast lightly to deepen flavor. Grind into a meal or paste. Use in cookies, breads, or made into hazelnut butter. Dry nuts store for months.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive hazel nut in its ragged leafy husk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Serviceberry / Juneberry
*Amelanchier* spp. (*A. canadensis*, *A. arborea*, *A. laevis*)

![Serviceberry (Amelanchier) — dark purple berries and finely toothed oval leaves](images/native-plants/amelanchier.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub to small tree (6–25 feet) with oval, finely serrated alternate leaves. One of the first trees to flower in spring — white star-shaped five-petaled flowers appear before or with the first leaves, often while snow is still possible. Fruit ripens in early summer (hence "Juneberry"), turning from red to dark purple when fully ripe.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, slopes, stream banks, roadsides. Adaptable — grows in a wide range of conditions.

**Season**
One of the earliest fruit harvests of the year. Fruit ripe June–July, window of 2–3 weeks. Fully purple berries are sweet; red berries still tart.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent. Dried like raisins. Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe purple serviceberries with their characteristic fine-toothed oval leaves. Confirm the five-petaled flower or the characteristic berry with a tiny five-pointed crown at the blossom end (like a miniature blueberry crown).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Plum
*Prunus americana*

![Wild Plum (Prunus americana) — red-orange ripe plums on thorny branches](images/native-plants/prunus-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with thorny branches. Alternate, oval, sharply toothed leaves. Clusters of white five-petaled flowers in early spring before the leaves emerge. The fruit is a small plum (¾–1 inch diameter), ripening red to yellow, with a thin skin and juicy flesh enclosing a single pit.

**Where to Find It**
Thickets, roadsides, forest edges, stream banks, old fence lines. Often forms dense thickets.

**Season**
Flowers: early spring. Fruit ripens July–August. Pick when fully colored and soft to the touch.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not crack or chew pits.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw (tart but good). Jelly (excellent — high natural pectin). Jam. Butter (cooked down with spices). Wine. Dried. The tartness diminishes significantly with cooking.

**Safety**
Like all *Prunus* species, the pits (and bark and leaves) contain cyanogenic glycosides — do not chew pits, strain seeds from juices. The ripe flesh is safe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus canadensis* (American Elder)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark purple-to-black spheres in heavy drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, forest edges, fence rows, disturbed moist areas, floodplains.

**Season**
Flowers: late May through July. Berries: late August through October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems (don't eat stems), steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine, pie.

**Safety**
**LOOKALIKE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* has red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster — berries are toxic raw (though some traditions cook them). **Black/Blue Elder (S. nigra and S. canadensis) = flat-topped cluster + purple-black berries = edible when cooked. Red berries in domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Also distinguish from Poison Hemlock (*Conium*) which can share habitat — elder has pithy hollow stems and compound leaves; hemlock has spotted hollow stems.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Autumn Olive
*Elaeagnus umbellata*

![Autumn Olive (Elaeagnus umbellata) — silvery leaves and clusters of small red-speckled berries](images/native-plants/elaeagnus-umbellata.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–15 feet) with distinctive silvery-gray leaves — both surfaces covered in tiny silver scales giving a frosted appearance, especially on the underside. Fragrant tubular yellow-white flowers in spring. Fruit is a small (¼ inch) red berry speckled with silver dots in fall — visually distinctive and unmistakable.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, roadsides, disturbed areas, forest edges. An invasive species — aggressive foraging is encouraged.

**Season**
Berries ripen September–November. They sweeten significantly after the first frost.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries. Highly nutritious — one of the richest sources of lycopene of any fruit.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw (tart when not fully ripe, sweet-tart when ripe). Jelly, jam, wine. Dried. Dehydrated and ground into a powder as a seasoning.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive silver-speckled red berries and frosted silvery leaves. Foraging invasive species is ecologically beneficial.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Rose
*Rosa carolina / Rosa palustris / Rosa blanda* (several species)

![Wild Rose (Rosa carolina) — pink five-petaled flowers and oval leaflets](images/native-plants/rosa-carolina.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs with thorny or prickly canes, alternate compound leaves with 5–9 oval toothed leaflets, and classic five-petaled pink (occasionally white) single rose flowers. The red to orange-red fruit (rose hip) is a swollen receptacle containing seeds surrounded by fleshy tissue. Hips develop in late summer and persist through winter.

**Where to Find It**
Open areas, roadsides, meadow edges, forest edges, thickets. Multiple native species throughout the Northeast.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Rose hips: August through winter (best after first frost sweetens them).

**Edible Parts**
Petals (fresh), rose hips (processed), and young shoots in spring (peeled).

**Preparation**
Petals: fresh in salads, infused in syrups, dried for tea. Rose hips: cut off the blossom end and stem, slice in half, scrape out the seeds and fine hairs (both irritating), then use the flesh. Rose hip tea: dry hips and steep in hot water — extremely high in vitamin C. Rose hip jam, syrup, cordial.

**Safety**
Thorns are a physical hazard. The seeds and fine hairs inside the hip are irritating — always remove before consuming. No toxic lookalikes for the classic rose flower-and-hip combination.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Staghorn Sumac
*Rhus typhina*

![Staghorn Sumac (Rhus typhina) — compound leaves and upright red berry clusters](images/native-plants/rhus-typhina.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (10–25 feet) with large, pinnately compound leaves (11–31 leaflets) and velvety, densely-hairy branches (resembling deer antlers in velvet — hence the name). In summer, upright, cone-shaped clusters of tiny red berries develop at branch tips. Berries covered in short red hairs. The entire fruiting cluster is distinctly red and upright.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, old fields, forest edges, dry rocky slopes. Forms large clonal colonies.

**Season**
Berries (actually drupes) ripen red in July–August and persist through winter. Best harvested before heavy rains wash away the acidic coating.

**Edible Parts**
The red berry clusters. The acidic coating on the berries is the edible/useful part.

**Preparation**
Sumac-ade: rub the berry cluster vigorously in cool water (NOT hot — hot water releases bitter tannins), strain through a cloth to remove hairs, sweeten to taste. The resulting pink drink tastes like lemonade. Dried, ground berries make a tart, lemony spice (the same as the *sumac* used in Middle Eastern cooking).

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** *Poison Sumac (Toxicodendron vernix)* — CAUSES SEVERE ALLERGIC REACTION (worse than poison ivy) — grows in WET habitats (swamps, marshes), has **white to greenish-white** berry clusters that hang **downward** from branches, and has smooth (not hairy) leaves with smooth-edged leaflets. Staghorn sumac: red upright clusters, hairy stems, dry habitat. The habitat and berry color/orientation are foolproof distinguishers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Spicebush
*Lindera benzoin*

![Spicebush (Lindera benzoin) — bright red berries and smooth alternate leaves](images/native-plants/lindera-benzoin.jpg)

**Identification**
A woodland shrub (6–12 feet) with smooth, alternate, elliptical leaves. In early spring before leaves emerge, tiny clusters of yellow flowers appear directly on the bare stems. The most useful ID feature: crush any part of the plant and it releases a distinctive spicy-citrusy-aromatic smell. Red berries (¼–½ inch) ripen in fall on female plants.

**Where to Find It**
Moist, shaded woodland understory, stream banks, wooded floodplains. Often an indicator of rich, moist soil.

**Season**
Berries ripen August–October. Young leaves in spring (aromatic flavoring). Twigs and bark usable year-round as flavoring.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries (as spice), young leaves, and twigs/bark (as flavoring tea or spice).

**Preparation**
Berries: dry and grind as an allspice substitute — the flavor is remarkably similar to allspice, with notes of bay and clove. Use in spice rubs, mulled drinks, baked goods. Twig tea: steep young twigs in hot water for a mild aromatic tea. Used historically as a substitute for allspice and bay.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive spicy smell.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Persimmon
*Diospyros virginiana*

![American Persimmon (Diospyros virginiana) — orange persimmon fruit on bare branches](images/native-plants/diospyros-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
A medium tree (30–60 feet) with distinctive blocky, gray-to-black bark divided into small square or rectangular plates — one of the most recognizable bark patterns of any eastern tree. Alternate, oval, leathery leaves turn orange-red in fall. The fruit is round, about 1–2 inches, turning from green to orange-yellow when ripe, often with a powdery bloom. Calyx (dried flower base) persistent at fruit top.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, old fields, roadsides, dry slopes. Tolerates poor soil. Throughout the eastern half of North America.

**Season**
Fruit ripens in fall (September–November). Must be fully ripe — after the first frost is ideal. Unripe fruit is outrageously astringent.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin edible but thick. Seeds edible (roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Fully ripe fruit: eat raw, pulp out seeds, use in pudding (persimmon pudding is a classic). Dry for persimmon candy. Pulp freezes well. Beer. Seeds: roast as coffee substitute. Unripe persimmon puckering is legendary — always taste a tiny amount first.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive blocky bark and the characteristic orange fruit. If it puckers your mouth, it is not ripe — wait for full frost-softening.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Blueberry / Lowbush Blueberry
*Vaccinium angustifolium*

![Wild Blueberry (Vaccinium angustifolium) — low shrub with clusters of small dark blue berries](images/native-plants/vaccinium-angustifolium.jpg)

**Identification**
A low-growing shrub (6–24 inches) with small, elliptical, finely serrated alternate leaves. Tiny white to pink urn-shaped flowers in spring. Berries are small (¼–½ inch), blue-black with a waxy white bloom, growing in clusters. The five-pointed star-shaped calyx at the blossom end is the key identification feature — all *Vaccinium* species have it.

**Where to Find It**
Acidic, well-drained, sandy or rocky soils. Open barrens, forest edges, burned-over areas, heath-covered slopes. Very common throughout the Northeast, particularly in Maine and the Adirondacks.

**Season**
Berries ripen July–August. In productive years, hillsides turn blue.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries only.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — intense flavor, far superior to cultivated blueberries. Jam, pie, muffins, syrup, dried. Freezes exceptionally well.

**Safety**
The five-pointed calyx crown at the berry tip is the key feature distinguishing blueberries from any toxic lookalike. No dangerous lookalikes once the crown is confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wintergreen
*Gaultheria procumbens*

![Wintergreen (Gaultheria procumbens) — dark glossy leaves and red berries low to forest floor](images/native-plants/gaultheria-procumbens.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, creeping evergreen shrub (2–6 inches tall) with small, thick, glossy, oval dark green leaves with finely toothed edges. Small white urn-shaped flowers in summer. Bright red berries (¼ inch) persist through winter. The most reliable ID feature: crush a leaf and the scent is unmistakably wintergreen — identical to wintergreen candy or toothpaste.

**Where to Find It**
Acidic, sandy, or rocky forest soil, often under conifers and heath shrubs. Throughout the Northeast in boreal and mixed forest.

**Season**
Berries: October through spring (ripe fall, persist through winter, best after freeze-thaw cycles sweeten them). Leaves: year-round.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries, leaves (primarily as tea).

**Preparation**
Berries: eat fresh (spicy, minty). Add to trail mix. Brew tea from fresh or dried leaves. The natural source of methyl salicylate (the "wintergreen" compound).

**Safety**
Methyl salicylate in large quantities can be toxic — do not eat very large amounts of leaves. Berries in reasonable quantities are safe. No dangerous lookalikes with the wintergreen smell.

---

## FUNGI

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel Mushroom
*Morchella* spp. (*M. esculenta*, *M. elata*, *M. importuna*)

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — this is the single most important identification criterion. Cut vertically: the cap and stem form one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base. Cap color: tan to gray to near-black depending on species.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood forests — especially near dying or recently dead elm, ash, apple, and tulip poplar. South-facing slopes where soil warms first. Recently burned areas (burn morels the spring after wildfire). Often in the same spots year after year.

**Season**
Spring only. February–early June depending on latitude and elevation. Soil temperature around 50°F triggers emergence. Black morels come first; yellow morels follow.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset even in correctly identified specimens. Sauté in butter over medium-high heat 8–12 minutes until golden. Clean carefully: slice vertically and rinse, as the hollow cavity traps soil and insects. Add to cream sauces, eggs, risotto, pasta. Dry and store for year-round use.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* and *Verpa* species) — POISONOUS, some potentially FATAL. *Gyromitra* contains gyromitrin, a compound metabolized to monomethylhydrazine (similar to rocket fuel). False morels differ: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap attaches only at the top (not at the base), cap surface is wrinkled, brain-like, or saddle-shaped (not regular honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chanterelle
*Cantharellus cibarius* and related species

![Chanterelle (Cantharellus cibarius) — golden mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-cibarius.jpg)

**Identification**
Golden-yellow to egg-yolk orange mushrooms with a wavy, irregular cap margin, often funnel-shaped with age. The critical feature: **false gills** — beneath the cap are blunt, forking ridges (not thin knife-like gills) that run partway down the stem. These ridges are thick, widely spaced, and fork repeatedly. Flesh is solid white. Fruity, apricot-like smell.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood and mixed forests, particularly under oak, beech, maple. Grows from soil (not wood). Solitary or scattered, not in clusters.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Most productive after rain followed by warm weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously — absorbs water and becomes mushy. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. The flavor is fruity, slightly peppery, excellent in cream sauces and pasta.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus illudens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at the base of trees or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), and gills glow faintly in the dark (observable). Causes severe vomiting and diarrhea. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicken of the Woods
*Laetiporus sulphureus / L. cincinnatus*

![Chicken of the Woods (Laetiporus sulphureus) — bright orange-yellow shelf fungus on tree trunk](images/native-plants/laetiporus-sulphureus.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: bright orange-to-yellow shelf-like brackets growing in overlapping tiers directly from tree trunks or stumps. The top surface is vibrant orange; the underside is pale yellow to cream with tiny pores (no gills). When fresh, the flesh is tender, moist, and white. *L. sulphureus* grows on oaks, chestnuts, and other hardwoods; *L. cincinnatus* grows at the base of oaks with a paler, salmon-pink color.

**Where to Find It**
Living or dead oaks, cherry, locust, beech, willow. Can appear on the same tree annually.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Fresh specimens are best — older specimens become tough and bitter.

**Edible Parts**
Young, tender outer edges of fresh specimens. Avoid the tough inner portions near the wood.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or olive oil. The flavor and texture genuinely resemble chicken — hence the name. Use in stir-fries, tacos, sandwiches, pasta. Must be fully cooked — some people experience gastrointestinal reactions to undercooked specimens.

**Safety**
Some people react to specimens growing on certain tree species (black locust, eucalyptus, conifers) — stick to oak-grown specimens. Some individuals are simply sensitive regardless of host tree. Try a small amount first. No dangerous lookalikes — the color, shelf form, and pore surface (not gills) are distinctive.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hen of the Woods / Maitake
*Grifola frondosa*

![Hen of the Woods (Grifola frondosa) — overlapping gray-brown fronds at tree base](images/native-plants/grifola-frondosa.jpg)

**Identification**
A large polypore mushroom (can weigh 20–100 lbs) growing at the base of oaks or from buried roots as a dense cluster of overlapping, fan-shaped gray-to-brown fronds with white to cream pore surfaces underneath. The fronds are thin and flexible, not shelf-like. The entire mass rises from a single central base. Smells pleasantly earthy and mushroom-like.

**Where to Find It**
Base of mature oaks (especially red and white oak), sometimes beech. Returns to the same spot annually.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October). Appears around the same time each year at productive spots.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh fruiting body. Tender outer fronds are best.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or oil, roast in the oven, add to soups and stir-fries. One of the most flavorful and versatile wild mushrooms. Dries well for storage. Medicinally significant — studied for immune modulation (not a foraging claim but relevant context).

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes for a large gray-brown overlapping frond cluster at an oak base. Berkeley's Polypore (*Bondarzewia berkeleyi*) is similar but much paler, grows from roots, and is edible (though tougher).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. **True gills** run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, stubby, off-center or absent. White spore print. Found in cool weather on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead or dying hardwoods — beech, oak, maple, elm, willow. Grows on the sides of standing dead trees or fallen logs.

**Season**
Spring and fall, especially when temperatures drop. May survive brief freezes.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well. One of the most widely cultivated mushrooms — the wild version has identical flavor and food value.

**Safety**
Angel wings (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) grows on conifer wood and is smaller and pure white. Avoid. Oyster mushrooms on hardwoods with the described characteristics are generally reliable, but avoid specimens that are old, soft, or smell sour.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, meadow edges, forest clearings, lawns, disturbed ground. Appears in late summer.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white. Once any yellow, purple, or green coloring develops inside, it is no longer safe to eat.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden — one of the best preparations. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. The inside of an edible puffball is a uniform white solid, with no differentiation. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which means it might be a deadly *Amanita* button — also round and white but with developing structure inside), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating. Other edible puffballs include Gem-studded Puffball (*Lycoperdon perlatum*) — tiny, studded with white spines, edible when all-white inside.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lion's Mane
*Hericium erinaceus*

![Lion's Mane (Hericium erinaceus) — white cascading icicle-like teeth on dead tree](images/native-plants/hericium-erinaceus.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the most distinctive mushrooms in any region. A pure white, cascading, icicle-like mass of downward-pointing teeth (spines), 2–4 inches long, growing as a single globe-shaped clump from a wound in a hardwood tree. When fresh: bright white, firm, with a sweet, seafood-like smell. When old: yellowing to tan. Completely unmistakable — no other mushroom in the Northeast looks like it.

**Where to Find It**
Growing from wounds or branch scars on living or dead hardwoods — beech, maple, oak. Sometimes high on a tree trunk.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–November).

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Slice, sauté in butter until golden and slightly crispy. The flavor is remarkable — sweet, mild, with the texture and taste often compared to crab or lobster. Use in any seafood recipe. Also studied medicinally (nerve growth factor — not a food claim but contextually notable).

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. The cascading white icicle-tooth form is unmistakable.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Black Trumpet
*Craterellus cornucopioides*

![Black Trumpet (Craterellus cornucopioides) — dark funnel-shaped mushrooms in leaf litter](images/native-plants/craterellus-cornucopioides.jpg)

**Identification**
A small, dark gray to nearly black, thin-walled, vase-to-trumpet-shaped mushroom, 1–3 inches tall, with a flared, irregular opening at the top. The outer surface (which functions as the spore surface) is smooth to very finely wrinkled — no true gills, just slight undulations. Very thin-fleshed. Often grows in clusters in leaf litter and is extremely difficult to see — the difficulty of finding them is part of their mystique.

**Where to Find It**
Rich, moist hardwood forest under oak, beech, and mixed hardwoods. Often in moss or deep leaf litter on slopes. They blend almost perfectly with their surroundings.

**Season**
Summer through fall (July–October). Look after heavy rain in moist forest.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Intense, smoky, earthy, excellent flavor. Sauté in butter, cream sauces, omelets, risotto. Dry and grind into a black powder — one of the most flavorful culinary spices available from the forest. Small amounts add extraordinary depth to sauces.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes once you understand the form. The thin, black, vase-shaped funnel growing from forest soil is not easily confused with anything toxic.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Meadow Mushroom
*Agaricus campestris*

*[Photo needed: Agaricus campestris — white-capped mushroom in lawn/meadow with pink gills. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A classic white-capped mushroom of lawns and meadows: 2–4 inch smooth white cap, white to pale gills turning pink then dark brown as spores mature, ring on the stem (partial veil remnant), no volva (cup) at the base. **Key:** cut the cap or base — flesh turns **slightly pink** or does not change color (not yellow). Smell is pleasantly mushroom-like, not phenolic or like chemicals.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, pastures, parks. Often appears after rain in late summer and fall.

**Season**
Late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté, add to pasta, pizza, soups, or eat raw (older tradition). The cultivated white button mushroom is a relative.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: Destroying Angel / Death Cap (*Amanita bisporigera / A. phalloides*)** — DEADLY (no antidote, fatal liver failure). *Amanita* species have a cup (volva) at the base of the stem, a ring, and the flesh does NOT turn pink when cut. Always dig up the entire mushroom to check for the volva. **If the base has a cup, do not eat it.** Also, *Agaricus xanthodermus* — TOXIC — is similar but the cap base flesh turns **bright yellow** when cut and smells phenolic (like carbolic acid or ink). **Rule: if it smells like chemicals or turns yellow when cut at the base, do not eat it.**

---

---

# REGION 2: SOUTHEAST AND APPALACHIA
*Virginia, West Virginia, Kentucky, Tennessee, North Carolina, South Carolina, Georgia, Florida (northern), Alabama, Mississippi, Arkansas, Louisiana*

Appalachia may be the single richest foraging region in the eastern United States — the Southern Appalachian Mountains harbor more plant species per acre than any other temperate region in North America. The growing season is long, and the overlap of northern and southern species creates extraordinary biodiversity. The lowland Southeast adds subtropical species unavailable further north.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ramps / Wild Leeks
*Allium tricoccum*

![Ramps (Allium tricoccum) — broad leaves in Appalachian spring forest](images/native-plants/allium-tricoccum.jpg)

**Identification**
Broad, smooth-edged, lance-shaped leaves emerge in early spring from a slender reddish-pink stem. Leaves are 4–10 inches long, 1–3 inches wide, growing in clumps of 2–3 from a thin white bulb. The single most reliable ID feature: crush a leaf and it releases an unmistakable pungent onion-garlic scent. No onion smell = not ramps.

**Where to Find It**
In the Southeast, ramps grow at higher elevations — the Southern Appalachians (NC, TN, WV, VA). Rich cove hardwood forests at 2,000–5,000 feet elevation under yellow birch, sugar maple, beech, and tulip poplar. The famous Appalachian ramp festivals (Richwood, WV; Cosby, TN) mark prime harvest areas.

**Season**
Slightly later than Northeast: mid-April through mid-May at mid-elevation; into late May at higher elevations.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: bulbs, stems, and leaves. Flowers also edible.

**Preparation**
Raw leaves in salads, pesto, or as wraps. Bulbs pickled in white wine vinegar with salt and sugar. Sautéed whole in butter — flavor mellows significantly with heat. Grilled whole over open fire. Ramp kimchi.

**Safety**
*Lily of the Valley (Convallaria majalis)* — TOXIC — grows in similar habitat with similar broad leaves. Lacks any onion smell. Crush the leaf: if it smells like flowers or nothing, put it down immediately. The smell test is absolute.

**Notes**
Ramps are deeply embedded in Appalachian foodways. The smell can linger on the breath for 24–48 hours. Harvest pressure from festivals and commercial foragers has significantly depleted some Appalachian patches — harvest only one leaf per plant. A patch takes 7+ years to recover from heavy harvest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — basal rosette and flower](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Basal rosette of deeply lobed, toothed leaves arising from a central crown at ground level — no stem leaves, ever. A single hollow leafless stem from the crown bears one yellow flower head, which matures into the familiar spherical seed ball. White milky latex bleeds from any broken stem or leaf. Taproot is long, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, fields, disturbed areas, gardens. Virtually everywhere throughout the Southeast.

**Season**
Year-round at lower elevations; spring and fall at higher Appalachian elevations. Young spring leaves are least bitter.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: leaves, flowers, roots, even flower buds.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads with vinaigrette. Older leaves: blanch 2 minutes, sauté with garlic and olive oil. Flowers battered and fried, steeped in sugar syrup for cordial, or made into wine. Flower buds pickled like capers. Roots roasted at 350°F until dark and fragrant, ground as a caffeine-free coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. Avoid plants from lawns treated with herbicides.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Forms dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small (1–3 cm), opposite, oval, pointed, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 petals each so deeply notched they appear to be 10. The single most reliable feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, under shrubs, at lawn edges, disturbed areas with partial shade. Throughout the Southeast in cooler seasons.

**Season**
Fall through spring in the Southeast. Dies back in summer heat. Often the first edible green of the cool season.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant. Young plants are most tender.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy flavor. Tossed into smoothies. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green combined with more flavorful foraged greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis stricta*

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped leaflets and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Small, low-growing plant with three heart-shaped leaflets arranged like a clover, each with a distinct fold down the center. Immediately distinguished from clover by heart-shaped (not oval) leaflets. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers. The entire plant has a distinctive sharp, lemony-sour taste from oxalic acid — tasting a small leaf is a reliable identification aid.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, gardens, sidewalk cracks, disturbed ground, forest edges. Very common throughout the Southeast year-round.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Most tender and abundant in spring and fall. Persists through mild Southeast winters.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods. Use as seasoning or snack, not as a primary food source.

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail snack or salad ingredient. The sour flavor functions as a lemon substitute — add to salads, grain dishes, cold drinks. Brew in water for a tart wild "lemonade."

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid. Safe in moderate amounts. **Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, arthritis, or kidney disease.** Same caution as spinach and chard.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. The diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder. Stems are grooved and often striped in red, green, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides, compost piles. One of the most abundant edible weeds across the Southeast.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Young leaves and shoot tips best in spring and early summer. Common in disturbed Southeast soils from March through October.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Add to soups, omelets, pasta, anywhere cooked greens work. Nutritionally superior to spinach in protein, iron, and vitamin B2.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating, do not eat in very large quantities daily. Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, or kidney disease.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips. The entire plant has a slightly mucilaginous quality when cut.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, dry, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, driveways, fields. Especially abundant in the hot, humid lowland Southeast summer when most other greens have bolted.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Peaks in hot weather — the Southeast summer is ideal. One of the few edible plants that thrives in the peak of Gulf Coast heat.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (mucilaginous quality thickens dressings). Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. Seeds ground into flour. In Mediterranean and Middle Eastern cuisines, purslane is a mainstream salad ingredient.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants. Exceptional in omega-3 fatty acid content for a land plant.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems are hollow and float. The flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Cold springs, spring-fed streams, and cold running water throughout the Southeast — particularly abundant in the limestone spring systems of Tennessee, Alabama, and the Appalachian foothills. **Harvest only from verified clean water sources** (upstream of farms, roads, and septic systems).

**Season**
Best in cool months (fall and spring). Available year-round where springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
