Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — opposite toothed leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 2–5 feet tall with opposite, coarsely toothed, pointed leaves on a square stem. Identification is immediate and painful: hollow stinging hairs covering all surfaces inject formic acid and histamine on contact. Drooping clusters of inconspicuous green flowers appear from leaf axils.

**Where to Find It**
Nitrogen-rich disturbed soil — stream banks, old farmyards, compost piles, roadsides, moist forest edges. Common throughout the Southeast in moist disturbed areas and along stream corridors.

**Season**
Harvest young shoots and top 4–6 inches of growth in early spring (March–May in the Southeast) **before the plant flowers.** Post-flowering nettles develop kidney-irritating alkaloids and should not be eaten.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and tender leaves only, before flowering.

**Preparation**
Wear thick gloves to harvest. Cooking for 5 minutes (boiling, steaming, or blanching) completely neutralizes the sting. Rich flavor, similar to spinach but earthier. Nettle soup with potato and onion. Blanched and pressed into pasta dough. Dried and brewed as tea.

**Safety**
**Do not eat after the plant flowers.** Wear thick gloves — thin rubber gloves do not stop the hairs.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia*

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — sword-like leaves and brown cylindrical seed spike in wetland](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: flat, sword-like leaves up to 9 feet tall growing from wetland, topped by the distinctive brown cylindrical "hot dog on a stick" seed spike. *T. latifolia* (broadleaf) has male and female flower spikes that nearly touch; *T. angustifolia* (narrowleaf) has a gap between them. Both are fully edible.

**Where to Find It**
Freshwater wetlands, marshes, swamps, ponds, slow-moving streams, ditches throughout the Southeast. One of the most common wetland plants in the region.

**Season**
Multiple harvest windows throughout the year:
- **Spring:** Young shoots at 4–18 inches (peel to white core)
- **Early summer:** Green female flower spikes before pollen develops
- **Summer:** Yellow pollen from the male spike
- **Fall–Winter:** Starchy rhizomes

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots, green flower spikes, pollen, and rhizomes.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel to pale inner core; eat raw (cucumber-like) or steamed. Green flower spikes: boil 10 minutes; eat like corn on the cob. Pollen: shake male spike into a bag; mix into pancakes or bread as 25% flour replacement. Rhizomes: pound in water, settle starch, drain, dry — produces a high-starch flour.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in its wetland habitat. One of the most forager-friendly plants — the "supermarket of the swamp."

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Jerusalem Artichoke / Sunchoke
*Helianthus tuberosus*

![Jerusalem Artichoke (Helianthus tuberosus) — tall sunflower plant with knobby tubers](images/native-plants/helianthus-tuberosus.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall, coarse-stemmed sunflower, 5–10 feet tall with rough, alternate leaves and bright yellow sunflower-like flowers 2–3 inches across in late summer. The key feature for the forager is underground: irregularly shaped, knobby tubers connected in clusters, brown-skinned with white flesh, resembling ginger root.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, stream banks, old fields, disturbed ground throughout the Southeast. Often persists at old homestead sites.

**Season**
Tubers best in fall (after frost sweetens them) through early spring. Dig after the plant dies back — the dead stalks mark the location.

**Edible Parts**
Tubers only. Raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Eat raw (crisp, sweet). Roast whole in coals or oven. Slice thin and pan-fry in fat until golden. Mash. Soup. Pickle. **Warning:** the inulin in Jerusalem artichoke causes significant gas and digestive discomfort in many people, especially when eaten in large quantities raw. Start with small amounts.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes once the tubers are found underground and the above-ground plant confirmed as a sunflower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Maypop / Wild Passionflower
*Passiflora incarnata*

![Maypop (Passiflora incarnata) — intricate purple corona flower and round fruit](images/native-plants/passiflora-incarnata.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the most extraordinary flowers in North American botany. A climbing vine with deeply three-lobed alternate leaves and tendrils. The lavender-purple flower has five petals and five sepals, above which rises a complex fringe of filaments (corona) in purple and white — completely unmistakable once seen. The fruit is oval, 1½–3 inches, green ripening to yellow-orange.

**Where to Find It**
Open disturbed ground, roadsides, field edges, forest margins. Full sun to partial shade. Southeast from Virginia to Florida and west to Texas.

**Season**
Flowers: June–August. Fruit: August–October (ripe when soft to pressure).

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit interior — the seedy, aromatic pulp. Flowers edible as garnish. Leaves and roots have traditional medicinal use (mild sedative) but are not food.

**Preparation**
Cut fruit in half, scoop out the seedy pulp. Eat fresh, strain for juice, use in drinks or desserts. Flavor: guava-like, tart, and aromatic. More intense than commercial passion fruit.

**Safety**
The distinctive flower makes misidentification virtually impossible. Unripe green fruit is less palatable but not toxic.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Groundnut / Hopniss
*Apios americana*

![Groundnut (Apios americana) — vine with bead-like chain of tubers underground](images/native-plants/apios-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A twining vine growing 4–20 feet, climbing over other vegetation. Compound leaves with 5–7 egg-shaped leaflets. Dense clusters of brownish-pink flowers in midsummer. Underground: a chain of oval starchy tubers connected like beads on a cord, ½–3 inches long, with rough brown skin and white starchy interior. **The chain-of-tubers root structure is the primary confirmation.**

**Where to Find It**
Moist disturbed areas, stream banks, meadows, thickets, forest edges. Prefers shade and moisture throughout the eastern United States.

**Season**
Tubers harvestable fall through spring. Best in fall after vine dies back — locate by the dead vine. Seeds in pods: edible when cooked, harvest in summer.

**Edible Parts**
Tubers (primary food), seed beans (cooked), flowers and leaves (edible).

**Preparation**
Peel the fibrous brown skin. Boil, steam, roast, or bake — treat like a small, drier, nuttier potato. Slice and pan-fry in fat until crispy. Mash with butter. Seed beans: boil like any bean.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive chain-of-tubers root structure. A foundational survival food in the eastern US.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mayapple
*Podophyllum peltatum*

![Mayapple (Podophyllum peltatum) — umbrella-like leaves and single white flower](images/native-plants/podophyllum-peltatum.jpg)

**Identification**
A woodland spring wildflower with large, deeply lobed, umbrella-like leaves (up to 12 inches across) on stems 12–18 inches tall. Non-fruiting plants have a single leaf; fruiting plants have two leaves with a single white, waxy, six-to-nine petaled flower nodding at the fork between the two leaf stalks. The oval fruit (1–2 inches) ripens from green to yellow in late summer.

**Where to Find It**
Rich, moist deciduous forest, floodplain forest, forest edges. Forms large colonies. Throughout the eastern United States.

**Season**
Flowers in April–May. Fruit ripens July–August. Ripe fruit is yellow, soft, and has a fragrant, tropical-like smell.

**Edible Parts**
**Ripe fruit only.** ALL OTHER PARTS ARE TOXIC — leaves, stems, roots, and unripe fruit contain podophyllotoxin, a powerful cytotoxin used pharmaceutically.

**Preparation**
Ripe yellow fruit: eat the flesh and seeds (seeds are safe when swallowed, not when chewed heavily). The flavor is tropical, sweet, with a slight fermented note. Use in jam or jelly. Strain seeds out.

**Safety**
**NEVER eat any part except the fully ripe yellow fruit.** The green unripe fruit, leaves, stems, and especially the roots are highly toxic — historically used as a cathartic drug and now as a source of cancer-treating compounds. **Confirm fruit is fully yellow and soft before tasting.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Lotus
*Nelumbo lutea*

![American Lotus (Nelumbo lutea) — round leaves and yellow flowers on still water](images/native-plants/nelumbo-lutea.jpg)

**Identification**
Large aquatic perennial with enormous round leaves (1–2 feet across) that rise above the water surface on long petioles, shedding water like a water-resistant coating. Large, pale yellow flowers 4–8 inches across in summer. Distinctive seed head: a flat-topped structure resembling a shower head with oval seeds embedded in holes. Rhizomes are thick, chambered, resembling water chestnuts, buried in pond mud.

**Where to Find It**
More widespread and productive in the Southeast than the Northeast — common in bayous, oxbow lakes, slow rivers, and coastal plain wetlands from Virginia to Florida and west to Texas. Also found in ponds and river backwaters throughout the region.

**Season**
Young leaves emerging: spring. Seeds in the seed head: fall. Rhizomes: fall through winter.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked), seeds (raw or roasted), seed head (as a vessel/container when dry), rhizomes (cooked), and young leaf stalks.

**Preparation**
Seeds: remove from seed head, shell, eat raw (starchy, mild) or roast. Rhizomes: dig from mud, wash, slice into rounds, sauté, or add to soups — similar to lotus root used in Asian cuisine, with characteristic holes in cross-section. Young leaves: steam and use as wraps or cooked greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive round leaves and yellow flowers. Wade carefully in pond mud.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Kudzu
*Pueraria montana var. lobata*

![Kudzu (Pueraria montana) — large three-lobed leaves blanketing everything in sight](images/native-plants/pueraria-montana.jpg)

**Identification**
The "vine that ate the South." Large, three-lobed leaves (similar to a giant bean leaf), hairy stems, and grape-like clusters of fragrant purple-pink flowers in late summer. Forms massive, dense mats covering trees, power lines, and entire landscapes. Unmistakable by growth habit alone.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, forest edges, disturbed areas, abandoned fields throughout the Southeast. An invasive species — foraging is ecologically encouraged.

**Season**
Young leaves and shoot tips: spring and early summer. Flowers: late summer (August–September). Roots: fall through winter (extremely large taproots, 6–12 inches in diameter).

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, flowers, root starch.

**Preparation**
Young leaves: sauté as greens, used in salads when very young (slightly starchy flavor). Flowers: eat fresh as garnish, make into jelly (the purple-pink flowers produce a beautiful pink jelly). Root: the massive taproot is high in starch — process like kudzu starch powder (traditional in Japan, used as a thickener). Wash and grate root, mix with water, strain through cloth, allow starch to settle, pour off water, dry the starch.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for kudzu in its coverage pattern. Young tender growth is safe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Spiderwort
*Tradescantia ohiensis / T. virginiana*

![Spiderwort (Tradescantia ohiensis) — blue-purple three-petaled flowers and strap-like leaves](images/native-plants/tradescantia-ohiensis.jpg)

**Identification**
An upright perennial (1–3 feet) with long, narrow, strap-like leaves and clusters of bright blue-to-violet three-petaled flowers at the stem tips. The sap from broken stems is mucilaginous and forms strings when pulled apart — hence "spiderwort." The flowers are distinctive and open in the morning, closing by midday.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, open woodlands, meadows. Throughout the Southeast.

**Season**
Young shoots: spring. Flowers: spring through summer. Leaves: spring through early summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and leaves (cooked as greens). Flowers (raw as garnish). Mucilaginous sap (used as a thickener).

**Preparation**
Young shoots and leaves: steam, sauté, or add to soups — mild flavor, slightly mucilaginous texture. Flowers: raw in salads. The mucilaginous quality makes spiderwort useful for thickening soups.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive three-petaled blue-violet flower. No toxic *Tradescantia* species in the Southeast.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Muscadine Grape
*Vitis rotundifolia*

![Muscadine Grape (Vitis rotundifolia) — large dark purple round grapes in small clusters](images/native-plants/vitis-rotundifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
A native grape vine with distinctively large, round berries (¼–1 inch diameter). Leaves are rounded, heart-shaped, with coarsely toothed edges and **shiny, non-lobed surfaces** (unlike most wild grapes). Bark is reddish-brown and somewhat shaggy. Fruit grows in small, loose clusters of 3–10 berries rather than the dense clusters of northern wild grapes.

**Where to Find It**
Southeastern lowland forests, forest edges, stream banks, roadsides, fence lines. Primary range: Southeast from Maryland to Florida and west to Texas.

**Season**
Late August through October. Berries ripen progressively — pick when they fall easily with gentle pressure.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries — the thick skin is edible but tough; most people pop the pulp out of the skin. Seeds edible.

**Preparation**
Fresh eating: pop the pulp out of the thick skin with your tongue. Jelly (excellent — traditional muscadine jelly is a Southern classic). Wine. Juice. Jam.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the distinctive large, round muscadine berry. Other wild *Vitis* species in the region are also edible.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pawpaw
*Asimina triloba*

![Pawpaw (Asimina triloba) — large drooping leaves and yellow-green custard-like fruit](images/native-plants/asimina-triloba.jpg)

**Identification**
Small understory tree (10–25 feet) with large, simple, drooping leaves (8–12 inches long). In spring, unusual maroon-to-brown bell-shaped flowers appear before the leaves fully emerge. The fruit: oblong, 2–6 inches long, green ripening to yellow-green, with soft, custard-like flesh and two rows of large dark seeds.

**Where to Find It**
Moist hardwood forest understory, stream banks, river bottomlands. More common and productive in the Southeast and mid-Atlantic than further north.

**Season**
Fruit ripens late August through October (peak September). Once ripe, falls from the tree within days and spoils quickly.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin and seeds not eaten.

**Preparation**
Fresh: split in half, scoop flesh with a spoon. Puréed (seeds removed): smoothies, ice cream, custards. Freezes well as purée. Flavor: banana, mango, and vanilla custard — North America's only truly tropical-flavored native fruit.

**Safety**
Skin and seeds contain alkaloids — avoid eating them. Small number of people are sensitive to the fruit itself and experience nausea; try a small amount first.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pecan
*Carya illinoinensis*

![Pecan (Carya illinoinensis) — compound leaves and elongated nut clusters](images/native-plants/carya-illinoinensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large tree (70–150 feet) with 9–17 narrowly lance-shaped, toothed, alternate leaflets. Bark is gray-brown with irregular ridges. Nuts grow in clusters of 2–6, enclosed in a thin four-valved husk that splits at maturity revealing the familiar elongated pecan nut with a thin, smooth shell.

**Where to Find It**
Bottomland hardwood forests, stream banks, river valleys. Native from Illinois to Texas; widely planted throughout the Southeast.

**Season**
Nuts ripen and fall September–November. Harvest from ground.

**Edible Parts**
Nutmeat inside the thin shell.

**Preparation**
Crack the thin shell (much easier than black walnut). Eat raw — outstanding flavor. Use in any recipe requiring pecans. Calorie-dense and a critical survival food.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive elongated pecan nut in its thin-hulled four-valved husk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Red Mulberry
*Morus rubra*

![Red Mulberry (Morus rubra) — variable-shaped leaves and dark red to black fruit](images/native-plants/morus-rubra.jpg)

**Identification**
Native tree (20–70 feet) with highly variable leaves — same tree simultaneously has simple oval, mitten-shaped (one-lobe), and multi-lobed leaves. Leaves are alternate, toothed, and rough-textured on the upper surface. The fruit resembles a large elongated blackberry — 1–1½ inches, ripening from pink to deep red to nearly black when fully ripe.

**Where to Find It**
Bottomland forests, field edges, stream banks, fence lines, urban areas. Throughout the Southeast.

**Season**
Early summer (May–June in the Deep South; earlier in Florida). A mature tree produces enormous quantities of fruit over several weeks.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit. Fully dark ripe berries are sweet and excellent; less-ripe pink ones are tart.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw. Jam. Wine. Cordial. Pie. Freeze for year-round use. The flavor is rich, sweet, slightly wine-like.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe dark mulberries on a tree. Unripe mulberries (white or pale pink) are mildly hallucinogenic/laxative — eat only when dark ripe. White mulberry (*M. alba*), introduced from Asia, has also naturalized — smaller, less flavorful white to pale fruit, also edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Persimmon
*Diospyros virginiana*

![American Persimmon (Diospyros virginiana) — orange persimmon fruit on bare branches](images/native-plants/diospyros-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
A medium tree (30–60 feet) with distinctive blocky, gray-to-black bark divided into small square or rectangular plates — one of the most recognizable bark patterns of any eastern tree. Alternate, oval, leathery leaves turn orange-red in fall. The fruit is round, about 1–2 inches, turning from green to orange-yellow when ripe, often with a powdery bloom. Calyx (dried flower base) persistent at fruit top.

**Where to Find It**
More widespread and productive in the Southeast than the Northeast — common throughout all lowland and mid-elevation areas from Virginia to Florida and west to Texas. Forest edges, old fields, roadsides, dry slopes. Tolerates poor soil.

**Season**
Fruit ripens in fall (September–November). Must be fully ripe — after the first frost is ideal. Unripe fruit is outrageously astringent.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin edible but thick. Seeds edible (roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Fully ripe fruit: eat raw, pulp out seeds, use in pudding (persimmon pudding is a classic). Dry for persimmon candy. Pulp freezes well. Beer. Seeds: roast as coffee substitute. Unripe persimmon puckering is legendary — always taste a tiny amount first.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive blocky bark and the characteristic orange fruit. If it puckers your mouth, it is not ripe — wait for full frost-softening.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hawthorn
*Crataegus* spp.

![Hawthorn (Crataegus) — small red berries and thorny branches with lobed leaves](images/native-plants/crataegus.jpg)

**Identification**
Small thorny trees or shrubs (10–30 feet) with alternate, deeply lobed leaves, stiff thorns (1–3 inches), white five-petaled flowers in spring, and small apple-like fruit (haws) 1/4–1 inch diameter, red to orange to dark purple when ripe. Over 200 species in North America — identification to species level is difficult, but all are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, old fields, thickets. Throughout the Southeast.

**Season**
Fruit (haws) ripen August–October. Best after frost.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not chew seeds — they contain cyanogenic compounds.

**Preparation**
Jelly and jam (high pectin). Fruit butter. Dried haws as a tart fruit snack. The flavor ranges from bland to pleasantly tart depending on species and ripeness.

**Safety**
Do not crack or chew seeds. All North American hawthorn species have edible fruit. No toxic lookalikes among small thorny trees with the characteristic haws.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mayhaw
*Crataegus aestivalis / C. opaca*

*[Photo needed: Crataegus aestivalis — small red spring-ripening haws on thorny branches in Gulf Coast bottomland. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A specific hawthorn species native to the Gulf Coast swamps and stream banks of the Southeast. The fruit is smaller than most haws (¼–½ inch), bright red, and ripens unusually early — in April–May ("May haw"). Same leaf, thorn, and fruit structure as other hawthorns but the early-spring ripening is diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Wet bottomlands, swamp margins, flooded forests of the Gulf Coastal Plain from Virginia to Texas.

**Season**
Fruit ripens April–May. Collect by spreading a sheet under trees and shaking, or harvest from water (flooded haws float).

**Preparation / Safety:** Same as hawthorn above. Mayhaw jelly is a celebrated regional specialty throughout Louisiana, Mississippi, and Georgia.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Highbush Blueberry
*Vaccinium corymbosum*

![Highbush Blueberry (Vaccinium corymbosum) — clusters of ripe blue berries with five-pointed crown](images/native-plants/vaccinium-corymbosum.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with small oval leaves with fine serrations, white to pink urn-shaped flowers in spring, and blue to blue-black berries with a characteristic five-pointed star-shaped crown (calyx) at the blossom end. The crown is the key distinguishing feature from toxic lookalikes.

**Where to Find It**
Wet bogs, pocosins, swamp margins, acidic forest edges. Throughout the Southeast coastal plain.

**Season**
June–August depending on location.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries.

**Preparation / Safety:** Same as Wild Blueberry entry in Northeast. The five-pointed crown distinguishes blueberries from any toxic lookalike.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Blueberry / Lowbush Blueberry
*Vaccinium angustifolium* and related low-bush species

![Wild Blueberry (Vaccinium angustifolium) — low shrub with clusters of small dark blue berries](images/native-plants/vaccinium-angustifolium.jpg)

**Identification**
A low-growing shrub (6–24 inches) with small, elliptical, finely serrated alternate leaves. Tiny white to pink urn-shaped flowers in spring. Berries are small (¼–½ inch), blue-black with a waxy white bloom, growing in clusters. The five-pointed star-shaped calyx at the blossom end is the key identification feature shared by all *Vaccinium* species.

**Where to Find It**
Acidic, sandy, or rocky soils throughout the Appalachian highlands and coastal plain of the Southeast. Open barrens, forest edges, burned-over areas, and heath-covered mountain slopes. Particularly productive at higher elevations in the Southern Appalachians.

**Season**
Berries ripen July–August at higher elevations; earlier at lower elevations and on the coastal plain.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries only.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — intense flavor, far superior to cultivated blueberries. Jam, pie, muffins, syrup, dried. Freezes exceptionally well.

**Safety**
The five-pointed calyx crown at the berry tip is the key distinguishing feature. No dangerous lookalikes once the crown is confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus canadensis* (American Elder)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark purple-to-black spheres in heavy drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, forest edges, fence rows, disturbed moist areas, floodplains. Very common throughout the Southeast, from Virginia to Florida. One of the most productive edible shrubs in the region.

**Season**
Flowers: late May through July. Berries: late August through October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. **Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.**

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems (don't eat stems), steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine, pie.

**Safety**
**LOOKALIKE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* has red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster — berries are toxic raw. **Blue/black berries in a flat-topped cluster = edible when cooked. Red berries in domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Also distinguish from Poison Hemlock (*Conium*) which can share habitat — elder has pithy hollow stems and compound leaves; hemlock has spotted hollow stems.

---

## FUNGI

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chanterelle
*Cantharellus cibarius* and related species

![Chanterelle (Cantharellus cibarius) — golden mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-cibarius.jpg)

**Identification**
Golden-yellow to egg-yolk orange mushrooms with a wavy, irregular cap margin, often funnel-shaped with age. The critical feature: **false gills** — beneath the cap are blunt, forking ridges (not thin knife-like gills) that run partway down the stem. These ridges are thick, widely spaced, and fork repeatedly. Flesh is solid white. Fruity, apricot-like smell.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood and mixed forests, particularly under oak, beech, and maple. The Southeast, especially the Southern Appalachians, has one of the highest chanterelle densities in North America. Grows from soil (not wood). Solitary or scattered.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Most productive after rain followed by warm weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously — absorbs water and becomes mushy. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. Excellent in cream sauces and pasta.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus illudens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at the base of trees or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), and gills glow faintly in the dark. Causes severe vomiting and diarrhea. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicken of the Woods
*Laetiporus sulphureus / L. cincinnatus*

![Chicken of the Woods (Laetiporus sulphureus) — bright orange-yellow shelf fungus on tree trunk](images/native-plants/laetiporus-sulphureus.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: bright orange-to-yellow shelf-like brackets growing in overlapping tiers directly from tree trunks or stumps. The top surface is vibrant orange; the underside is pale yellow to cream with tiny pores (no gills). When fresh, the flesh is tender, moist, and white. *L. sulphureus* grows on oaks and hardwoods; *L. cincinnatus* grows at the base of oaks with a paler, salmon-pink color.

**Where to Find It**
Living or dead oaks, cherry, locust, beech, willow. Very common throughout the Southeast on oaks and other hardwoods. Productive summer through fall. Can appear on the same tree annually.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Fresh specimens are best — older specimens become tough and bitter.

**Edible Parts**
Young, tender outer edges of fresh specimens.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or olive oil. The flavor and texture genuinely resemble chicken. Use in stir-fries, tacos, sandwiches, pasta. Must be fully cooked.

**Safety**
Some people react to specimens growing on certain tree species (black locust, eucalyptus, conifers) — stick to oak-grown specimens. Try a small amount first. No dangerous lookalikes — the color, shelf form, and pore surface (not gills) are distinctive.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hen of the Woods / Maitake
*Grifola frondosa*

![Hen of the Woods (Grifola frondosa) — overlapping gray-brown fronds at tree base](images/native-plants/grifola-frondosa.jpg)

**Identification**
A large polypore mushroom growing at the base of oaks or from buried roots as a dense cluster of overlapping, fan-shaped gray-to-brown fronds with white to cream pore surfaces underneath. The fronds are thin and flexible, not shelf-like. The entire mass rises from a single central base. Smells pleasantly earthy.

**Where to Find It**
Base of mature oaks throughout the Southeast. Returns to the same spot annually. Productive fall harvest.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh fruiting body. Tender outer fronds are best.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or oil, roast in the oven, add to soups and stir-fries. One of the most flavorful and versatile wild mushrooms. Dries well for storage.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes for a large gray-brown overlapping frond cluster at an oak base. Berkeley's Polypore (*Bondarzewia berkeleyi*) is similar but much paler, grows from roots, and is edible (though tougher).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. **True gills** run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, stubby, off-center or absent. White spore print. Found in cool weather on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead or dying hardwoods — beech, oak, maple, elm, willow throughout the Southeast. Year-round in the Southeast — can be found in winter months during mild weather on dead hardwoods.

**Season**
Fall through spring in the Southeast; most productive when temperatures drop below 60°F. May survive brief freezes.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well.

**Safety**
Angel wings (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) grows on conifer wood and is smaller and pure white. Avoid. Oyster mushrooms on hardwoods with the described characteristics are generally reliable, but avoid specimens that are old, soft, or smell sour.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, meadow edges, forest clearings, lawns, disturbed ground throughout the Southeast. Appears in late summer and fall.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which could indicate a deadly *Amanita* button), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel Mushroom
*Morchella* spp.

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — this is the single most important identification criterion. Cut vertically: the cap and stem form one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood forests — especially near dying or recently dead elm, ash, apple, and tulip poplar. South-facing slopes where soil warms first. In the Southeast, morels appear earlier than in the Northeast — February at lower elevations in the Deep South, March–April in Appalachia. Look under dying elms and tulip poplars.

**Season**
January–April depending on elevation and latitude. Earlier than in the Northeast — one of the first significant foraging events of the Southeast year.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset. Sauté in butter over medium-high heat 8–12 minutes until golden. Clean carefully: slice vertically and rinse, as the hollow cavity traps soil. Add to cream sauces, eggs, risotto, pasta. Dry and store for year-round use.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* and *Verpa* species) — POISONOUS, some potentially FATAL. False morels differ: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap attaches only at the top (not at the base), cap surface is wrinkled or brain-like (not regular honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lobster Mushroom
*Hypomyces lactifluorum*

![Lobster Mushroom (Hypomyces lactifluorum) — bright orange-red parasitized mushroom](images/native-plants/lobster-mushroom.jpg)

**Identification**
Not a single mushroom species but a parasitic fungus (*Hypomyces lactifluorum*) that transforms its host mushroom (typically *Russula* or *Lactarius* species) into a bright orange-to-red, dense, distorted mass with a lobster-colored exterior and white interior. The surface is rough-textured with tiny pimple-like pores. The bright orange color is unmistakable.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood and mixed forests, especially under beech and oak. Appears summer through fall.

**Season**
Summer through fall (July–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire transformed fruiting body when fresh and firm.

**Preparation**
The flavor is outstanding — mild, dense, with a seafood-like quality resembling lobster bisque. Sauté in butter, add to cream sauces, soups. The firm flesh holds up well to cooking.

**Safety**
The parasite transforms a *Russula* or *Lactarius* host — both of which may include some mildly toxic species. However, once fully parasitized and the entire external surface is bright orange, the host is no longer identifiable and the *Hypomyces* transformation is considered to eliminate any toxicity concern. Do not eat if the surface is not fully orange or if any white host mushroom gills remain visible.

---

---

# REGION 3: MIDWEST AND GREAT LAKES
*Ohio, Indiana, Illinois, Michigan, Wisconsin, Minnesota, Iowa, Missouri*

The Midwest foraging landscape moves from prairie edges to Great Lakes hardwood forests. Spring morels are legendary — the sandy soils around dying elms produce extraordinary flushes. The Great Lakes moderate temperatures and create unique plant communities on their shores. Fall brings chanterelles, hickory nuts, and elderberries.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ramps / Wild Leeks
*Allium tricoccum*

![Ramps (Allium tricoccum) — broad leaves emerging in moist hardwood forest](images/native-plants/allium-tricoccum.jpg)

**Identification**
Broad, smooth-edged, lance-shaped leaves emerge in early spring from a slender reddish-pink stem. Leaves are 4–10 inches long, 1–3 inches wide, growing in clumps of 2–3 from a thin white bulb. The single most reliable ID feature: crush a leaf and it releases an unmistakable pungent onion-garlic scent. No onion smell = not ramps.

**Where to Find It**
Common in moist hardwood ravines and stream corridors throughout the southern Midwest (OH, IN, IL, MO). Less common in Minnesota and northern Wisconsin. Look under sugar maple, yellow birch, and basswood in rich bottomland forest.

**Season**
Mid-April through mid-May. Window is 3–4 weeks.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: bulbs, stems, and leaves. Flowers also edible.

**Preparation**
Raw leaves in salads, pesto, or as wraps. Bulbs pickled in white wine vinegar. Sautéed whole in butter. Grilled whole over open fire. Ramp kimchi.

**Safety**
*Lily of the Valley (Convallaria majalis)* — TOXIC — grows in similar habitat with similar broad leaves. No onion smell. Crush the leaf: if it smells like flowers or nothing, put it down. The smell test is absolute.

**Notes**
Harvest only one leaf per plant and leave the bulb, or take no more than 10% of any patch's bulbs.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fiddlehead Fern (Ostrich Fern)
*Matteuccia struthiopteris*

![Fiddlehead Fern (Matteuccia struthiopteris) — tightly coiled spring shoots](images/native-plants/matteuccia-struthiopteris.jpg)

**Identification**
Tightly coiled, bright emerald-green fronds emerging from a central crown in early spring. Two structural features confirm *Matteuccia* specifically: (1) a **deep U-shaped groove** (not V-shaped) on the smooth green inner face of the stem; (2) **papery brown sheaths** that wrap the coil loosely and rub off cleanly between your fingers. Stems are smooth and hairless.

**Where to Find It**
Floodplains, stream banks, moist low-lying woodlands throughout the Midwest. Forms large colonies in river bottomlands — particularly abundant along Great Lakes tributaries and Midwest river systems.

**Season**
2–4 weeks in early-to-mid spring (April–May). Once coils begin to unfurl, they toughen and become less palatable.

**Edible Parts**
Young tightly-coiled fronds only. Harvest when 4–16 inches tall and still fully coiled.

**Preparation**
Must be cooked — raw fiddleheads cause gastrointestinal upset even when correctly identified. Boil or steam 5–10 minutes. Sauté with butter, garlic, lemon. Add to pasta or stir-fry. Pickle in white wine vinegar brine.

**Safety**
**Cinnamon Fern (*Osmunda cinnamomea*)** produces fiddleheads in similar habitat but has woolly brown sheaths that do not rub off cleanly. Not considered safe to eat. Always confirm the U-shaped groove and clean-rubbing papery sheath. Cook before eating.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — opposite toothed leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 2–5 feet tall with opposite, coarsely toothed, pointed leaves on a square stem. Hollow stinging hairs covering all surfaces inject formic acid and histamine on contact. Drooping clusters of inconspicuous green flowers appear from leaf axils.

**Where to Find It**
Nitrogen-rich disturbed soil — stream banks, old farmyards, compost piles, roadsides, forest edges. Very common in moist disturbed areas throughout the Midwest.

**Season**
Harvest young shoots and top 4–6 inches of growth in early spring (March–May) **before the plant flowers.** Post-flowering nettles develop kidney-irritating alkaloids and should not be eaten.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and tender leaves only, before flowering.

**Preparation**
Wear thick gloves to harvest. Cooking for 5 minutes completely neutralizes the sting. Rich flavor, similar to spinach but earthier. Nettle soup with potato and onion. Blanched into pasta dough. Dried and brewed as tea.

**Safety**
**Do not eat after the plant flowers.** Wear thick gloves — thin rubber gloves do not stop the hairs.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — basal rosette and flower](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Basal rosette of deeply lobed, toothed leaves from a central crown at ground level — no stem leaves, ever. A single hollow leafless stem bears one yellow flower head, maturing into the familiar spherical seed ball. White milky latex bleeds from any broken stem or leaf. Taproot is long, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, fields, disturbed areas throughout the Midwest. Virtually everywhere.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Young spring leaves are least bitter. Roots most concentrated in fall.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: leaves, flowers, roots, flower buds.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads with vinaigrette. Older leaves: blanch 2 minutes, sauté with garlic and olive oil. Flowers battered and fried, or made into cordial or wine. Flower buds pickled like capers. Roots roasted at 350°F until dark, ground as caffeine-free coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. Avoid plants from lawns treated with herbicides.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small, opposite, oval, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 deeply notched petals appearing as 10. The key feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, at lawn edges, disturbed areas. Common throughout the Midwest in partially shaded moist spots.

**Season**
Late winter through spring. Often the first edible green of the season in the Midwest — appearing while frost is still possible.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy flavor. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green combined with more flavorful foraged greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Asparagus
*Asparagus officinalis*

![Wild Asparagus (Asparagus officinalis) — spears emerging along sunny fence line](images/native-plants/asparagus-officinalis.jpg)

**Identification**
Escaped cultivated asparagus — the same species, naturalized for centuries. Spring spears are smooth, green, and identical to supermarket asparagus but often thinner. In late summer and fall, mature plants grow to 3–5 feet as feathery, fern-like masses of fine needle-like foliage that turn golden in fall — this is when to locate and mark patches for the following spring.

**Where to Find It**
Particularly abundant along rural roadsides and drainage ditches throughout the Great Plains fringe and the Midwest. Full-sun locations: fence lines, field edges, ditches, abandoned homesteads. Once established, a patch persists for decades.

**Season**
Harvest in early spring (April–May) when spears are 4–8 inches tall and before tips open.

**Edible Parts**
Young spears only. Snap at the natural breaking point where tender meets tough.

**Preparation**
Steam, roast, grill, or sauté. Raw when very young and thin. Wild asparagus flavor is often more intense than cultivated.

**Safety**
Distinctive enough in both spear and mature form that misidentification is unlikely.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis stricta*

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped leaflets and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Small, low-growing plant with three heart-shaped leaflets arranged like a clover, each with a distinct fold down the center. Immediately distinguished from clover by heart-shaped (not oval) leaflets. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers. Sharp, lemony-sour taste from oxalic acid — tasting a small leaf is a reliable identification aid.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, gardens, sidewalk cracks, disturbed ground, forest edges throughout the Midwest.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Most tender and abundant in spring.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods. Use as seasoning or snack, not as a primary food source.

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail snack or salad ingredient. The sour flavor functions as a lemon substitute. Brew in water for a tart wild "lemonade."

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid. Safe in moderate amounts. **Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, arthritis, or kidney disease.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia*

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — sword-like leaves and brown cylindrical seed spike in wetland](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: flat, sword-like leaves up to 9 feet tall in wetland settings, topped by the distinctive brown cylindrical seed spike. Both *T. latifolia* and *T. angustifolia* are fully edible.

**Where to Find It**
Freshwater wetlands, marshes, swamps, ponds, slow-moving streams, ditches throughout the Midwest. Common along Great Lakes shorelines and in inland marshes.

**Season**
Multiple harvest windows: young shoots (spring), green flower spikes and pollen (early summer), rhizomes (fall–winter).

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots, green flower spikes, pollen, and rhizomes.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel to pale inner core; eat raw or steamed. Green flower spikes: boil 10 minutes; eat like corn on the cob. Pollen: shake into a bag; mix into pancakes as 25% flour replacement. Rhizomes: pound in water, settle starch, drain, dry into flour.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in its wetland habitat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Jerusalem Artichoke / Sunchoke
*Helianthus tuberosus*

![Jerusalem Artichoke (Helianthus tuberosus) — tall sunflower plant with knobby tubers](images/native-plants/helianthus-tuberosus.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall, coarse-stemmed sunflower, 5–10 feet tall with rough, alternate leaves and bright yellow sunflower-like flowers in late summer. Underground: irregularly shaped, knobby tubers connected in clusters, brown-skinned with white flesh, resembling ginger root.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, stream banks, old fields throughout the Midwest. Often persists at old homestead sites.

**Season**
Tubers best in fall (after frost sweetens them) through early spring. Dig after the plant dies back.

**Edible Parts**
Tubers only. Raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Eat raw (crisp, sweet). Roast whole in coals or oven. Slice thin and pan-fry. Mash. Soup. Pickle. **Warning:** inulin causes significant gas and digestive discomfort in many people, especially raw. Start with small amounts.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes once tubers are confirmed as a sunflower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. Diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides, compost piles. One of the most abundant edible weeds in Midwest farmland and rural areas.

**Season**
Late spring through fall. Young leaves and shoot tips best in early to midsummer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Add to soups, omelets, pasta.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating. Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, or kidney disease.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, dry, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, driveways, fields throughout the Midwest. Thrives in summer heat.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Peaks in hot weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads. Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. Exceptional in omega-3 fatty acid content.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems hollow and float. Distinctly peppery flavor.

**Where to Find It**
Clear springs, spring-fed streams, cold running water throughout the Midwest. **Harvest only from verified clean water sources** upstream of farms, roads, and septic systems.

**Season**
Best in cool months (spring and fall). Available year-round where springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. Peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Burdock
*Arctium minus / Arctium lappa*

![Burdock (Arctium minus) — large dock-like leaves and burr-covered seed heads](images/native-plants/arctium-minus.jpg)

**Identification**
A large biennial with enormous leaves (up to 2 feet across in the first year) — heart-shaped, woolly-white on the undersides, on long hollow petioles. Stout taproot 1–3 feet long, brown-skinned, white-fleshed. Second year: tall branching stalk (3–6 feet) with smaller leaves and distinctive round burr-covered seed heads.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, roadsides, forest edges, waste places, old fields throughout the Midwest. Very common in rural and agricultural areas.

**Season**
First-year root: fall through early spring. Leaf petioles and young leaves: spring. Young flower stalks of second-year plants: spring, before elongating.

**Edible Parts**
Root (primary food), leaf petioles (inner core), young second-year flower stalks (peeled).

**Preparation**
Root (gobo in Japanese cuisine): scrub, peel, slice thin. Sauté with sesame oil, sake, and soy (kinpira gobo). Add to stews and soups. Leaf petioles: peel outer stringy skin, boil 10 minutes, use like celery.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once confirmed with the distinctive burrs (second year) or enormous leaves with white woolly undersides.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicory
*Cichorium intybus*

![Chicory (Cichorium intybus) — bright blue daisy-like flowers and branching stems](images/native-plants/cichorium-intybus.jpg)

**Identification**
Stout perennial with basal rosette of toothed leaves similar to dandelion, but with clasping upper stem leaves. The most distinctive feature: bright blue daisy-like ray flowers, 1–2 inches across, that open only in morning sunlight and close by afternoon. Stems branching, hairy, angular. Thick taproot.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, fields, disturbed areas, meadows throughout the Midwest. Very common along rural roads.

**Season**
Young basal rosette leaves: spring and fall. Roots: fall (most concentrated). Flowers: summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (raw or cooked), roots (roasted coffee substitute), flowers (edible garnish).

**Preparation**
Young spring leaves raw in salads (bitter — combine with milder greens). Root coffee: dig fall roots, wash, chop, roast at 350°F until very dark, grind, brew — earthy, rich, caffeine-free coffee substitute. Same root used commercially as a coffee additive.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. The distinctive blue flowers and angular branching stem make chicory recognizable from a distance in bloom.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated. Leaves have prominently toothed edges with the central tooth at the tip smaller than or equal to adjacent teeth.

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, old fields, forest edges, roadsides throughout the Midwest. Full sun to partial shade.

**Season**
Flowers in May. Fruit ripens June–July. Leaves available spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea — high in vitamin C).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, baked goods, or dried. Flavor far more intense than cultivated strawberries. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*) — the small red berry with similar leaves is insipid but NOT toxic. Distinguished by upward-facing yellow flowers and berries that point upward. No dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Blackberry
*Rubus allegheniensis*

![Wild Blackberry (Rubus allegheniensis) — ripe black berries on thorny canes](images/native-plants/rubus-allegheniensis.jpg)

**Identification**
Thorny, arching canes 3–6 feet tall with compound leaves of 3–5 toothed leaflets. White five-petaled flowers in spring clusters. Fruit is a blackberry — an aggregate of drupelets ripening from green to red to glossy black.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, forest edges, roadsides, clearings, powerline cuts throughout the Midwest. Abundant in disturbed open areas.

**Season**
Flowers May–June. Fruit ripens July–August. Fully ripe fruit pulls free easily.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit, young spring shoots (peeled, eaten raw or cooked), and leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, in jam, pie, cordial, wine. Young spring shoots: peel the outer skin, eat the pale tender inner shoot raw.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes among blackberries in the Midwest. Pokeweed berries (TOXIC) grow on a distinctly different plant — large herbaceous stalk with magenta stems, no thorns.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Blue Violet
*Viola sororia*

![Common Blue Violet (Viola sororia) — heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers](images/native-plants/viola-sororia.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with heart-shaped, scallop-edged basal leaves 1–3 inches across. Flowers are deep violet-blue (sometimes white) with five petals, the lower three with darker veins. Grows from a short, thick rhizome.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, woodland edges, moist disturbed ground throughout the Midwest.

**Season**
Leaves: spring through early summer (most tender in spring). Flowers: April–May.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves and flowers both edible. Leaves are high in vitamin C and vitamin A.

**Preparation**
Leaves raw in salads or cooked as greens. Flowers as colorful garnish on salads and desserts. Flowers candied (dipped in egg white, coated in sugar, dried). Leaves can thicken soups.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for violets with the characteristic heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Ginger
*Asarum canadense*

![Wild Ginger (Asarum canadense) — heart-shaped leaves and single maroon flower at base](images/native-plants/asarum-canadense.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, slow-spreading ground cover with large, paired, heart-shaped, velvety leaves 3–6 inches across, growing close to the ground in colonies. A single small maroon-brown, three-lobed, urn-shaped flower hides at ground level under the leaf pair in early spring. The roots (rhizomes) smell strongly of ginger when crushed — the most reliable ID feature.

**Where to Find It**
Rich, moist deciduous forest with well-drained, loamy soil throughout the Midwest. Often associated with trillium and bloodroot on rich forest slopes.

**Season**
Rhizomes: spring through fall. The plant is slow-growing — harvest sparingly.

**Edible Parts**
Root/rhizome only (as a spice/flavoring). Used as a ginger substitute.

**Preparation**
Dig small sections of rhizome, wash, peel, and use fresh like ginger — in teas, marinades, baking. Dry and grind as a spice powder. Use in very small quantities as flavoring.

**Safety**
Wild ginger contains aristolochic acid, a compound associated with kidney damage in large quantities over long periods. Use as a flavoring spice only, in small amounts. Do not consume in medicinal doses daily.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Garlic / Canada Garlic
*Allium canadense*

![Wild Garlic (Allium canadense) — grass-like leaves and spherical flower cluster with bulblets](images/native-plants/allium-canadense.jpg)

**Identification**
Narrow, grass-like leaves rising from a central basal point. The flower cluster at the top of a single round stem bears pink to white flowers but often replaces flowers entirely with small purple-tipped bulblets. Smell is the primary identification: crush any part — it must smell unmistakably of onion or garlic. No smell = not edible.

**Where to Find It**
Meadows, prairie edges, forest edges, stream banks, roadsides, lawns. Throughout the Midwest.

**Season**
Spring through early summer for leaves and bulbs. Fall for bulb harvest after top dies back.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, bulbs, and bulblets.

**Preparation**
Leaves chopped fresh like chives. Bulbs cooked in any dish requiring onion or garlic. Pickled whole. Dried and stored. Bulblets planted like garlic cloves.

**Safety**
The smell test is the safety protocol — if it smells like onion, it is safe. No dangerous lookalike plant will smell like onion. See Ramps entry for the Lily of the Valley warning (applies here as well if grass-like leaves are found in moist forest — always smell test).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Milkweed
*Asclepias syriaca*

![Common Milkweed (Asclepias syriaca) — large leaves and spherical flower clusters](images/native-plants/asclepias-syriaca.jpg)

**Identification**
Stout, upright plant 2–5 feet tall with large, opposite, oval leaves 4–8 inches long. Stems and leaves exude white milky latex when broken. Rounded clusters of pink-to-lavender flowers in midsummer develop into distinctive warty, boat-shaped seed pods 3–5 inches long that split open in fall releasing silky-tailed seeds.

**Where to Find It**
One of the most abundant and productive wild food plants of the Midwest — old fields, roadsides, meadows, disturbed open ground, and prairie edges throughout Illinois, Indiana, Ohio, Michigan, Wisconsin, Missouri, and Iowa. Full sun, dry to moist soil. Essentially ubiquitous in disturbed open areas throughout the region.

**Season**
Young shoots: spring (May–June). Flower buds and flowers: early summer. Young pods: midsummer when pods are 1–2 inches and still firm inside. Seeds: fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (4–6 inches), flower buds, flowers, very young pods (before silk develops).

**Preparation**
All parts require boiling — change water once to reduce bitterness. Young shoots boiled 5 minutes taste like asparagus. Flower buds boiled briefly, tossed in butter. Young pods boiled, similar to okra. Raw milkweed is mildly toxic — always cook.

**Safety**
**Dogbane (*Apocynum cannabinum*)** — TOXIC — looks similar with opposite oval leaves and milky sap. Dogbane stems are smooth and often reddish; milkweed stems are hairy. Dogbane has narrow tubular white flowers; milkweed has globe-shaped pink clusters. Do not eat dogbane. When in doubt, smell the broken stem — milkweed has a distinctive sweet-bitter smell; dogbane is more acrid.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ground Ivy
*Glechoma hederacea*

![Ground Ivy (Glechoma hederacea) — scalloped kidney-shaped leaves and small purple flowers](images/native-plants/glechoma-hederacea.jpg)

**Identification**
Low creeping perennial with small, rounded, kidney-shaped leaves with scalloped edges on square stems. Small purple-blue tubular flowers appear in spring. The leaves are strongly aromatic when crushed — a distinctive sharp, minty-musty smell.

**Where to Find It**
Shaded lawns, garden edges, woodland edges, moist disturbed areas throughout the Midwest. Essentially found in every shaded lawn and disturbed area — an introduced species now completely naturalized throughout the region. Very common in Ohio, Indiana, Illinois, Michigan, and Wisconsin.

**Season**
Year-round. Most tender in spring.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots.

**Preparation**
Use fresh as a tea herb — steep in hot water for a pleasant mild tea with a hint of mint and resin. Add small amounts to salads. Use as a flavoring herb in soups and cooked dishes. Used historically to clarify ale ("alehoof").

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive scalloped kidney-shaped leaves on square stems and the minty-musty smell. Use in moderation — the volatile oils in large quantities may cause digestive irritation in some people.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Japanese Knotweed
*Fallopia japonica*

![Japanese Knotweed (Fallopia japonica) — hollow segmented stems and shovel-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/fallopia-japonica.jpg)

**Identification**
An aggressive invasive reaching 6–12 feet by midsummer. Hollow, bamboo-like stems with distinct nodes and shovel-shaped leaves with a flat or slightly notched base (not heart-shaped). Young spring shoots emerge as reddish-green asparagus-like spears. In fall, small white-cream flowers appear in clusters from leaf axils.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, stream banks, river floodplains, and disturbed areas throughout the Midwest. Japanese knotweed is invasive — it forms dense monoculture stands displacing native vegetation. Foraging it is ecologically encouraged. Particularly common along Illinois, Indiana, and Ohio river corridors and in urban riparian zones.

**Season**
Young shoots in early spring (April–May in the Midwest) when less than 8 inches tall. Older stems are too fibrous.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots only. Hollow stems at 4–8 inches, before leaves fully expand.

**Preparation**
Flavor similar to rhubarb — sour and tangy from oxalic acid. Chop and cook in pies, crumbles, chutneys, or sauces exactly as you would rhubarb. Can be juiced and fermented. Do not eat large quantities raw.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — same cautions as rhubarb. Do not eat in large raw quantities. Foraging it is ecologically encouraged (invasive species).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Groundnut / Hopniss
*Apios americana*

![Groundnut (Apios americana) — vine with bead-like chain of tubers underground](images/native-plants/apios-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A twining vine growing 4–20 feet, climbing over other vegetation. Compound leaves with 5–7 egg-shaped leaflets. Dense clusters of brownish-pink flowers in midsummer. Underground: a chain of oval starchy tubers connected like beads on a cord, ½–3 inches long, with rough brown skin and white starchy interior. The chain-of-tubers root structure is the primary confirmation.

**Where to Find It**
Moist disturbed areas, stream banks, woodland edges, and thickets throughout the Midwest. Widely distributed from Ohio and Indiana south through Missouri and Illinois. Prefers moist, shaded areas along stream corridors and floodplain forests — look for the vine climbing on shrubs along river bottomlands.

**Season**
Tubers harvestable fall through spring. Best in fall after the vine dies back — locate by the dead vine. Seed pods with edible beans: summer.

**Edible Parts**
Tubers (primary food), seed beans (cooked), flowers and leaves (edible).

**Preparation**
Peel the fibrous brown skin. Boil, steam, roast, or bake — treat like a small, drier, nuttier potato. Slice and pan-fry in fat until crispy. Mash with butter. Seed beans: boil like any bean.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive chain-of-tubers root structure. A foundational survival food historically used by Indigenous nations throughout the Midwest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Lotus
*Nelumbo lutea*

![American Lotus (Nelumbo lutea) — large round floating leaves and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/nelumbo-lutea.jpg)

**Identification**
Large aquatic perennial with enormous round leaves (1–2 feet across) that rise above the water surface on long petioles, shedding water like a water-resistant coating. Large, pale yellow flowers 4–8 inches across in summer. Distinctive seed head: a flat-topped structure resembling a shower head with oval seeds embedded in holes. Rhizomes are thick, chambered, resembling water chestnuts, buried in pond mud.

**Where to Find It**
River backwaters, oxbow lakes, shallow ponds, and slow-moving river systems throughout the Midwest. Exceptionally productive in the Mississippi, Missouri, and Ohio River watersheds — some of the most extensive lotus stands in North America are found in the backwaters and oxbow lakes of the Illinois and Mississippi River systems. Look for the large round leaves rising above still or slow-moving water in midsummer.

**Season**
Young leaves emerging: spring. Seeds in the seed head: fall. Rhizomes: fall through winter.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked), seeds (raw or roasted), seed head (as a vessel/container when dry), rhizomes (cooked), and young leaf stalks.

**Preparation**
Seeds: remove from seed head, shell, eat raw (starchy, mild) or roast. Rhizomes: dig from mud, wash, slice into rounds, sauté, or add to soups — similar to lotus root used in Asian cuisine, with characteristic holes in cross-section. Young leaves: steam and use as wraps or cooked greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive round leaves and yellow flowers. Wade carefully in pond mud.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus canadensis*

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark purple-to-black spheres in heavy drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, forest edges, fence rows, disturbed moist areas, and floodplains throughout the Midwest. One of the most productive edible shrubs in the region — abundant along stream corridors in Illinois, Indiana, Ohio, Michigan, Wisconsin, and Missouri. Common in riparian corridors and old farm fields throughout the region.

**Season**
Flowers: late May through July. Berries: late August through October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems (don't eat stems), steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine, pie.

**Safety**
**LOOKALIKE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* has red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster — berries are toxic raw. **Black/Blue Elder = flat-topped cluster + purple-black berries = edible when cooked. Red berries in domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Also distinguish from Poison Hemlock (*Conium*) which can share habitat — elder has pithy hollow stems and compound leaves; hemlock has spotted hollow stems.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Black Walnut
*Juglans nigra*

![Black Walnut (Juglans nigra) — compound leaves and round green-hulled nuts](images/native-plants/juglans-nigra.jpg)

**Identification**
A large tree (50–100 feet) with alternate, pinnately compound leaves (15–23 leaflets). The bark of mature trees is deeply ridged and dark gray to nearly black. The fruit is a round nut (1–3 inches diameter) enclosed in a thick green husk that turns black when ripe. The inner nut shell is very hard and corrugated. Crush any part of the husk — a strong, distinctive, pungent walnut smell.

**Where to Find It**
Moist, rich, well-drained soils — forest edges, stream banks, valleys, old homesteads, and farmstead windbreaks throughout the Midwest. Very common in Ohio, Indiana, Illinois, and Missouri. One of the most productive wild nut trees in the region; production years alternate between heavy and light crops, but a good crop year yields enormous quantities of nuts.

**Season**
Nuts ripen and fall September–October. Collect from the ground after they fall.

**Edible Parts**
The nutmeat inside the hard inner shell.

**Preparation**
**WEAR GLOVES** — the green hull contains juglone, which permanently stains skin dark brown. Remove the green husk (stomp on it or use gloves), rinse nuts, cure in a single layer in a cool, dry spot for 2–3 weeks. Crack the very hard shell with a hammer or dedicated black walnut cracker. The flavor is distinctly more intense and complex than English walnut. Use in baked goods, ice cream, salads.

**Safety**
Juglone from the hull stains skin (harmless but persistent) and is toxic to some plants — do not compost hulls near tomatoes or other juglone-sensitive plants. The nut itself is safe and nutritious.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Shagbark Hickory
*Carya ovata*

![Shagbark Hickory (Carya ovata) — distinctive shaggy peeling bark and compound leaves](images/native-plants/carya-ovata.jpg)

**Identification**
A large tree (60–100 feet) whose name is its best identification: the gray bark peels in long, distinctive shaggy plates curling away from the trunk at both ends like a shaggy coat — unmistakable. Compound leaves with 5 leaflets (the terminal leaflet largest). Nuts enclosed in a thick, four-valved green husk that turns brown and splits at maturity, revealing a tan to white shell.

**Where to Find It**
Dry to moist upland hardwood forests throughout the Midwest — very common in Ohio, Indiana, Illinois, Michigan, and Wisconsin. Common in mixed hardwood stands with oaks and maples. The distinctive shaggy bark is visible from a distance, making this tree easy to locate.

**Season**
Nuts ripen and fall September–October.

**Edible Parts**
Nutmeat inside the shell.

**Preparation**
Crack with a hammer. Nutmeat is sweet and excellent — among the best-tasting of all North American native nuts. Use raw, in baked goods, or infuse into cream for ice cream and desserts. Traditional hickory nut cake is a classic Midwestern preparation.

**Safety**
Distinguish from bitternut hickory (*Carya cordiformis*) which has yellow-tipped buds, thinner husks, and bitter inedible nuts. Shagbark's unique peeling bark distinguishes it from all other trees.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Hazelnut
*Corylus americana*

![American Hazelnut (Corylus americana) — clusters of nuts in leafy husks](images/native-plants/corylus-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (3–10 feet) with rounded, finely toothed, alternate leaves with a heart-shaped base. The nuts grow in clusters of 2–4, each enclosed in ragged, leafy husks (involucres) that are longer than the nut. The round nut has a brown shell and resembles a small cultivated hazelnut.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, thickets, roadsides, stream banks, and open woodlands throughout the Midwest. Very common in Michigan, Wisconsin, Minnesota, Illinois, and Indiana — forms large thickets at forest edges and along roadsides. A productive hazel patch can yield pounds of nuts per visit.

**Season**
Nuts ripen August–September. Harvest before squirrels find them — check productive patches daily once nuts are visible.

**Edible Parts**
Nutmeat inside the shell.

**Preparation**
Crack and eat fresh (the best way — sweet, mild, rich). Roast lightly to deepen flavor. Grind into a meal or paste. Use in cookies, breads, or made into hazelnut butter. Dry nuts store for months.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive hazel nut in its ragged leafy husk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Grape
*Vitis labrusca* (Fox Grape) and *V. riparia* (Riverbank Grape)

![Fox Grape (Vitis labrusca) — large heart-shaped leaves and blue-black grapes](images/native-plants/vitis-labrusca.jpg)

**Identification**
Climbing vines on other vegetation with large, heart-shaped, sometimes lobed, coarsely toothed leaves. Bark of older vines shreds and peels. Tendrils and flower/fruit clusters grow from the vine. Grapes grow in dense clusters, ripening from green to red to dark purple or nearly black.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, thickets, stream banks, fence rows, open woodlands. Very common throughout the Midwest.

**Season**
Grapes ripen August–October. Fully dark grapes are sweet; partially ripe ones are tart.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit. Young spring leaves (edible — used in Greek and Middle Eastern cuisines as wraps). Tendrils (raw as trail nibble).

**Preparation**
Fresh eating. Juice (excellent). Jelly (classic Concord grape jelly comes from a *V. labrusca* cultivar). Wine. Young leaves: blanch 5 minutes, fill with seasoned rice or meat, roll, steam.

**Safety**
**Canada Moonseed (*Menispermum canadense*)** — TOXIC — has similar leaf shape and grows in similar habitat. Moonseed berries grow in grape-like clusters of black berries but have a single crescent-shaped seed (grape seeds are round). Moonseed has no tendrils (grapes do). Always check for tendrils.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pawpaw
*Asimina triloba*

![Pawpaw (Asimina triloba) — large drooping leaves and yellow-green custard-like fruit](images/native-plants/asimina-triloba.jpg)

**Identification**
Small understory tree (10–25 feet) with large, simple, drooping leaves (8–12 inches long). In spring, unusual maroon-to-brown bell-shaped flowers appear before the leaves fully emerge. The fruit: oblong, 2–6 inches long, green ripening to yellow-green, with soft, custard-like flesh and two rows of large dark seeds.

**Where to Find It**
Moist hardwood forest understory, stream banks, river bottomlands throughout the Midwest. Reaches its northern range limit in the Midwest — common along stream corridors in southern Michigan, Indiana, Ohio, and Missouri. Particularly productive in river bottomlands along the Ohio, Wabash, and Missouri Rivers.

**Season**
Fruit ripens late August through October (peak September). Once ripe, falls from the tree within days and spoils quickly — check daily once ripening begins.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin and seeds not eaten.

**Preparation**
Fresh: split in half, scoop flesh with a spoon. Puréed (seeds removed): smoothies, ice cream, custards. Freezes well as purée. Flavor: banana, mango, and vanilla custard — North America's only truly tropical-flavored native fruit.

**Safety**
Skin and seeds contain alkaloids — avoid eating them. A small number of people are sensitive to the fruit itself and experience nausea; try a small amount first.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Serviceberry / Juneberry
*Amelanchier* spp. (*A. arborea*, *A. canadensis*, *A. laevis*)

![Serviceberry (Amelanchier) — dark purple berries and finely toothed oval leaves](images/native-plants/amelanchier.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub to small tree (6–25 feet) with oval, finely serrated alternate leaves. One of the first trees to flower in spring — white star-shaped five-petaled flowers appear before or with the first leaves, often while snow is still possible. Fruit ripens in early summer (hence "Juneberry"), turning from red to dark purple when fully ripe.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, slopes, stream banks, roadsides, and open woodlands throughout the Midwest. Particularly abundant and productive along Great Lakes shorelines — serviceberry flowers in spectacular masses in spring along Lake Michigan, Lake Superior, and Lake Huron shorelines. Common throughout Michigan, Wisconsin, Minnesota, and northern Illinois.

**Season**
One of the earliest fruit harvests of the year. Fruit ripe June–July, window of 2–3 weeks. Fully purple berries are sweet; red berries still tart.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent. Dried like raisins. Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe purple serviceberries with their characteristic fine-toothed oval leaves. Confirm the five-petaled flower or the characteristic berry with a tiny five-pointed crown at the blossom end (like a miniature blueberry crown).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Red Mulberry
*Morus rubra*

![Red Mulberry (Morus rubra) — variable-shaped leaves and dark red to black fruit](images/native-plants/morus-rubra.jpg)

**Identification**
Native tree (20–70 feet) with highly variable leaves — same tree simultaneously has simple oval, mitten-shaped (one-lobe), and multi-lobed leaves. Leaves are alternate, toothed, and rough-textured on the upper surface. The fruit resembles a large elongated blackberry — 1–1½ inches, ripening from pink to deep red to nearly black when fully ripe.

**Where to Find It**
