Bottomland forests, field edges, stream banks, fence lines, and urban areas throughout the southern and central Midwest. Productive in Missouri, Illinois, Indiana, and southern Ohio — reaches the northern fringe of its range in the Great Lakes region. A mature tree produces enormous quantities of fruit over several weeks in early summer.

**Season**
Late spring to early summer (June in the southern Midwest; later further north). Fully dark ripe berries are sweet and excellent.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit. Fully dark ripe berries are sweet and excellent; less-ripe pink ones are tart.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw. Jam. Wine. Cordial. Pie. Freeze for year-round use. The flavor is rich, sweet, slightly wine-like.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe dark mulberries on a tree. Unripe mulberries (white or pale pink) are mildly hallucinogenic/laxative — eat only when dark ripe. White mulberry (*M. alba*), introduced from Asia, has also naturalized throughout the Midwest — smaller, less flavorful white to pale fruit, also edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Plum
*Prunus americana*

![Wild Plum (Prunus americana) — red-orange ripe plums on thorny branches](images/native-plants/prunus-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with thorny branches. Alternate, oval, sharply toothed leaves. Clusters of white five-petaled flowers in early spring before the leaves emerge. The fruit is a small plum (¾–1 inch diameter), ripening red to yellow, with a thin skin and juicy flesh enclosing a single pit.

**Where to Find It**
Thickets, roadsides, forest edges, stream banks, and old fence lines throughout the Midwest. Very common — forms dense thickets across the entire region, particularly in Iowa, Missouri, Illinois, Indiana, and Ohio. One of the most reliable and productive fruit sources in the Midwest landscape.

**Season**
Flowers: early spring (April in the southern Midwest, May further north). Fruit ripens July–August. Pick when fully colored and soft to the touch.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not crack or chew pits.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw (tart but good). Jelly (excellent — high natural pectin). Jam. Butter (cooked down with spices). Wine. Dried. The tartness diminishes significantly with cooking.

**Safety**
Like all *Prunus* species, the pits (and bark and leaves) contain cyanogenic glycosides — do not chew pits, strain seeds from juices. The ripe flesh is safe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chokecherry
*Prunus virginiana*

![Chokecherry (Prunus virginiana) — elongated fruit clusters (racemes) of dark berries](images/native-plants/prunus-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with alternate, oval, finely toothed leaves (1–4 inches). Bark grayish-red with small pores (lenticels). White flowers in elongated cylindrical clusters (racemes) — distinctly cylindrical, not flat-topped like elder. Berries ripen dark red to nearly black in late summer, in the same elongated cluster configuration.

**Where to Find It**
Thickets, stream banks, valley edges, rocky slopes, roadsides, forest edges. Throughout the Midwest and Great Plains.

**Season**
Flowers late April–June. Berries ripen July–September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berry flesh and juice only. Do not chew seeds.

**Preparation**
Very astringent raw — the name "chokecherry" is accurate. Jelly (excellent — natural pectin). Cordial (simmer berries with water and sugar, strain). Syrup. Dried fruit leather (traditional Plains preparation). **Do not chew pits** — cyanogenic.

**Safety**
Pits, leaves, and bark contain cyanogenic glycosides. Strain seeds from all processed products. The astringency of raw berries deters overconsumption.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Persimmon
*Diospyros virginiana*

![American Persimmon (Diospyros virginiana) — orange persimmon fruit on bare branches](images/native-plants/diospyros-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
A medium tree (30–60 feet) with distinctive blocky, gray-to-black bark divided into small square or rectangular plates — one of the most recognizable bark patterns of any eastern tree. Alternate, oval, leathery leaves turn orange-red in fall. The fruit is round, about 1–2 inches, turning from green to orange-yellow when ripe, often with a powdery bloom. Calyx (dried flower base) persistent at fruit top.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, old fields, roadsides, and dry slopes throughout the southern Midwest. Common in Missouri, Illinois, Indiana, and southern Ohio. Tolerates poor soil and is often found on dry upland ridges and in old fields. Reaches its northern range limit in the central Midwest.

**Season**
Fruit ripens in fall (September–November). Must be fully ripe — after the first frost is ideal. Unripe fruit is outrageously astringent.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin edible but thick. Seeds edible (roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Fully ripe fruit: eat raw, pulp out seeds, use in pudding (persimmon pudding is a classic Midwestern fall tradition). Dry for persimmon candy. Pulp freezes well. Seeds: roast as coffee substitute. Unripe persimmon puckering is legendary — always taste a tiny amount first.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive blocky bark and the characteristic orange fruit. If it puckers your mouth, it is not ripe — wait for full frost-softening.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Rose
*Rosa* spp. (*R. carolina*, *R. blanda*, *R. palustris*)

![Wild Rose (Rosa carolina) — pink five-petaled flowers and oval leaflets](images/native-plants/rosa-carolina.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs with thorny or prickly canes, alternate compound leaves with 5–9 oval toothed leaflets, and classic five-petaled pink (occasionally white) single rose flowers. The red to orange-red fruit (rose hip) is a swollen receptacle containing seeds surrounded by fleshy tissue. Hips develop in late summer and persist through winter.

**Where to Find It**
Open areas, roadsides, meadow edges, forest edges, and thickets throughout the Midwest. Very common — multiple native species grow across the region. Rose hips persist through winter, making this one of the most important late-season vitamin C sources in the Midwest landscape.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Rose hips: August through winter (best after first frost sweetens them).

**Edible Parts**
Petals (fresh), rose hips (processed), and young shoots in spring (peeled).

**Preparation**
Petals: fresh in salads, infused in syrups, dried for tea. Rose hips: cut off the blossom end and stem, slice in half, scrape out the seeds and fine hairs (both irritating), then use the flesh. Rose hip tea: dry hips and steep in hot water — extremely high in vitamin C. Rose hip jam, syrup, cordial.

**Safety**
Thorns are a physical hazard. The seeds and fine hairs inside the hip are irritating — always remove before consuming. No toxic lookalikes for the classic rose flower-and-hip combination.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Staghorn Sumac
*Rhus typhina*

![Staghorn Sumac (Rhus typhina) — compound leaves and upright red berry clusters](images/native-plants/rhus-typhina.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (10–25 feet) with large, pinnately compound leaves (11–31 leaflets) and velvety, densely-hairy branches (resembling deer antlers in velvet — hence the name). In summer, upright, cone-shaped clusters of tiny red berries develop at branch tips. Berries covered in short red hairs. The entire fruiting cluster is distinctly red and upright.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, old fields, forest edges, and dry rocky slopes throughout the Midwest. Forms large clonal colonies. Common throughout the Great Lakes states and Ohio valley. Staghorn sumac-ade is an excellent fall drink — the dried clusters persist through winter and can be harvested well into November before rain washes away the acidic coating.

**Season**
Berries ripen red in July–August and persist through winter. Best harvested before heavy rains wash away the acidic coating.

**Edible Parts**
The red berry clusters. The acidic coating on the berries is the edible/useful part.

**Preparation**
Sumac-ade: rub the berry cluster vigorously in cool water (NOT hot — hot water releases bitter tannins), strain through a cloth to remove hairs, sweeten to taste. The resulting pink drink tastes like lemonade. Dried, ground berries make a tart, lemony spice (the same as the *sumac* used in Middle Eastern cooking).

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** *Poison Sumac (Toxicodendron vernix)* — CAUSES SEVERE ALLERGIC REACTION (worse than poison ivy) — grows in WET habitats (swamps, marshes), has **white to greenish-white** berry clusters that hang **downward** from branches, and has smooth (not hairy) leaves with smooth-edged leaflets. Staghorn sumac: red upright clusters, hairy stems, dry habitat. The habitat and berry color/orientation are foolproof distinguishers.

---

## FUNGI

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel
*Morchella* spp. (*M. esculenta*, *M. elata*, *M. importuna*)

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — this is the single most important identification criterion. Cut vertically: the cap and stem form one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base. Cap color: tan to gray to near-black depending on species.

**Where to Find It**
The Midwest — especially Michigan, Ohio, and Indiana — is widely considered the heart of morel country in North America. Hardwood forests near dying or recently dead elm, ash, apple, and tulip poplar. Sandy soils with dying elm populations are particularly productive. South-facing slopes where soil warms first. Many Midwesterners consider morel hunting as serious a seasonal tradition as deer hunting, with closely guarded secret spots.

**Season**
Spring only. April–May in the Midwest. Soil temperature around 50°F triggers emergence. Black morels come first; yellow morels follow.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset. Sauté in butter over medium-high heat 8–12 minutes until golden. Slice vertically and rinse (the hollow cavity traps soil and insects). Add to cream sauces, eggs, risotto, pasta. Dry and store for year-round use.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* and *Verpa* species) — POISONOUS, some potentially FATAL. *Gyromitra* contains gyromitrin, a compound metabolized to monomethylhydrazine. False morels differ: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap attaches only at the top (not at the base), cap surface is wrinkled, brain-like, or saddle-shaped (not regular honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chanterelle
*Cantharellus cibarius* and related species

![Chanterelle (Cantharellus cibarius) — golden mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-cibarius.jpg)

**Identification**
Golden-yellow to egg-yolk orange mushrooms with a wavy, irregular cap margin, often funnel-shaped with age. The critical feature: **false gills** — beneath the cap are blunt, forking ridges (not thin knife-like gills) that run partway down the stem. These ridges are thick, widely spaced, and fork repeatedly. Flesh is solid white. Fruity, apricot-like smell.

**Where to Find It**
Hardwood and mixed forests under oak, beech, and maple throughout the Midwest. Common in Michigan, Ohio, Wisconsin, and Indiana hardwood forests from June through October. Most productive after summer rains followed by warm weather. Grows from soil, solitary or scattered, never in clusters.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Most productive after rain followed by warm weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously — absorbs water and becomes mushy. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. Excellent in cream sauces and pasta.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus illudens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at the base of trees or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), and gills glow faintly in the dark. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicken of the Woods
*Laetiporus sulphureus / L. cincinnatus*

![Chicken of the Woods (Laetiporus sulphureus) — bright orange-yellow shelf fungus on tree trunk](images/native-plants/laetiporus-sulphureus.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: bright orange-to-yellow shelf-like brackets growing in overlapping tiers directly from tree trunks or stumps. The top surface is vibrant orange; the underside is pale yellow to cream with tiny pores (no gills). When fresh, the flesh is tender, moist, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Living or dead oaks, cherry, locust, beech, willow throughout the Midwest. Very common on oaks throughout the entire region. Can appear on the same tree annually. *L. cincinnatus* grows at the base of oaks with a paler, salmon-pink color.

**Season**
Summer through fall (June–October). Fresh, tender specimens are best — older specimens become tough and bitter.

**Edible Parts**
Young, tender outer edges of fresh specimens. Avoid the tough inner portions near the wood.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or olive oil. The flavor and texture genuinely resemble chicken — hence the name. Use in stir-fries, tacos, sandwiches, pasta. Must be fully cooked.

**Safety**
Some people react to specimens growing on certain tree species (black locust, eucalyptus, conifers) — stick to oak-grown specimens. Some individuals are simply sensitive regardless of host tree. Try a small amount first. No dangerous lookalikes — the color, shelf form, and pore surface (not gills) are distinctive.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hen of the Woods / Maitake
*Grifola frondosa*

![Hen of the Woods (Grifola frondosa) — overlapping gray-brown fronds at tree base](images/native-plants/grifola-frondosa.jpg)

**Identification**
A large polypore mushroom (can weigh 20–100 lbs) growing at the base of oaks or from buried roots as a dense cluster of overlapping, fan-shaped gray-to-brown fronds with white to cream pore surfaces underneath. The fronds are thin and flexible, not shelf-like. The entire mass rises from a single central base. Smells pleasantly earthy and mushroom-like.

**Where to Find It**
Base of mature oaks (especially red and white oak) throughout the Midwest. Returns to the same spot annually — once you find a productive oak, mark it and return every fall. Common in Ohio, Indiana, Michigan, and Illinois hardwood forests. Peak season September–October.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh fruiting body. Tender outer fronds are best.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or oil, roast in the oven, add to soups and stir-fries. One of the most flavorful and versatile wild mushrooms. Dries well for storage.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes for a large gray-brown overlapping frond cluster at an oak base. Berkeley's Polypore (*Bondarzewia berkeleyi*) is similar but much paler, grows from roots, and is edible (though tougher).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. True gills run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, stubby, off-center or absent. White spore print. Found in cool weather on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead or dying hardwoods — beech, oak, maple, elm, willow — throughout the Midwest. Common on dead hardwoods throughout the entire region, particularly in spring and fall. Frequently found on dead elm trees in riparian corridors.

**Season**
Spring and fall, especially when temperatures drop. May survive brief freezes.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well. One of the most widely cultivated mushrooms — the wild version has identical flavor and food value.

**Safety**
Angel wings (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) grows on conifer wood and is smaller and pure white — avoid. Oyster mushrooms on hardwoods with the described characteristics are generally reliable, but avoid specimens that are old, soft, or smell sour.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, meadow edges, forest clearings, and lawns throughout the Midwest. Very common in Midwest meadows and old fields in late summer. Some of the largest specimens in North America are found in the rich soils of the Midwest and Great Plains fringe — specimens over a foot in diameter are not uncommon.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white. Once any yellow, purple, or green coloring develops inside, it is no longer safe to eat.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden — one of the best preparations. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which means it might be a deadly *Amanita* button), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating. Other edible puffballs include Gem-studded Puffball (*Lycoperdon perlatum*) — tiny, studded with white spines, edible when all-white inside.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Shaggy Mane
*Coprinus comatus*

![Giant Shaggy Mane (Coprinus comatus) — white cylindrical shaggy-scaled cap before ink stage](images/native-plants/coprinus-comatus.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall, cylindrical, white mushroom with a cap covered in shaggy scales that curve upward, resembling a lawyer's wig. The cap starts as a tight white cylinder 2–6 inches tall, then expands and auto-digests (deliquesces) from the bottom up into black inky liquid over several hours. Find them in the cylindrical white stage before any black ink appears.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed ground, lawns, roadsides, compost-rich soil, old gardens. Often grows in lines or arcs.

**Season**
Spring and fall, often appearing after rain.

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom in the white cylindrical stage only.

**Preparation**
Must be used immediately — the auto-digestion begins within hours of harvest. Sauté quickly in butter. The flavor is mild and excellent. Do not wait.

**Safety**
**Do not consume with alcohol** — Shaggy Mane (and related *Coprinus* species) contains coprine, which causes a disulfiram-like reaction when combined with alcohol (flushing, nausea, vomiting). Eat them without drinking alcohol within 24–48 hours. Ink-stage specimens are not eaten.

---

---

# REGION 4: GREAT PLAINS
*North Dakota, South Dakota, Nebraska, Kansas, Oklahoma, Texas (north)*

The Great Plains sustained enormous human populations for millennia on prairie turnip, chokecherries, buffalo berries, wild plums, and dozens of wild onion species. This is not a landscape of abundant forest mushrooms — it is a landscape of starchy roots, calorie-dense fruits, and prairie greens. The forager who dismisses the plains for lack of trees misses one of the continent's richest root-crop landscapes.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Prairie Turnip / Tipsin / Indian Breadroot
*Pediomelum esculentum*

![Prairie Turnip (Pediomelum esculentum) — palmate leaves with 5 leaflets and blue-purple flower spike](images/native-plants/pediomelum-esculentum.jpg)

**Identification**
A low prairie forb, 8–16 inches tall when flowering. Leaves are **palmate** — 5 leaflets radiating from a central point like fingers from a palm. Leaflets are elliptic and the entire plant is covered in fine hairs. Flowers are small and blue-purple, clustered on a dense spike. Underground: a single taproot swelling into an oval starchy tuber, usually 2–4 inches long with brown fibrous skin and white starchy interior. The plant collapses and the aboveground parts disperse after fruiting — locate in spring while flowering.

**Where to Find It**
Well-drained prairie soils, open grasslands, dry hillsides, dry meadows. Full sun. Mixed-grass and shortgrass prairie of the central Great Plains.

**Season**
Flowers May–June. Tubers best in fall (highest starch) or spring before flowering (easiest to locate).

**Edible Parts**
Starchy tuber — the primary calorie source. High in protein for a root vegetable.

**Preparation**
Peel the rough skin. Eat raw (starchy, slightly sweet), boiled, roasted, or dried. When dried and ground: a nutritious flour. One of the most important carbohydrate foods for Plains tribes for thousands of years.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive 5-leaflet palmate leaf pattern and prairie habitat. The palmate leaf is diagnostic.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Ground Plum / Milk Vetch
*Astragalus crassicarpus*

![Ground Plum (Astragalus crassicarpus) — plump oval green pods along low-growing stems](images/native-plants/astragalus-crassicarpus.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, sprawling prairie plant with compound leaves of 15–27 small oval leaflets. Purple to reddish-blue flowers in dense clusters in spring. The edible part is unmistakable: plump, succulent, oval pods (1–2 inches) growing along the stems, resembling small green plums. They grow directly on or near the ground.

**Where to Find It**
Dry prairies, roadsides, hillsides, rocky outcrops throughout the central and northern Great Plains.

**Season**
Pods ripen and are best in late April–June while still green and firm. Once they dry, they toughen significantly.

**Edible Parts**
Young green pods — eat raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Eat raw like peas — the firm, crunchy pod has a mild, slightly sweet flavor similar to raw snap peas. Steam or boil briefly. The seeds inside are edible. The flavor diminishes as the pods dry.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** Many *Astragalus* species are TOXIC (containing swainsonine or nitrotoxins). *A. crassicarpus* (Ground Plum) is an exception — but the identification must be confirmed. The distinctive plum-like fleshy green pod of *A. crassicarpus* is distinctive among Great Plains Astragalus species. Do not eat other *Astragalus* species without expert confirmation.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Plains Prickly Pear
*Opuntia polyacantha*

![Plains Prickly Pear (Opuntia polyacantha) — low-growing cactus with yellow flowers on prairie](images/native-plants/opuntia-polyacantha.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, spreading prickly pear cactus (less than 1 foot tall) with flat, oval pads covered in clusters of long spines and shorter glochids. Yellow flowers in early summer, small red to purplish-red fruit in late summer. More cold-hardy than southwestern species — common across the northern Great Plains.

**Where to Find It**
Dry prairie, rocky hillsides, sandy soil throughout the Great Plains from North Dakota south.

**Season**
Pads (nopales): spring. Fruit (tunas): late summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young pads (nopales, cooked), ripe fruit (fresh or processed).

**Preparation**
**GLOCHIDS FIRST.** The tiny hair-like barbed glochids in the areoles are far more dangerous than the large spines — they penetrate skin invisibly. Remove by: (1) burning over a gas flame until they char off, (2) scrubbing vigorously with a stiff brush, or (3) pressing with newspaper and peeling away. **Rubber gloves do not stop glochids — use leather.**

Pads: once glochids removed, peel the outer skin, slice the interior, boil in salted water 10 minutes to reduce mucilage, sauté with onion and garlic (nopalitos). Fruit: roll in dry grass to remove glochids, cut off ends, slit and peel back skin, eat the sweet-tart interior. Juice/syrup for drinks.

**Safety**
Glochids are the primary hazard — not the large spines. If glochids enter skin, use tweezers under magnification, or spread white glue over the area, let dry, and peel off. The pads and fruit are safe once glochids are removed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Onion / Wild Garlic
*Allium textile, A. stellatum, A. drummondii* and other plains species

![Wild Garlic (Allium vineale) — grass-like leaves and round flower head with tiny bulblets](images/native-plants/allium-vineale.jpg)

**Identification**
Multiple *Allium* species grow on the Great Plains — all are edible. They share the essential features: grass-like to flat basal leaves growing from a bulb, with a small umbel-shaped flower head (round cluster of small flowers or bulblets) on a leafless stalk. The diagnostic feature for the entire genus: **crush any leaf — if it smells like onion, garlic, or chives, it is edible.** If it does not smell like onion, do not eat it. Plains species tend to have narrow, grass-like leaves and grow in dry prairie soils rather than moist woodland.

**Where to Find It**
Dry prairies, rocky slopes, roadsides, meadows, and disturbed soil throughout the Great Plains. Common in all five Great Plains states — look in dry grassland, especially on rocky south-facing slopes in spring.

**Season**
Spring leaves and bulbs (April–June). Fall bulbs after tops die back.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: bulbs, leaves, stems, flowers. All edible.

**Preparation**
Leaves chopped fresh like chives. Bulbs cooked in any dish requiring onion or garlic. Pickled whole. Dried and stored. Bulblets planted like garlic cloves.

**Safety**
The smell test is the safety protocol — if it smells like onion, it is safe. No dangerous lookalike plant will smell like onion. The one exception worth knowing: **Death Camas (*Anticlea/Zigadenus* spp.)** — a toxic grass-like plant that grows in similar prairie habitat and looks superficially similar to wild onion but has NO onion smell whatsoever. Always perform the smell test.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. The diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder. Stems are grooved and often striped in red, green, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, roadsides, and compost piles throughout the Great Plains. One of the most abundant edible weeds in the region — found in virtually every disturbed area.

**Season**
Late spring through fall. Young leaves and shoot tips are best in early to midsummer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Add to soups, omelets, pasta. Nutritionally superior to spinach in protein, iron, and vitamin B2.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating, do not eat in very large quantities daily.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips. The entire plant has a slightly mucilaginous quality.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, dry, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, driveways, fields throughout the Great Plains. Thrives in the hot summer conditions of the plains — common in gardens and agricultural fields from North Dakota to Texas.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Peaks in hot weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (mucilaginous quality thickens dressings slightly). Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. Seeds ground into flour. Exceptional in omega-3 fatty acid content for a land plant.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — yellow flower, toothed leaves, and white seedhead](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with deeply toothed, lance-shaped basal leaves (the "lion's teeth" of the name). Single bright yellow composite flower heads on hollow stems that bleed white latex when broken. Transforms into the familiar white spherical seedhead. Taproot is substantial, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, roadsides, disturbed areas, meadows throughout the Great Plains. Universally present — one of the most abundant wild edibles in North America.

**Season**
Leaves: early spring through fall (best in spring before flowering, when least bitter). Flowers: spring. Roots: fall through early spring.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves (raw or cooked), flowers (raw or cooked), roots (cooked or roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Young spring leaves: raw in salads (bitter but nutritious). Blanch briefly to reduce bitterness. Flowers: battered and fried, made into jelly or wine. Roots: roast at 350°F until very dark, grind, brew as coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the combination of toothed basal leaves, hollow latex-bearing stem, and yellow composite flower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis stricta / O. violacea* and related species

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped clover-like leaflets and tiny yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Low plants with distinctive three-part leaves, each leaflet heart-shaped (resembling shamrocks). Yellow (or violet, depending on species) five-petaled flowers. Leaves fold down at night. The stems and leaves taste distinctly tart and lemony — the sour taste is immediate and diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, roadsides, woodland edges, disturbed ground throughout the Great Plains. Common as a lawn weed.

**Season**
Spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods (all tart and lemony).

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail nibble — the sour taste is pleasant in small quantities. Add to salads for tartness. Chew as a thirst quencher.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — do not eat in large quantities. Fine in small amounts as a flavoring or trail nibble.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems are hollow and float. The flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Clear springs, spring-fed streams, cold running water throughout the Great Plains. Found in spring-fed streams and cold clear waterways throughout the region — look in protected ravines, spring seeps, and cold creek headwaters.

**Season**
Best in cool months (spring and fall). Available year-round where springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia / T. angustifolia*

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — tall grass-like leaves and hot dog-shaped brown seed head](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall (4–9 feet), grass-like aquatic plant with long, flat, strap-like leaves and the unmistakable hot-dog-shaped brown seed head on a stiff stalk. Grows in dense stands in shallow water or wet soil. The large, sausage-shaped brown seed head is one of the most recognizable plant forms in North America.

**Where to Find It**
Ponds, marshes, stream banks, irrigation channels, and all wetlands throughout the Great Plains. Common in all freshwater wetlands — a cattail stand represents one of the most productive wild food patches in the landscape.

**Season**
Young shoots: early spring. Green pollen heads (male): late spring–early summer. Pollen: early summer. Young green female heads: late spring. Rhizomes: fall through winter.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (raw or cooked), green male pollen heads (cooked like corn), pollen (flour), green female seed heads before turning brown (cooked), starchy rhizomes (year-round).

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel outer leaves to the tender white core, eat raw or sauté. Pollen heads: boil before pollen is released, eat like corn on the cob with butter. Pollen: collect into a bag, use as a bright yellow flour supplement in pancakes and bread. Rhizomes: dig from mud, peel, pound to extract starch — a significant calorie source.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once the mature brown seed head is confirmed. Young shoots before the head develops — confirm habitat (standing water/mud) and that the inner white core smells like cattail.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated. Leaves have prominently toothed edges.

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, old fields, forest edges, and roadsides throughout the Great Plains. Common in prairie edges, woodland margins, and disturbed ground — particularly in the eastern Great Plains where moisture supports them.

**Season**
Flowers in May. Fruit ripens June–July. Leaves available spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea — high in vitamin C).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, baked goods, or dried. The flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*) has insipid but non-toxic berries — distinguished by yellow flowers (wild strawberry has white flowers).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Amaranth
*Amaranthus retroflexus, A. hybridus, A. tuberculatus* and related species

![Amaranth (Amaranthus retroflexus) — dense seed heads and large dark green leaves on tall stem](images/native-plants/amaranthus-retroflexus.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall, coarse, upright plants (1–6 feet) with large, alternate, oval leaves. Dense, bristly, club-shaped or branched seed heads at the top. The stem and leaf underside often have a reddish tinge. Young plants are soft and leafy; older plants become coarser. All North American *Amaranthus* species are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, roadsides, agricultural fields, and waste areas throughout the Great Plains. Extremely common and abundant — found in essentially every disturbed field and roadside from North Dakota to Texas.

**Season**
Young leaves: spring through summer. Seeds: late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots (cooked greens), seeds (grain).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: boil, steam, or sauté — excellent nutritious greens, mild and slightly spinach-like. Seeds: shake seed heads into a bag, winnow chaff, cook as porridge, grind into flour, or pop like popcorn. One of the ancient grains of the Americas — highly nutritious, high in protein and lysine.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid in the leaves — cook before eating. All *Amaranthus* species are edible; none are confused with toxic plants.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — serrated leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
An upright perennial (2–6 feet) with opposite, deeply toothed, heart-shaped leaves covered in hollow, silica-tipped stinging hairs that inject formic acid on contact. The sting is immediate and distinctive — burning and itching for minutes to hours. Stems are square. Small, inconspicuous greenish flowers in drooping clusters. The stinging sensation upon touching is the most reliable identification feature.

**Where to Find It**
Moist disturbed areas, stream banks, woodland edges, old barnyards, and nitrogen-rich soils throughout the Great Plains. Found along stream corridors and in moist low areas — less common than in the eastern states but present throughout.

**Season**
Young shoots and top leaves: early spring (best before flowering). Older plants become fibrous and should be avoided.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and leaves (cooked only). Cooking destroys the sting completely.

**Preparation**
Wear gloves to harvest. Blanch in boiling water 2–3 minutes (destroys the sting). Use like cooked spinach — in soups, omelets, pasta, risotto, pesto. Nutritionally exceptional: high in iron, calcium, vitamins A and C. Nettle tea: steep dried leaves in hot water.

**Safety**
**Always cook before eating** — raw nettles sting the mouth and throat. Use gloves when handling fresh plants. No toxic lookalikes once stinging hairs are confirmed.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chokecherry
*Prunus virginiana*

![Chokecherry (Prunus virginiana) — elongated fruit clusters (racemes) of dark berries](images/native-plants/prunus-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with alternate, oval, finely toothed leaves (1–4 inches). Bark grayish-red with small pores (lenticels). White flowers in elongated cylindrical clusters (racemes) — distinctly cylindrical, not flat-topped like elder. Berries ripen dark red to nearly black in late summer, in the same elongated cluster configuration.

**Where to Find It**
Thickets, stream banks, valley edges, rocky slopes, roadsides throughout the Great Plains. Extremely common and productive — a primary food plant of the Great Plains. Forms dense thickets along stream corridors and valley edges. One of the most important fruit-bearing shrubs in the otherwise tree-sparse plains landscape.

**Season**
Flowers late April–June. Berries ripen July–September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berry flesh and juice only. Do not chew seeds.

**Preparation**
Very astringent raw — the name "chokecherry" is accurate. Jelly (excellent — natural pectin). Cordial (simmer berries with water and sugar, strain). Syrup. Dried fruit leather (traditional Plains preparation by many tribal nations). **Do not chew pits** — cyanogenic.

**Safety**
Pits, leaves, and bark contain cyanogenic glycosides. Strain seeds from all processed products. The astringency of raw berries deters overconsumption.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Buffalo Berry / Soapberry
*Shepherdia argentea*

![Buffalo Berry (Shepherdia argentea) — silvery-scaled leaves and red-orange berries](images/native-plants/shepherdia-argentea.jpg)

**Identification**
A thorny shrub (4–10 feet) with **distinctive silver-gray color** — opposite leaves densely covered in silver-gray scales giving the plant a frosted, silvery appearance visible from a distance. Small yellowish flowers in early spring. Small red to orange-red berries (¼ inch) in clusters in late summer.

**Where to Find It**
River banks, thickets, dry rocky slopes, valley bottoms. Primarily the northern and central Great Plains.

**Season**
Berries ripen late July–September. Best after first frost, which reduces astringency.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries.

**Preparation**
Fresh (tart — better after frost). Traditional "Indian ice cream" / buffalo berry pudding: beat berries vigorously with a small amount of fat or water — saponins in the berries cause the mixture to foam into a frothy, mousse-like dessert. Jam. Cordial. High in vitamin C.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes — the silver leaf color is unmistakable. Do not eat the raw berries in very large quantities (saponins cause digestive upset in excess).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Plum
*Prunus americana*

![Wild Plum (Prunus americana) — red-orange ripe plums on thorny branches](images/native-plants/prunus-americana.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with thorny branches. Alternate, oval, sharply toothed leaves. Clusters of white five-petaled flowers in early spring before the leaves emerge. The fruit is a small plum (¾–1 inch diameter), ripening red to yellow, with a thin skin and juicy flesh enclosing a single pit.

**Where to Find It**
Thickets, roadsides, stream banks, and valley bottoms throughout the Great Plains. Very common — forms dense thickets along stream corridors. A critical fruit source in this otherwise tree-sparse landscape, particularly important in the riparian zones of the Platte, Republican, and other Great Plains rivers.

**Season**
Flowers: early spring. Fruit ripens July–August. Pick when fully colored and soft to the touch.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not crack or chew pits.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw (tart but good). Jelly (excellent — high natural pectin). Jam. Butter (cooked down with spices). Wine. Dried. The tartness diminishes significantly with cooking.

**Safety**
Like all *Prunus* species, the pits (and bark and leaves) contain cyanogenic glycosides — do not chew pits, strain seeds from juices. The ripe flesh is safe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Serviceberry / Saskatoon
*Amelanchier alnifolia* (Saskatoon Serviceberry)

![Serviceberry (Amelanchier) — dark purple berries and finely toothed oval leaves](images/native-plants/amelanchier.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub to small tree (3–15 feet) with oval, finely serrated alternate leaves. One of the first shrubs to flower in spring — white star-shaped five-petaled flowers appear before or with the first leaves. Fruit ripens in early summer, turning from red to dark purple when fully ripe. *A. alnifolia* (Saskatoon) is the prairie-adapted species — slightly rounder leaves, very productive, sweeter than eastern species.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, stream banks, coulees, hillsides, and open prairie throughout the northern Great Plains. The Saskatoon serviceberry is one of the most important wild food plants of the northern Great Plains — highly productive and sweet. Common in the Dakotas, Nebraska, Kansas, and throughout the Canadian prairies. Revered by many Plains tribal nations and an important pemmican ingredient.

**Season**
Fruit ripe June–July, window of 2–3 weeks. Fully purple berries are sweet; red berries still tart.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent, sweeter than eastern serviceberries. Dried like raisins (traditional — dried saskatoons are a pemmican ingredient). Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe purple serviceberries with their characteristic fine-toothed oval leaves. Confirm the five-petaled flower or the characteristic berry with a tiny five-pointed crown at the blossom end.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus canadensis*

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark purple-to-black spheres in heavy drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Stream corridors, valley bottoms, irrigation channels, moist thickets throughout the Great Plains. Found wherever moisture concentrates in the plains landscape — look in draw bottoms, along creeks, and in riparian zones. Less abundant than in the east but highly productive where it occurs.

**Season**
Flowers: late May through July. Berries: late August through October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems, steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine, pie.

**Safety**
**LOOKALIKE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* has red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster — berries are toxic raw. **Flat-topped cluster + purple-black berries = edible when cooked. Red berries in domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Also distinguish from Poison Hemlock (*Conium*) — elder has pithy hollow stems; hemlock has spotted hollow stems.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hackberry
*Celtis occidentalis* and related species

![Hackberry (Celtis occidentalis) — warty corky bark and small orange-purple berries](images/native-plants/celtis-occidentalis.jpg)

**Identification**
A medium to large tree (30–80 feet) with distinctive **warty, corky ridges and knobs** on the gray bark — one of the most recognizable bark textures of any eastern tree. Leaves are alternate, oval to lance-shaped, with an asymmetrical base (one side of the leaf joins the stem slightly lower than the other). Small orange to dark purple berries (¼ inch) in fall, each on a short stem.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, valley bottoms, woodland edges, roadsides throughout the Great Plains and Midwest.

**Season**
Berries ripen September–October. Persist on the tree through winter (good winter emergency food).

**Edible Parts**
Berries — the thin sweet flesh over a large seed. Seeds are edible (nutritious but hard).

**Preparation**
Eat fresh — the sweet, date-like flesh is thin but good. Dry and grind entire berries (including seeds) into a nutritious meal — traditional preparation by Plains peoples who ground dried hackberries into pemmican and cornmeal preparations.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive corky bark and small orange-purple berries on individual stems.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Smooth Sumac / Staghorn Sumac
*Rhus glabra* (Smooth Sumac) and *R. typhina* (Staghorn Sumac)

![Staghorn Sumac (Rhus typhina) — compound leaves and upright red berry clusters](images/native-plants/rhus-typhina.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs or small trees (6–20 feet) with large, pinnately compound leaves and upright, cone-shaped clusters of tiny red berries at branch tips. *R. glabra* (Smooth Sumac) is the plains-adapted version — stems are smooth and hairless. *R. typhina* has velvety hairy branches. Both produce the same red, upright, hairy berry clusters. Berries covered in short red hairs that carry the acidic flavor compound.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, old fields, forest edges, and dry rocky slopes throughout the Great Plains. Smooth Sumac is particularly well-adapted to the plains — common in thickets at the edges of stream corridors and on dry hillsides throughout the region.

**Season**
Berries ripen red in July–August and persist through winter. Best harvested before heavy rains wash away the acidic coating.

**Edible Parts**
The red berry clusters. The acidic coating on the berries is the edible/useful part.

**Preparation**
Sumac-ade: rub the berry cluster vigorously in cool water (NOT hot — hot water releases bitter tannins), strain through a cloth to remove hairs, sweeten to taste. The resulting pink drink tastes like lemonade. Dried, ground berries make a tart, lemony spice (the same as the *sumac* used in Middle Eastern cooking).

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** *Poison Sumac (Toxicodendron vernix)* — CAUSES SEVERE ALLERGIC REACTION (worse than poison ivy) — grows in WET habitats (swamps, marshes), has **white to greenish-white** berry clusters that hang **downward** from branches. Staghorn/Smooth Sumac: red upright clusters, dry habitat. The habitat and berry color/orientation are foolproof distinguishers. Poison Sumac is rare on the Great Plains (too dry).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Rose / Prairie Rose
*Rosa arkansana* and related species

![Wild Rose (Rosa carolina) — pink five-petaled flowers and oval leaflets](images/native-plants/rosa-carolina.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs with thorny or prickly canes, alternate compound leaves with 5–9 oval toothed leaflets, and classic five-petaled pink single rose flowers. *R. arkansana* (Prairie Rose) is the dominant species of the Great Plains — low-growing (1–3 feet), with bright pink flowers and small rose hips. The red to orange-red fruit (rose hip) develops in late summer and persists through winter.

**Where to Find It**
Open prairies, roadsides, meadow edges, and stream banks throughout the Great Plains. Prairie Rose is the dominant wild rose of the Great Plains — low-growing and abundant in prairie settings. Rose hips persist through winter and are an important emergency vitamin C source on the treeless plains.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Rose hips: August through winter (best after first frost sweetens them).

**Edible Parts**
Petals (fresh), rose hips (processed), and young shoots in spring (peeled).

**Preparation**
Petals: fresh in salads, infused in syrups, dried for tea. Rose hips: cut off blossom end and stem, slice in half, scrape out seeds and fine hairs (both irritating), use flesh. Rose hip tea: dry hips and steep in hot water — extremely high in vitamin C. Rose hip jam, syrup, cordial.

**Safety**
Thorns are a physical hazard. The seeds and fine hairs inside the hip are irritating — always remove before consuming. No toxic lookalikes for the classic rose flower-and-hip combination.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hawthorn
*Crataegus* spp. (*C. mollis*, *C. crus-galli*, *C. succulenta* and others)

![Hawthorn (Crataegus) — small red berries and thorny branches with lobed leaves](images/native-plants/crataegus.jpg)

**Identification**
Small thorny trees or shrubs (10–30 feet) with alternate, often deeply lobed leaves, stiff thorns (1–3 inches), white five-petaled flowers in spring, and small apple-like fruit (haws) ¼–1 inch diameter, red to orange to dark purple when ripe. Multiple hawthorn species grow in stream corridors throughout the Great Plains. All are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Stream corridors, valley bottoms, thickets, and woodland edges throughout the Great Plains. Less common than on the eastern forest fringe but present in all riparian habitats — look in draw bottoms and along rivers and creeks.

**Season**
Fruit (haws) ripen August–October. Best after frost.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not chew seeds — they contain cyanogenic compounds.

**Preparation**
Jelly and jam (high pectin — an excellent jelling agent). Fruit butter. Dried haws as a tart fruit snack. The flavor ranges from bland to pleasantly tart depending on species and ripeness.

**Safety**
Do not crack or chew seeds. All North American hawthorn species have edible fruit. No toxic lookalikes among small thorny trees with the characteristic apple-like haws.

---

## FUNGI

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
The Great Plains grasslands and meadows are among the most productive habitats for giant puffballs in North America. Old fields, meadow edges, disturbed ground, and prairie grass throughout the region. Specimens can reach 2 feet in diameter in rich prairie soils — some of the largest individuals in North America are found on the Great Plains.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white. Once any yellow, purple, or green coloring develops inside, it is no longer safe to eat.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden — one of the best preparations. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which means it might be a deadly *Amanita* button), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Meadow Mushroom
*Agaricus campestris*

*[Photo needed: Agaricus campestris — white-capped mushroom in lawn/meadow with pink gills. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A classic white-capped mushroom of lawns and meadows: 2–4 inch smooth white cap, white to pale gills turning pink then dark brown as spores mature, ring on the stem (partial veil remnant), no volva (cup) at the base. **Key:** cut the cap or base — flesh turns **slightly pink** or does not change color (not yellow). Smell is pleasantly mushroom-like, not phenolic or like chemicals.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, pastures, and grassland throughout the Great Plains. Common in pastured grassland and lawn turf. Very common in the fertile soils of the Great Plains.

**Season**
Late summer through fall. Most common after rains in August–October.

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté, add to pasta, pizza, soups. The cultivated white button mushroom is a close relative.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: Destroying Angel / Death Cap (*Amanita* species)** — DEADLY — have a cup (volva) at the base of the stem, and the flesh does NOT turn pink when cut. Always dig up the entire mushroom to check for the volva. **Also:** *Agaricus xanthodermus* — TOXIC — similar but flesh turns **bright yellow** when cut at the base and smells phenolic (like carbolic acid). **Rule: if it smells like chemicals or turns yellow when cut at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel
*Morchella* spp.

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — cut vertically to confirm one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb**. The cap attaches to the stem at its base. Cap color: tan to gray to near-black depending on species.

**Where to Find It**
Riparian corridors, orchards, stream-bank woodlands, and areas with dying elms throughout the Great Plains. Less dense than in the Midwest but still productive in stream-bank woodlands in spring. Look in cottonwood and willow corridors along rivers and in old orchards.

**Season**
Spring (April–May in the Great Plains). Soil temperature around 50°F triggers emergence.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked.** Sauté in butter over medium-high heat 8–12 minutes until golden. Slice vertically and rinse to remove soil and insects from the hollow cavity.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* species) — POISONOUS, potentially FATAL. False morels: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap is wrinkled or brain-like (not honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. True gills run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, stubby, off-center or absent. Found in cool weather on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead cottonwood, willow, elm, and other stream-corridor hardwoods throughout the Great Plains. The cottonwood-lined rivers of the Great Plains (Platte, Republican, Arkansas, Red) are productive oyster mushroom habitat. Look on standing dead cottonwoods after fall rains.

**Season**
Spring and fall, especially when temperatures drop.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well.

**Safety**
Avoid specimens that are old, soft, or smell sour. Angel wings (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) grows on conifer wood — avoid. Oyster mushrooms on hardwoods are generally reliable.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Shaggy Mane
*Coprinus comatus*

![Giant Shaggy Mane (Coprinus comatus) — white cylindrical shaggy-scaled cap before ink stage](images/native-plants/coprinus-comatus.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall, cylindrical, white mushroom with a cap covered in shaggy scales that curve upward, resembling a lawyer's wig. The cap starts as a tight white cylinder 2–6 inches tall, then expands and auto-digests (deliquesces) from the bottom up into black inky liquid over several hours. Find them in the cylindrical white stage before any black ink appears.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed ground, lawns, roadsides, compost-rich soil, and old gardens throughout the Great Plains. Common in disturbed ground and lawn turf across the entire region, often appearing after summer and fall rains.

**Season**
Spring and fall, often appearing after rain.

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom in the white cylindrical stage only.

**Preparation**
Must be used immediately — auto-digestion begins within hours of harvest. Sauté quickly in butter. The flavor is mild and excellent. Do not wait.

**Safety**
**Do not consume with alcohol** — contains coprine, which causes a disulfiram-like reaction when combined with alcohol (flushing, nausea, vomiting). Avoid alcohol within 24–48 hours of eating. Ink-stage specimens are not eaten.

---

---

# REGION 5: SOUTHWEST AND DESERT
*New Mexico, Arizona, Nevada, Utah (desert), Texas (west and south)*

The desert rewards the prepared forager and punishes the careless. Water is life, and desert plants have evolved extraordinary nutrient density. Prickly pear has sustained desert peoples for thousands of years. Mesquite flour predates corn in many cultures. The monsoon season (July–September) brings a brief, explosive flush of mushrooms and annuals after the rains.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Prickly Pear Cactus
*Opuntia* spp. (*O. engelmannii*, *O. phaeacantha*, *O. ficus-indica*)

![Prickly Pear (Opuntia ficus-indica) — ripe red-purple fruit and flat green pads](images/native-plants/opuntia-ficus-indica.jpg)

**Identification**
Flat, fleshy, paddle-shaped pads (cladophylls), 4–16 inches long, green to blue-green, covered in clusters of spines up to 3 inches long and tiny hair-like glochids in the areoles. Showy yellow to orange flowers in late spring–early summer. Fruit (tuna): oval, 1–3 inches, red to deep purple when ripe.

**Where to Find It**
Desert scrub, arid grasslands, rocky slopes, dry sandy soils. Full sun. Very common throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Young pads (nopales): spring (smaller, more tender, fewer developed spines). Fruit: late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young pads (cooked), ripe fruit (fresh or processed).

**Preparation**
**GLOCHIDS FIRST.** The tiny hair-like barbed glochids in the areoles are far more dangerous than the large spines — they penetrate skin invisibly. Remove by: (1) burning over a gas flame until they char off, (2) scrubbing with a stiff brush, or (3) pressing with newspaper and peeling away. **Rubber gloves do not stop glochids — use leather.**

Pads: once glochids removed, peel the outer skin, slice the interior, boil in salted water 10 minutes to reduce mucilage, sauté with onion, garlic, tomatoes (nopalitos). Fruit: roll on rough ground to remove glochids, cut off ends, slit and peel back skin, eat sweet-tart interior. Juice/syrup for drinks.

**Safety**
Glochids are the primary hazard — not the large spines. If glochids enter skin, use tweezers under magnification, or spread white glue over the area, let dry, and peel off.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mesquite
*Prosopis glandulosa* (Honey Mesquite) and *P. velutina* (Velvet Mesquite)

![Honey Mesquite (Prosopis glandulosa) — feathery compound leaves and golden-brown seed pods](images/native-plants/prosopis-glandulosa.jpg)

**Identification**
Thorny shrub or tree (up to 30 feet) with graceful branches and fine, twice-compound leaves giving a cloud-like, feathery texture. Paired straight thorns on branches. Seed pods: long (4–10 inches), flattened, papery tan to golden-brown, constricted between each seed, dangling in clusters. Ripe dry pods rattle with hard seeds inside.

**Where to Find It**
Desert scrubland, washes, roadsides, dry riverbeds, disturbed desert. Very common across the Sonoran and Chihuahuan Desert regions.

**Season**
Pods ripen and dry late summer (July–September). Harvest when dry and golden-brown — green pods are not ready.

**Edible Parts**
Dry ripe pods — the outer pod wall (sweet, edible) and the ground meal from the entire pod.

**Preparation**
Gather dry fallen pods. Grind the entire pod in a hand mill or blender into a coarse meal. Sift out large seed fragments. The resulting flour is sweet, high in protein and minerals, and has a low glycemic index. Use in bread, pancakes, tortillas (substitute 25–30% for wheat flour), porridge. The sweet, caramel-like flavor pairs well with meat rubs.

Store dried pods for months; ground flour for weeks without refrigeration.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. Ensure pods are fully dry and ripe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Yucca
*Yucca baccata* (Banana Yucca), *Y. glauca*, *Y. elata*

![Banana Yucca (Yucca baccata) — tall flower stalk with creamy bell-shaped flowers from sword-leaf rosette](images/native-plants/yucca-baccata.jpg)

**Identification**
Basal rosette of long, stiff, sword-shaped leaves with very sharp spine-tipped ends. Leaf margins often have curling thread-like fibers. A tall flower stalk (3–8 feet) rises in spring bearing clusters of creamy white, waxy, bell-shaped flowers. *Y. baccata* (Banana Yucca) produces large, fleshy, banana-shaped fruit pods — the primary food-use species in the Southwest.

**Where to Find It**
Desert and semi-arid areas, rocky slopes, mesa tops, dry grasslands throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Flowers: April–June. *Y. baccata* fruit: late summer–fall.

**Edible Parts**
Petals (edible, mildly bitter), *Y. baccata* fruit (ripe), flower stalk (young, before flowering).

**Preparation**
Petals: pluck individual flowers, remove the bitter inner reproductive parts, boil or sauté. Add to soups. Dry and grind. *Y. baccata* fruit: roast whole in coals (traditional) or bake — the flesh becomes sweeter with cooking. The seeds are edible but bland. Young flower stalks before blooming: peel, boil, eat like asparagus.

**Safety**
The sharp leaf tips are a genuine physical hazard — approach with care, protect your eyes. The saponin-rich roots are not food (used as soap).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Desert Chia
*Salvia columbariae*

![Desert Chia (Salvia columbariae) — whorls of blue flowers and seed heads on slender stems](images/native-plants/salvia-columbariae.jpg)

**Identification**
A small annual (4–24 inches) with deeply lobed, wrinkled, hairy basal leaves. The stem bears whorls of tiny blue to purple flowers at intervals, surrounded by spiny bracts. After flowering, the seed heads at each whorl contain tiny oval seeds — gray-brown, mucilaginous when wet. Same genus as commercial chia (*S. hispanica*) but smaller.

**Where to Find It**
Sandy desert washes, open desert, chaparral edges, dry open areas. Southern California and Arizona primarily.

**Season**
Flowers: March–May. Seeds mature: late spring–early summer before plant dies.

**Edible Parts**
Seeds (primary food). Very high in omega-3 fatty acids, protein, and fiber.

**Preparation**
Gather dry seed heads, thresh to release seeds. Eat raw (soak in water to form gel — chia fresca). Add to water as a thickened energy drink. Grind into flour. Sprinkle on food.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in the same desert habitat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Saguaro Cactus
*Carnegiea gigantea*

![Saguaro (Carnegiea gigantea) — tall columnar cactus with red fruit at column tops](images/native-plants/carnegiea-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
The iconic Arizona columnar cactus — unmistakable. Tall (up to 50 feet), single or branched columns covered in prominent ribs armed with clusters of long spines. White flowers at the top of columns in late spring. Red fruit (saguaro fruit): oval, 3 inches, splitting open when ripe to reveal bright red flesh studded with tiny black seeds. The fruit split is the harvest signal.

**Where to Find It**
Sonoran Desert — the Tucson basin, southern Arizona, and a small strip of California. Not found outside this range.

**Season**
Fruit ripens June–July. Traditional harvest using a long pole (kuibit) to knock fruit down.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit — the red flesh and the small seeds. The opened shell can be pressed into a paste.

**Preparation**
Eat fresh directly from the split fruit. Boil the flesh to make syrup (traditional O'odham preparation). Ferment into a traditional ceremonial wine. Dry and press the pulp into cakes for storage. Seeds ground as flour. Extremely sweet and nutritious.

**Safety**
The saguaro is a protected species in Arizona — harvesting is only legal on tribal lands and with permission. Check local regulations. No toxic lookalikes within the saguaro's range.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wolfberry / Desert Tomato
*Lycium pallidum* (Pale Wolfberry) and related species

![Wolfberry (Lycium pallidum) — small red berries and gray-green thorny branches](images/native-plants/lycium-pallidum.jpg)

**Identification**
Thorny shrubs (2–6 feet) with small, succulent, gray-green leaves and small lavender to white tubular flowers. The berries are small (¼–½ inch), round, ripening red to orange. Related to goji berry (same genus). The berry shape, color, and desert habitat are diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Desert flats, washes, disturbed desert areas, canyon bottoms. Throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Berries ripen spring through early summer (earlier than most desert fruits), and again in fall after monsoon rains in some species.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe red to orange berries.

**Preparation**
Fresh eating (mild, slightly sweet-tart). Dry for storage (raisin-like). Cook in soups or as a sauce base.

**Safety**
Distinguish wolfberry from other small red-berried desert shrubs. The combination of thorns, tubular flowers, and small round red berries is diagnostic for *Lycium*. All *Lycium* species in the Southwest are considered edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fourwing Saltbush
*Atriplex canescens*

![Fourwing Saltbush (Atriplex canescens) — gray-green shrub with distinctive four-winged seed clusters](images/native-plants/atriplex-canescens.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, gray-green shrub (2–6 feet) with narrowly oblong, grayish-scaly leaves (similar surface texture to lamb's quarters — same family). The distinctive feature is the fruit: four-winged, papery seed structures in clusters that hang from the branches in summer and fall. Used as a landmark plant throughout the Southwest.

**Where to Find It**
Desert flats, sandy washes, alkaline soil, rangelands. Very common throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Seeds: summer–fall. Young leaves: spring.

**Edible Parts**
Seeds (ground as flour); young tender leaves (cooked as greens).

**Preparation**
Seeds: gather ripe seed clusters, thresh to separate seeds from wings, grind into a nutritious meal (high in protein, calcium). Mix into bread or mush. Young leaves: boil as greens (salty flavor — the plant concentrates salt). No added salt needed.

**Safety**
The salty leaf flavor may be overwhelming in large quantities. Mild caution for high salt intake. No toxic lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Amaranth
*Amaranthus retroflexus, A. hybridus, A. palmeri* and related species

![Amaranth (Amaranthus retroflexus) — dense seed heads and large dark green leaves on tall stem](images/native-plants/amaranthus-retroflexus.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall, coarse, upright plants (1–6 feet) with large, alternate, oval leaves. Dense, bristly, club-shaped or branched seed heads at the top. The stem and leaf underside often have a reddish tinge. Young plants are soft and leafy; older plants become coarser. All North American *Amaranthus* species are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, roadsides, agricultural fields, washes, desert edges throughout the Southwest. Very common and abundant.

**Season**
Young leaves: spring through summer. Seeds: late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots (cooked greens), seeds (grain).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: boil, steam, or sauté — excellent nutritious greens (mild, slightly spinach-like). Seeds: shake seed heads into a bag, winnow chaff, cook as a grain (porridge), grind into flour, or pop like popcorn. One of the ancient grains of the Americas — highly nutritious, high in protein and lysine.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid in the leaves — cook before eating. The edible *Amaranthus* species are not confused with any toxic plants; all *Amaranthus* are edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hackberry
*Celtis reticulata* (Netleaf Hackberry) and *C. pallida* (Desert Hackberry)

![Netleaf Hackberry (Celtis reticulata) — netted leaf veins and small dark red berries](images/native-plants/celtis-reticulata.jpg)

**Identification**
Small trees or shrubs with asymmetrical, oval leaves with prominent netted venation on the rough upper surface. Distinctive corky, warty bark on larger specimens (same as eastern hackberry). Small, dark purple berries (¼ inch) on individual stems in fall.

**Where to Find It**
Desert washes, canyon walls, rocky slopes, stream corridors throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Berries ripen September–November. Persist through winter.

**Edible Parts / Preparation:** Same as Great Plains Hackberry entry. Thin sweet flesh over large seed; grind dried berries whole for nutritious meal.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Desert Marigold
*Baileya multiradiata*

![Desert Marigold (Baileya multiradiata) — bright yellow daisy-like flowers on woolly gray-green stems in desert](images/native-plants/baileya-multiradiata.jpg)

**Identification**
A showy perennial wildflower with woolly, gray-green, deeply lobed basal leaves and bright yellow, daisy-like flower heads (1–2 inches across) on slender, woolly stems (6–18 inches). One of the most conspicuous and abundant wildflowers of the Sonoran and Chihuahuan deserts — in good years, it colors entire roadsides and desert flats brilliant yellow. Blooms nearly year-round at lower elevations, peaking spring and fall.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, desert flats, gravelly desert, bajadas, and disturbed open desert throughout Arizona, New Mexico, western Texas, and adjacent Nevada. Very common and often abundant.

**NOT FOR INTERNAL CONSUMPTION.**

**Safety**
**DO NOT EAT — NOT AN EDIBLE PLANT.** Desert Marigold is included here because of its extreme visibility and abundance — foragers frequently encounter it and may assume it is edible. It is not. *Baileya multiradiata* contains sesquiterpene lactones and other compounds that cause toxicity in livestock (sheep and cattle grazing it develop neurological symptoms). Human toxicity from ingestion has not been well-characterized but internal consumption is not recommended. **This plant should not be eaten as food.** It is used in traditional medicine (external preparations) but never as a food source. Do not confuse the caution here with edibility — the word "marigold" in common names is not predictive of edibility. Note this clearly if sharing plant information with others.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Manzanita
*Arctostaphylos pungens* (Pointleaf Manzanita) and related species

![Manzanita (Arctostaphylos pungens) — smooth red-brown bark and small round red-brown berries](images/native-plants/arctostaphylos-pungens.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs (2–8 feet) with distinctive smooth, mahogany-red bark — one of the most beautiful barks in the Southwest. Stiff, oval, often glossy leaves. Pink urn-shaped flowers in winter–spring. Small round berries (¼–½ inch) resembling tiny apples — mealy, reddish-brown when ripe. The name "manzanita" means "little apple" in Spanish.

**Where to Find It**
Dry rocky slopes, chaparral, mountain foothills (3,000–8,000 feet elevation). Throughout the Southwest.

**Season**
Berries ripen June–August at appropriate elevations.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries — the mealy flesh and seeds.

**Preparation**
Eat fresh (mealy, slightly sweet-tart). Grind dried berries into a meal or flour. Traditional preparation: soak ground berries in water, strain, drink as a cider-like beverage. The berries are too mealy to be enjoyable raw in quantity; processing into drinks or flour is the traditional approach.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive smooth red bark. Other *Arctostaphylos* species have the same edible berries.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus mexicana* (Mexican Elder) and *S. nigra* (Blue/Black Elder)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub or small tree with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with toothed edges). Large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers in spring. Berries in late summer — tiny dark blue-to-black spheres in heavy drooping clusters. *S. mexicana* is common in Arizona, New Mexico, and Texas; *S. nigra* (Blue Elder) dominates in California and the Pacific Southwest.

**Where to Find It**
Riparian areas, canyon bottoms, stream banks, and moist mountain slopes throughout the Southwest. Grows wherever water is present — a reliable indicator of water in dry landscapes.

**Season**
Flowers: spring (March–May at low elevations, later at higher elevations). Berries: late summer–fall (July–September).

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems, steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine.

**Safety**
**LOOKALIKE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* has red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster — toxic raw. **Flat-topped cluster + dark blue/black berries = edible when cooked. Red berries in domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Do not eat leaves, stems, or unripe berries of any elderberry species.

---

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pinyon Pine
*Pinus edulis* (Two-Needle Pinyon) and *P. monophylla* (Single-Leaf Pinyon)

![Pinyon Pine (Pinus edulis) — short needles in pairs and plump resin-sticky cones with large edible seeds](images/native-plants/pinus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
A small, rounded pine tree (10–35 feet) with short needles in bundles of two (*P. edulis*) or singly (*P. monophylla*). The cones are small (1–2 inches), rounded, and resinous. The seeds (pinyon nuts) are large relative to the cone — about ½ inch — with a thin papery wing or almost wingless. Bark is gray-brown, scaly. Grows in the distinctive pinyon-juniper woodland zone of the Southwest.

**Where to Find It**
Dry rocky foothills, mesas, and plateaus at 4,500–7,500 feet elevation. Widespread throughout New Mexico, Arizona, Utah, Nevada, and Colorado in characteristic pinyon-juniper woodland. Often grows in mixed stands with one-seed juniper (*Juniperus monosperma*).

**Season**
Cones ripen late summer through fall (August–October). Harvest when cones are green and opening or just fallen. Green cones can be roasted to open; fully ripe cones can be shaken out.

**Edible Parts**
Seeds (pinyon nuts) — one of the highest-calorie wild foods in the Southwest.

**Preparation**
Harvest cones before squirrels and jays claim them — competition is fierce. Green cones: roast in coals or oven at 300°F until they open. Shake out seeds. Shell by cracking between rocks or with a nutcracker. Eat raw (sweet, rich, buttery) or toast lightly. Grind into a rich paste (pinyon butter). Add to stews, breads, and piñole (traditional cornmeal drink). Dried shelled nuts store well for months in a cool dry place.

**Safety**
