No dangerous lookalikes. All true pines produce edible seeds, though not all are large enough to harvest practically. The resin on hands and tools can be removed with vegetable oil.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Juniper Berries
*Juniperus monosperma* (One-Seed Juniper), *J. osteosperma* (Utah Juniper), *J. deppeana* (Alligator Juniper)

![One-Seed Juniper (Juniperus monosperma) — scale-like foliage and blue-purple berry-like cones](images/native-plants/juniperus-monosperma.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs to small trees (6–20 feet) with scale-like or awl-like overlapping foliage (not needle-like — scale leaves pressed flat against the twig). The "berries" are technically fleshy seed cones — round to oval, blue-gray to reddish-brown, coated in a whitish waxy bloom, usually containing 1–2 seeds. The foliage of edible Southwest junipers has a resinous, piney-gin aroma when crushed. *J. deppeana* (Alligator Juniper) has distinctive checkered bark resembling alligator hide.

**Where to Find It**
Pinyon-juniper woodland, dry mesas, rocky slopes throughout the Southwest at 3,500–7,000 feet. One of the most abundant trees in the Southwest — the juniper-pinyon zone covers millions of acres.

**Season**
Berries ripen late summer to fall (August–November) and often persist through winter. Blue-gray fully ripe berries are best. Red-brown berries of some species are also ripe.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berry-like cones (the fleshy covering and seeds). Used primarily as a spice and for tea.

**Preparation**
Use sparingly — the flavor is intensely resinous and gin-like. Dry ripe berries and use as a seasoning (crush 2–4 per dish) in game meats, stews, and wild teas. Tea: steep 4–6 dried berries in boiling water for 10 minutes, add honey. The berries can be ground and added in small amounts to cornmeal or grain dishes. Traditional O'odham and Navajo use includes grinding dried berries as a flavoring.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: Savin Juniper (*Juniperus sabina*) is TOXIC.** Savin Juniper is primarily a European species and rare in wild US settings, but is widely planted as an ornamental in yards and parks throughout the Southwest. Its foliage has a fetid, unpleasant odor when crushed (vs. pleasant piney-resinous smell of edible junipers). Never harvest juniper berries from ornamental plantings — identify in the wild pinyon-juniper woodland only. Do not consume large quantities — the volatile oils are kidney-irritating in excess. Use as a spice in moderation, not a bulk food.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Tepary Bean
*Phaseolus acutifolius* var. *latifolius*

![Tepary Bean (Phaseolus acutifolius) — twining vine with small white flowers and small flattened pods](images/native-plants/phaseolus-acutifolius.jpg)

**Identification**
A slender, twining annual vine with lance-shaped to oval leaflets (three per leaf, typical bean family). Small white to pale lavender pea-like flowers. Pods are short (1.5–2.5 inches), flat, slightly curved, and contain 3–6 small, flattened seeds varying from white to tan to speckled or dark. Much smaller and more drought-adapted than common bean (*P. vulgaris*). Often found climbing through desert shrubs.

**Where to Find It**
Desert washes, canyon bottoms, bajadas, and areas that receive monsoon water. Southern Arizona, New Mexico, and south Texas. Also persists near former agricultural sites and Native American farming areas (was a major crop of the O'odham people and is still cultivated).

**Season**
Flowers during and after monsoon rains (July–September). Pods ripen and dry late summer through fall. Harvest dry pods before they shatter.

**Edible Parts**
Mature dry seeds (primary use); green immature pods (edible but small).

**Preparation**
Dry beans require long cooking — soak overnight, then simmer 1.5–2 hours until tender. One of the most drought-tolerant and heat-tolerant of all beans; highly nutritious with more protein per calorie than common beans. Use in soups, stews, and traditional desert bean dishes. Green pods can be cooked like snap beans but the primary use is the dry seed.

**Safety**
Like all raw beans, raw tepary beans contain lectins — always cook thoroughly. No toxic lookalikes in the Southwest — no other twining legume vine in the desert wash habitat produces small flattened pods at this scale.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mormon Tea
*Ephedra viridis* (Green Ephedra), *E. trifurca* (Longleaf Ephedra), *E. nevadensis* (Nevada Ephedra)

![Mormon Tea (Ephedra viridis) — bright green jointed leafless stems in dense clumps](images/native-plants/ephedra-viridis.jpg)

**Identification**
Distinctive shrubs (1–4 feet) consisting almost entirely of bright green, jointed, segmented stems — the leaves are reduced to tiny dry scales at each joint. The overall appearance is like a bundle of green drinking straws or a photosynthetic horsetail. No true leaves visible to the naked eye. Tiny seed cones appear at joints in spring. The plant is immediately distinctive — nothing else in the Southwest looks quite like it.

**Where to Find It**
Dry rocky slopes, desert flats, mesas, bajadas, and open desert throughout the Southwest. Very common and abundant in the Sonoran, Mojave, and Chihuahuan deserts.

**Season**
Stems harvested year-round. Youngest growth (brighter green, more tender) is best for tea.

**Edible Parts**
Green stems — steeped as tea. Not eaten directly.

**Preparation**
Harvest a handful of green stems. Rinse. Simmer in water for 15–20 minutes (longer = stronger, more bitter). Strain and drink. The flavor is mildly astringent, slightly piney, earthy. Sweeten with honey if desired. Traditional Mormon Tea was made this way — the name comes from early Mormon settlers who used it as a coffee substitute.

**Safety**
North American *Ephedra* species contain small amounts of ephedrine alkaloids (far less than Asian *E. sinica*, the source of commercial ephedra supplements). Drinking 1–2 cups of tea occasionally poses no significant risk for most people. Do not consume in large quantities, do not drink daily long-term, and avoid entirely if you have heart conditions, high blood pressure, or are pregnant. The alkaloid content varies by species — *E. viridis* and *E. nevadensis* have lower alkaloid content than *E. trifurca*. No toxic lookalikes — the jointed, leafless green stem is unmistakable.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Canyon Grape / Desert Wild Grape
*Vitis arizonica* (Canyon Grape)

![Canyon Grape (Vitis arizonica) — lobed leaves and clusters of small dark blue-purple grapes](images/native-plants/vitis-arizonica.jpg)

**Identification**
A vigorous woody vine climbing into riparian shrubs and trees along canyon walls and stream corridors. Leaves are heart-shaped, 3–5 lobed, with toothed margins and a woolly to hairy underside. Tendrils opposite the leaves (key grape feature). Fruit in loose clusters of small round berries (¼–½ inch), dark blue to purple-black when ripe, with a whitish bloom. The tendrils, opposite-leaf climbing habit, and grape-like berry clusters are diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Desert canyon bottoms, riparian areas, stream banks, and washes with access to permanent or seasonal water. Southern Arizona, New Mexico, west Texas, and Nevada. Often drapes over cottonwood and willow trees.

**Season**
Berries ripen late summer through fall (August–October). Leaves edible in spring (young, tender).

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries (fresh or cooked); young spring leaves (stuffed grape leaves).

**Preparation**
Berries: eat fresh — flavor ranges from sweet-tart to sour depending on location and ripeness. Excellent for jelly, juice, and wine (small seeds and thin skin). Young leaves: parboil to soften, stuff with grain and meat mixtures (dolmades-style).

**Safety**
**MOONSEED LOOKALIKE (*Menispermum canadense*):** Moonseed is toxic and can resemble wild grape vines. Key distinctions: moonseed leaves have smooth, unlobed or shallowly lobed margins and the leaf stem attaches to the underside of the leaf rather than at the notch; its fruit contains a single crescent-shaped seed (vs. round grape seeds); moonseed vines have no tendrils. Always check for tendrils (grape has them, moonseed does not) and verify seed shape before eating. *Vitis arizonica* range overlaps with moonseed only marginally in the eastern Southwest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Onion
*Allium macropetalum* (Desert Wild Onion), *A. cernuum* (Nodding Wild Onion)

![Wild Onion (Allium cernuum) — nodding pink flower umbel and grass-like leaves from bulb](images/native-plants/allium-cernuum.jpg)

**Identification**
Grass-like to strap-like basal leaves (flat to hollow-cylindrical depending on species) growing from a bulb. Flowers in a terminal cluster (umbel) at the top of a smooth, leafless stem — pink, rose, or white, with 6 petals. *A. macropetalum* is common in rocky desert slopes and grasslands of Arizona and New Mexico, with 2–3 flat leaves and pink flowers. *A. cernuum* has a nodding (drooping) flower head. All true *Allium* species smell strongly of onion or garlic when any part is crushed.

**Where to Find It**
Rocky desert slopes, dry grasslands, piñon-juniper edges, and open areas throughout the Southwest at various elevations.

**Season**
Bulbs available year-round (best spring). Leaves edible spring through early summer. Flowers in spring–early summer.

**Edible Parts**
Bulb, leaves, and flowers — all parts of true *Allium* are edible.

**Preparation**
Bulbs: use raw or cooked like green onions or small onions. Slice into salads, cook in soups and stews. Leaves: use fresh as scallion substitute. Flowers: edible garnish.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL SAFETY RULE — THE SMELL TEST IS MANDATORY:** **Every part of every true *Allium* species smells distinctly of onion or garlic when crushed. This is non-negotiable.** *Death Camas* (*Anticlea elegans*, formerly *Zigadenus*) grows in similar rocky slope and grassland habitats in the Southwest, produces similar grass-like leaves from a bulb, and can be confused with wild onion. **Death Camas has NO onion or garlic smell at all — it smells like nothing or mildly sweet.** If you crush a leaf or bulb and it does not immediately and unmistakably smell of onion or garlic, discard it entirely and do not eat any part of the plant. The smell test must be applied to every individual plant before eating.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Desert Willow
*Chilopsis linearis*

![Desert Willow (Chilopsis linearis) — long narrow leaves and showy orchid-like pink-purple flowers](images/native-plants/chilopsis-linearis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with long, very narrow, willow-like leaves (3–6 inches). The flowers are spectacular and unmistakable — showy, trumpet-shaped, orchid-like blooms in pink to lavender to white, with purple veining inside, appearing throughout summer. Long, narrow seed pods (6–12 inches) hang from the branches and persist into winter. Not a true willow — related to catalpa and bignonia. Grows almost exclusively along desert washes and dry streambeds.

**Where to Find It**
Desert washes, arroyos, dry riverbeds, and sandy canyon bottoms throughout the Sonoran and Chihuahuan deserts. A reliable indicator of underground water in an arroyo. Southern Arizona, New Mexico, west Texas, southern Nevada, and southern California.

**Season**
Flowers: May–September (peak summer). Pods ripen fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fresh flowers (edible in moderation). Young green seed pods (in moderation).

**Preparation**
Flowers: eat fresh as a salad garnish or trail nibble — mild, slightly sweet, faintly nectar-like. Use sparingly; do not eat in bulk. Young green pods: can be cooked and eaten like green beans in small quantities. The flavor is mild and somewhat bitter.

**Safety**
**Use in moderation.** Desert willow contains iridoid glycosides and other compounds that may cause gastrointestinal upset if consumed in large quantities. Traditional use is as a small trail food and medicinal plant — not as a bulk staple. Occasional small amounts of flowers or young pods are considered safe; do not eat large quantities. No dangerous lookalikes in desert wash habitat — the large orchid-like flowers are unmistakable. True willows (*Salix* spp.) in the same habitat have entirely different flowers (catkins) and round, not showy blooms.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Goosefoot
*Chenopodium berlandieri* (Pitseed Goosefoot) and *C. murale* (Nettleleaf Goosefoot)

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium berlandieri) — diamond-shaped mealy leaves with white-gray powder on new growth](images/native-plants/chenopodium-berlandieri.jpg)

**Identification**
Upright annual plants (1–4 feet) with distinctively diamond- to goosefoot-shaped leaves with toothed or wavy margins. Young leaves and stems are coated in a fine white-gray mealy powder (this is the key identification feature — rub a leaf and the white coating comes off on your fingers). Tiny inconspicuous flowers in dense branched clusters at the top. Very common desert weed. *C. berlandieri* has a pitted seed surface; *C. murale* has somewhat coarser, darker-green leaves. Both are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed desert soils, roadsides, washes, agricultural edges, vacant lots, and desert gardens throughout the Southwest. Appears aggressively wherever soil is disturbed — a reliable indicator of past human activity or fertile ground.

**Season**
Young growth: spring through summer (best before plant flowers). Seeds: late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots (cooked greens); seeds (grain).

**Preparation**
Leaves: boil or steam 5 minutes and eat as greens — mild, excellent flavor similar to spinach. High in vitamins A, C, calcium, and protein. Seeds: collect ripe seed heads, thresh, winnow, and cook as a grain (porridge) or grind into flour. Related to quinoa (same genus, formerly).

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating to reduce oxalate content. The white mealy coating is normal and desirable. No toxic lookalikes: no poisonous plants share the combination of mealy white coating, goosefoot-shaped leaves, and weedy desert habitat. Distinguish from nightshades (smooth leaves, no mealy coating, round berries) and from lambsquarters' own toxic-adjacent relatives by confirming the mealy coating is present.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Tumbleweed / Russian Thistle
*Salsola tragus* (syn. *S. iberica*)

![Russian Thistle (Salsola tragus) — young spring growth with soft fleshy green shoots before drying](images/native-plants/salsola-tragus.jpg)

**Identification**
The iconic tumbleweed of the American West — virtually everyone can identify the dry, spherical, spiny, gray-brown weed that rolls across roads and piles against fences. What most people do not know: the young spring plants are entirely different — soft, succulent, grass-like shoots with bright green, cylindrical, fleshy leaves (¼–1 inch long), finely tipped but not yet spiny. As the plant matures and dries through summer, the leaves become hard and spine-tipped, ultimately forming the woody tumbleweed ball.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, disturbed desert soil, agricultural margins, and open desert throughout the entire Southwest and beyond. An introduced invasive species from Eurasia, now ubiquitous.

**Season**
**Spring only (March–May)** — young shoots before the plant begins to harden. Once leaves become rigid or spine-tipped, the plant is no longer pleasant to eat.

**Edible Parts**
Young spring shoots and leaves.

**Preparation**
Harvest young shoots under 6 inches tall with soft, fleshy leaves. Use raw in salads (mild, slightly salty, succulent) or boil 5 minutes as cooked greens. The flavor is pleasant when young — similar to tender lamb's quarters. Once you know what you're looking at in the spring, this is one of the most abundant wild greens in the Southwest. The plant is an invasive weed — harvesting it is ecologically benign.

**Safety**
Spring shoots only — mature plants are unpleasant and painfully spiny. High in oxalic acid when raw; cooking reduces this. No toxic lookalikes at the young stage — the succulent cylindrical fleshy leaves emerging in masses from disturbed desert soil in early spring are distinctive. Do not confuse with young spurges (*Euphorbia* spp.) which exude white milky sap when broken; Russian thistle has clear sap.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Southern Cattail
*Typha domingensis*

![Southern Cattail (Typha domingensis) — brown cylindrical seed heads and tall strap-like leaves at water's edge](images/native-plants/typha-domingensis.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall (5–10 feet), emergent aquatic plant growing in standing or slow water. Long, flat, strap-like leaves (¾–1 inch wide). Unmistakable brown, sausage-shaped seed heads (1–1.5 inches in diameter, 6–15 inches long). *T. domingensis* (Southern Cattail) is slightly more slender than the Common Cattail (*T. latifolia*) and typically has a small gap between the male (upper) and female (lower) portions of the flower spike. Found at lower desert elevations in warmer climates than the common cattail.

**Where to Find It**
Desert springs, stock tanks, riparian corridors, marshes, irrigation channels, and desert washes with permanent or semi-permanent water throughout the Southwest. A highly reliable indicator of water in arid landscapes.

**Season**
Year-round — different parts are harvested at different times. See Preparation.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (spring); green flower heads (late spring); pollen (early summer); root starch (fall–winter).

**Preparation**
See full cattail entry in other regions — all edible parts and preparation methods are identical across cattail species. Summary: (1) Spring shoots: peel outer leaves to reveal white inner core — eat raw or cooked like hearts of palm. (2) Green female flower heads before pollen shed: boil and eat like corn on the cob. (3) Pollen: shake into a bag when male flowers are shedding — use as flour extender. (4) Roots: dig in fall and winter, pound starchy rhizomes in water to extract cattail starch.

**Cross-reference:** See also cattail entries in Region 2 (Northeast), Region 3 (Southeast), and Region 4 (Great Plains) for full detail. The species differs but all preparation is identical.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once the brown seed head is visible. Young shoots before seed heads develop: confirm plant is growing in water, the inner shoot is white (not green), and the cross-section shows concentric leaf layers (not a solid stem like iris, which is toxic). **Iris (*Iris* spp.) lookalike:** Iris also grows in wet areas with strap-like leaves but has no cylindrical seed head, grows in drier soil, and the iris rhizome is visible at the soil surface (knotted, not buried deep). When in doubt, wait for the seed head to confirm identity.

---

## FUNGI

After the monsoon rains of July–September, desert fungi appear in abundance — but disappear quickly in the heat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Desert Puffball
*Calvatia* spp. and *Lycoperdon* spp.

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
Various puffball species appear in the Southwest, ranging from golf ball to small soccer ball size. All are round, featureless white spheres with no cap, stem, or gills. The critical rule applies to all: slice vertically — the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout**. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Desert grasslands, desert edges, juniper grassland, and open areas throughout the Southwest. Most abundant after monsoon rains in July–September. Also found in high-elevation meadows under conifers.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October), peaking during and after monsoon season.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden. Add to soups. Must be used when completely white inside.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. Slice every puffball before eating to check the interior. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which could indicate a deadly *Amanita* button — also round and white), DO NOT EAT IT. The Desert Golfball Puffball (*Lycoperdon* spp.) is also edible when pure white inside — same rules apply.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel (Post-Fire)
*Morchella* spp.

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — cut vertically to confirm one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base. Cap color: tan to gray to near-black depending on species.

**Where to Find It**
Burned areas throughout the Southwest — especially pinon-juniper woodlands, ponderosa pine forests, and mixed conifer zones the spring following a wildfire. Post-fire morels can appear in extraordinary abundance — a burned slope that produced nothing before may be carpeted with morels the spring after the fire. Elevation: 5,000–9,000 feet in the Southwest. Timing: April–June, the spring following a summer/fall fire.

**Season**
Spring (April–June) following wildfire in conifer forests.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset. Sauté in butter over medium-high heat 8–12 minutes until golden. Slice vertically and rinse to remove soil. Add to cream sauces, eggs, risotto, pasta. Dry and store for year-round use — drying intensifies the flavor.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* species) — POISONOUS, potentially FATAL. False morels: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap is wrinkled or brain-like (not honeycomb pits). In burned conifer areas, *Gyromitra* species may appear alongside true morels. **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### King Bolete / Porcini
*Boletus edulis* and related species

![King Bolete / Porcini (Boletus edulis) — brown cap and thick white stem with pore surface](images/native-plants/boletus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, robust mushroom with a brown cap (2–8 inches across), creamy to brownish pore surface underneath (no gills — tiny tubes), and a thick, club-shaped stem with a fine white net pattern near the top (reticulation). The flesh is white to pale yellow and does not change color when cut. Smells pleasant, earthy, nutty.

**Where to Find It**
In the Southwest: mixed conifer and aspen forests at 7,000–10,000 feet elevation after monsoon rains. Under ponderosa pine, spruce, and fir.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October) following monsoon rains.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter, add to pasta, risotto, soups. Dry for storage — drying intensifies the flavor dramatically. One of the world's premier edible fungi (the same as commercial porcini).

**Safety**
**Avoid any bolete with a red or orange pore surface** — some are toxic. Avoid any bolete whose flesh turns blue rapidly when cut — some blue-staining boletes are toxic. The King Bolete has white to tan pores and white non-bluing flesh. When in doubt about a bolete, do not eat it.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Desert Inky Cap
*Coprinellus* spp. and *Coprinus* spp.

![Desert Inky Cap (Coprinellus micaceus) — bell-shaped caps with mica-like sheen, dissolving into black ink](images/native-plants/coprinellus-micaceus.jpg)

**Identification**
Small to medium mushrooms (cap 1–3 inches wide) appearing in tight clusters or loose groups in desert grasslands and disturbed desert soil after rain. Cap is bell-shaped to conical, pale tan to ochre-brown, often with a glistening mica-like surface when young (mica inky cap, *C. micaceus*). The most distinctive feature: as the mushroom ages, the cap and gills dissolve from the margin inward into a black inky liquid (autodigestion). Gills are white when young, turning gray then black. Stem white, hollow, fragile. Harvest window is brief — within hours of emergence after rain.

**Where to Find It**
Desert grasslands, dry washes, disturbed soils, around dead wood, desert parks and gardens throughout the Southwest. Appear explosively after monsoon rains and fall moisture, often in the same spots year after year. More common at desert grassland elevations (2,500–5,500 feet) than in core low desert.

**Season**
After rains — primarily July–October (monsoon season) and again after fall rains. Disappear within 24–48 hours of emergence.

**Edible Parts**
Young caps before any blackening begins.

**Preparation**
Harvest only when caps are still white to tan — the moment black ink appears at the margin, the window is closing. Use within a few hours of harvest; they cannot be stored. Cook immediately: sauté in butter 4–6 minutes over medium heat. Mild, delicate flavor. A quick morning forage after overnight rains is the classic approach — check your spot, harvest young caps, cook for breakfast.

**Safety**
**Alcohol interaction warning:** *Coprinopsis atramentaria* (Common Inky Cap), a close relative, contains coprine which causes a severe and unpleasant reaction when consumed with or within several days of drinking alcohol (flushing, nausea, rapid heartbeat). *Coprinellus micaceus* does NOT contain coprine and does not interact with alcohol, but it can be confused with *C. atramentaria*. **Rule:** if you are uncertain of the species, do not drink alcohol within 72 hours before or after eating any inky cap. *C. atramentaria* is grayish with a more fibrous surface (not mica-sparkly) and lacks the mica sheen. Key: sparkly mica sheen = *C. micaceus* (no coprine); smooth gray surface = *C. atramentaria* (coprine present). When in doubt, apply the alcohol avoidance rule. Both species look similar when young and must be harvested before autodigestion begins — never eat any inky cap that has begun to turn black.

---

---

# REGION 6: PACIFIC NORTHWEST
*Washington, Oregon, Northern California*

The Pacific Northwest may be the richest mushroom-foraging region on the continent. Pacific Golden Chanterelles, King Boletes, Matsutake, Oregon White Truffles — the fall forest here is extraordinary. It is also home to the most important safety warning in this guide: the difference between edible Blue Camas and deadly Death Camas.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Camas
*Camassia quamash*

![Common Camas (Camassia quamash) — blue-violet flower spikes coloring a Pacific Northwest meadow](images/native-plants/camassia-quamash.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows from a bulb (1–5 cm) with a black surface and white interior with papery layers. Leaves are grass-like, basal, and linear. The flower stalk bears 6–20 **blue to blue-violet** flowers opening progressively from bottom to top.

**THE COLOR IS THE CRITICAL LIFE-OR-DEATH FEATURE:** Blue-violet flowers = Common Camas = edible. White or cream flowers = Death Camas (*Toxicoscordion venenosum*) = potentially fatal.

**Where to Find It**
Wet meadows, prairies, valley bottoms, vernal pools throughout the Pacific Northwest, Rocky Mountains, and Intermountain West. Full bloom colors entire meadows blue — historically used to locate productive harvest sites.

**Season**
Flowers April–June. Bulbs harvestable fall through spring. **Only harvest where plants are in blue-violet bloom — never from unconfirmed sites.**

**Edible Parts**
Bulbs — traditionally one of the most important carbohydrate staples of the Pacific Northwest.

**Preparation**
Bulbs are best when slow-cooked — the inulin transforms from bland to rich, sweet, and molasses-like. Traditional pit cooking: stone-lined earth pit, heated rocks, layered with camas, covered, cooked 24–48 hours. Modern equivalent: bake at 250°F for 4–6 hours, or slow-cook in liquid for several hours. Can also be boiled.

**Safety**
**DEATH CAMAS (*Toxicoscordion venenosum* formerly *Zigadenus*)** — POTENTIALLY FATAL — grows in the same habitat with the same grass-like leaves and bulb structure. The bulbs CANNOT BE RELIABLY DISTINGUISHED. **The rule: never harvest camas bulbs unless you are standing next to the plant while it is actively in bloom with blue-violet flowers.** White or cream flowers = Death Camas. Cream with a slight yellow tinge = Death Camas. Any doubt = do not harvest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Miner's Lettuce
*Claytonia perfoliata*

![Miner's Lettuce (Claytonia perfoliata) — distinctive round leaf with stem growing through center](images/native-plants/claytonia-perfoliata.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the most distinctive plants in this guide. Adult leaves are unmistakable: two stem leaves fuse together forming a single round or diamond-shaped platform with the flower stem passing directly through the center — like a stake through a coin. No other plant looks like this. Tiny white flowers emerge from the center of the round leaf.

**Where to Find It**
Shaded moist areas — coastal hills, woodland understory, stream banks, north-facing slopes. Winter-to-spring annual, disappearing in summer heat. Western North America from British Columbia to Baja.

**Season**
Winter through spring (November–May). Best in cool wet conditions.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant above ground — raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — one of the mildest, most pleasant-tasting wild greens available. The round coin-shaped leaves are visually striking in presentations. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Used by California Gold Rush miners to prevent scurvy.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes — the round leaf-around-stem appearance is impossible to confuse.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — serrated leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
An upright perennial (2–6 feet) with opposite, deeply toothed, heart-shaped leaves covered in hollow, silica-tipped stinging hairs that inject formic acid on contact. The sting is immediate and distinctive — burning and itching for minutes to hours. Stems are square. Small, inconspicuous greenish flowers in drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Abundant throughout the Pacific Northwest in moist disturbed areas, stream banks, forest edges, and riparian zones. One of the most productive edible plants in the region — the combination of Pacific Northwest moisture, mild temperatures, and long growing season extends the harvest window significantly. Often available from March through June at lower elevations.

**Season**
Young shoots and top leaves: early spring (March–June in the Pacific Northwest — longer harvest window than eastern regions). Harvest before flowering.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and leaves (cooked only). Cooking destroys the sting completely.

**Preparation**
Wear gloves to harvest. Blanch in boiling water 2–3 minutes (destroys the sting). Use like cooked spinach — in soups, omelets, pasta, risotto, pesto. Nutritionally exceptional: high in iron, calcium, vitamins A and C. Nettle tea: steep dried leaves in hot water.

**Safety**
**Always cook before eating** — raw nettles sting the mouth and throat. Use gloves when handling fresh plants. No toxic lookalikes once stinging hairs are confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fireweed
*Epilobium angustifolium*

![Fireweed (Epilobium angustifolium) — tall spikes of bright magenta-pink flowers](images/native-plants/epilobium-angustifolium.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall (3–6 feet), striking perennial with lance-shaped leaves with pale green secondary veins. Dense spikes of bright magenta-to-pink four-petaled flowers from midsummer onward. After flowering, narrow elongated seed pods split open releasing silky-tailed seeds. One of the first plants to colonize burned and disturbed areas — often carpets entire mountainsides.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed areas, burns, clear-cuts, roadsides, alpine meadows. Especially dense in recently burned areas.

**Season**
Young shoots: spring (April–June) when less than 12 inches tall. Leaves: summer. Flowers: midsummer. Pith from older stems: year-round.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (before flowering), leaves (cooked or tea), flowers (fresh garnish), and the inner pith of older stems.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: steam or sauté like asparagus — mild, tender, excellent. Leaves: dry for tea or cook as greens. Flowers: garnish salads or infuse into syrups (beautiful pink color). Stem pith: scoop from older hollow stems, mix into foods as a thickener, or eat directly.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive magenta flower spikes and willow-like leaves.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — yellow flower, toothed leaves, and white seedhead](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with deeply toothed, lance-shaped basal leaves. Single bright yellow composite flower heads on hollow stems that bleed white latex when broken. Transforms into the familiar white spherical seedhead. Taproot is substantial, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, roadsides, disturbed areas, and meadows throughout the Pacific Northwest. One of the most productive edible plants in the cool, moist Pacific Northwest climate — available nearly year-round at lower coastal elevations.

**Season**
Leaves: nearly year-round at coastal lowland elevations. Best in spring and fall when least bitter. Flowers: spring. Roots: fall through early spring.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves (raw or cooked), flowers (raw or cooked), roots (cooked or roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Young spring leaves: raw in salads. Blanch briefly to reduce bitterness. Flowers: battered and fried, made into jelly or wine. Roots: roast at 350°F until very dark, grind, brew as coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the combination of toothed basal leaves, hollow latex-bearing stem, and yellow composite flower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Forms dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small (1–3 cm), opposite, oval, pointed, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 petals each so deeply notched they appear to be 10. The single most reliable feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, under shrubs, disturbed areas, and shaded spots throughout the Pacific Northwest. Among the most productive edible plants in the cool, moist Pacific Northwest climate — chickweed is available nearly year-round at lower coastal elevations, often producing lush mats through winter when little else is growing.

**Season**
Nearly year-round in the coastal Pacific Northwest. Best in winter and spring (November–May). Dies back in summer heat.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy flavor. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green combined with more flavorful foraged greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis oregana* (Redwood Sorrel) and *O. stricta*

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped clover-like leaflets and tiny yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Low plants with distinctive three-part leaves, each leaflet heart-shaped (resembling shamrocks). *O. oregana* (Redwood Sorrel) forms dense mats on the forest floor in the Pacific Northwest — leaves are larger and often purple-tinged on the underside. Yellow or pink flowers with five petals. Leaves fold down at night. The stems and leaves taste distinctly tart and lemony.

**Where to Find It**
*O. oregana*: dense forest understory under conifers and redwoods — very common in the moist Pacific Northwest forests. *O. stricta*: lawns, roadsides, and disturbed ground. Available throughout the region.

**Season**
Spring through fall. The tart leaves are available whenever the plant is growing.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods (all tart and lemony).

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail nibble or salad addition — the sour taste is pleasant. Chew as a thirst quencher on the trail.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — do not eat in large quantities. Fine in small amounts as a flavoring or trail nibble.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems are hollow and float. The flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Cold, clear, spring-fed streams and seeps throughout the Pacific Northwest. Very common — the abundant rainfall and cold springs of the region create ideal watercress habitat. Found in cold mountain streams, coastal seeps, and spring-fed channels throughout Washington, Oregon, and Northern California.

**Season**
Best in cool months (fall through spring). Available year-round where springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. The diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder. Stems are grooved and often striped in red, green, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides throughout the Pacific Northwest. Common in disturbed areas and garden beds throughout the region.

**Season**
Late spring through fall. Young leaves and shoot tips are best in early to midsummer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Add to soups, omelets, pasta. Nutritionally superior to spinach.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating, do not eat in very large quantities daily.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia / T. angustifolia*

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — tall grass-like leaves and hot dog-shaped brown seed head](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall (4–9 feet), grass-like aquatic plant with long, flat, strap-like leaves and the unmistakable hot-dog-shaped brown seed head on a stiff stalk. Grows in dense stands in shallow water or wet soil.

**Where to Find It**
Ponds, marshes, estuaries, stream banks, and all freshwater wetlands throughout the Pacific Northwest. Common in low-elevation wetlands, coastal marshes, and riverside habitats from British Columbia to California.

**Season**
Young shoots: early spring. Green pollen heads (male): late spring–early summer. Pollen: early summer. Rhizomes: year-round.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (raw or cooked), green male pollen heads (cooked), pollen (flour), starchy rhizomes (year-round).

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel outer leaves to the white core, eat raw or sauté. Pollen heads: boil before pollen is released, eat like corn on the cob. Pollen: collect and use as bright yellow flour supplement. Rhizomes: dig, peel, pound to extract starch.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once the mature brown seed head is confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria vesca* (Wood Strawberry) and *F. virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated. *F. vesca* (Wood Strawberry) is most common in Pacific Northwest forests.

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, forest edges, roadsides, and clearings throughout the Pacific Northwest. Common from lowland coastal areas to subalpine meadows.

**Season**
Flowers in late spring. Fruit ripens June–July (lower elevation) to August (subalpine). Leaves available spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, or dried. The flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*) has insipid but non-toxic berries — distinguished by yellow flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cow Parsnip
*Heracleum lanatum*

![Cow Parsnip (Heracleum lanatum) — large flat-topped white flower umbel and enormous leaves](images/native-plants/heracleum-lanatum.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, coarse herbaceous plant (3–8 feet) with enormous leaves (up to 2.5 feet across), broadly lobed and rough-textured, with sheaths at each petiole base. Stout hollow hairy stems. Large flat-topped clusters (umbels) of white flowers. Smells of carrot or parsley.

**Where to Find It**
Moist areas, stream banks, forest edges, mountain meadows throughout the Pacific Northwest and Rocky Mountains.

**Season**
Young plants in early spring (March–April) before the flower stalk elongates. Young peeled stems before flowering are the primary food.

**Edible Parts**
Peeled young stems before flowering.

**Preparation**
Peel the outer skin completely. The inner stem is mildly flavored and starchy when boiled or steamed. Do not eat the leaves.

**Safety**
**FURANOCOUMARINS CAUTION:** Cow parsnip sap causes severe phototoxic burns when contacted by skin in sunlight — chemical blisters that may leave permanent scars. Wear long sleeves and gloves when harvesting; avoid skin contact, especially in direct sunlight. Cooking eliminates this concern.

**DANGEROUS LOOKALIKE:** Water Hemlock (*Cicuta* spp.) — ONE OF THE MOST TOXIC PLANTS IN NORTH AMERICA — grows in the same moist habitats and has similar white flower umbels. Water hemlock is smaller, has narrower leaflets, and the rootstock when cut shows a series of chambers filled with amber-yellow sap that smells like parsnip. **Beginners should not harvest any plant from the carrot family (Apiaceae) without expert in-person guidance. The family contains multiple deadly species.**

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mountain Huckleberry
*Vaccinium membranaceum*

![Mountain Huckleberry (Vaccinium membranaceum) — dark berries on shrub in subalpine forest](images/native-plants/vaccinium-membranaceum.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs (1–4 feet) with small, oval, alternately arranged leaves with fine serrations tipped with glandular teeth (visible with a hand lens). Urn-shaped pink flowers in spring. Berries ripen from red through dark purple to nearly black at peak ripeness — smaller and more intense in flavor than cultivated blueberries.

**Where to Find It**
Subalpine forests and open slopes, mixed conifer forests at mid-to-high elevations throughout the Pacific Northwest.

**Season**
Depends sharply on elevation. Lower (2,000–4,000 feet): mid-July. Upper (5,000–7,000 feet): August into September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries only.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent. Dried like raisins. Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial. Freezes exceptionally well.

**Safety**
The five-pointed crown (calyx) at the blossom end of the berry distinguishes all *Vaccinium* species from any toxic lookalike. No dangerous lookalikes once the crown is confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Salal
*Gaultheria shallon*

![Salal (Gaultheria shallon) — leathery evergreen leaves and dark waxy berries in coastal forest](images/native-plants/gaultheria-shallon.jpg)

**Identification**
An evergreen coastal shrub (2–6 feet) with thick, leathery oval leaves and zigzag stems. White to pink urn-shaped flowers on red stems. Fruit: dark purple to nearly black, waxy, ¼–½ inch, in elongated clusters. The leathery texture of the leaves and the waxy dark berries are distinctive.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal forest understory, shaded slopes from Alaska to California. Tolerates heavy shade. Extremely common in the Pacific Northwest coastal zone.

**Season**
Berries ripen August–October.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries. Young tender leaves (tea).

**Preparation**
Berries: thick-skinned and seedy — best for jam, cordial, or dried rather than fresh eating in quantity. Jam made from salal has a deep, rich, spiced flavor. Traditional coastal food. Dry and grind for fruit leather.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the combination of leathery zigzag stems and dark waxy berry clusters.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oregon Grape
*Mahonia aquifolium* (Tall Oregon Grape) and *M. nervosa* (Cascade Oregon Grape)

![Oregon Grape (Mahonia aquifolium) — blue berries with white bloom and spiny holly-like leaves](images/native-plants/mahonia-aquifolium.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs (1–6 feet) with stiff, pinnately compound leaves resembling holly — the leaflets are oval with spiny teeth. Clusters of bright yellow flowers in spring. The blue-black berries ripen in late summer, covered in a white powdery bloom (bloom), in grape-like clusters. The bright yellow inner wood and roots are distinctive.

**Where to Find It**
Dry to moist forest understory, rocky slopes. Common throughout the Pacific Northwest at all elevations.

**Season**
Berries ripen July–September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries (processed), young flower clusters (edible raw), inner bark (medicinal tea — not food).

**Preparation**
Berries: intensely tart to sour — not typically eaten raw in quantity. Excellent for jelly and jam (high natural pectin, beautiful color). Cordial. Mix with sweeter berries for juice blends.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive spiny holly-like leaves and yellow flower-to-blue-berry combination.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Salmonberry
*Rubus spectabilis*

![Salmonberry (Rubus spectabilis) — salmon-pink berries on thorny canes](images/native-plants/rubus-spectabilis.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub (3–10 feet) with thorny canes and compound leaves of 3–5 leaflets. Large, showy, magenta-pink flowers in early spring (one of the first blooms). The berry resembles a raspberry but ranges from yellow-orange to deep salmon-pink to red, sometimes dark red, with drupelets like a raspberry.

**Where to Find It**
Moist forest, stream banks, coastal areas. Very common in the Pacific Northwest from Alaska to northern California.

**Season**
One of the earliest fruits — berries ripen June–July.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries. Young spring shoots (peeled, eaten raw or cooked like asparagus).

**Preparation**
Berries: fresh eating (quality ranges from excellent to watery depending on plant and conditions). Jam. Young spring shoots: peel outer skin, eat the pale inner shoot — mild, pleasant.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the salmon-pink to red raspberry-shaped berries.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Thimbleberry
*Rubus parviflorus*

![Thimbleberry (Rubus parviflorus) — large white flowers and soft red thimble-shaped berries](images/native-plants/rubus-parviflorus.jpg)

**Identification**
A thornless shrub (3–6 feet) with large, maple-like leaves (up to 10 inches across) and large, showy white flowers. The fruit is a soft, broad, flat, red raspberry-shaped berry — resembling a thimble. The thornless stems and distinctive maple-leaf-like leaves make thimbleberry immediately recognizable.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, clearings, roadsides, mountain slopes throughout the Pacific Northwest.

**Season**
Berries ripen July–August. The ripe berry is so soft it barely survives picking without crushing.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries, young leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Fresh eating (the best use — flavor is excellent, like a raspberry with more complexity). Jam. Too soft to transport well. Young leaf tea.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the combination of thornless stems, maple-like leaves, and raspberry-shaped berries.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry (Blue/Black Elder)
*Sambucus nigra* ssp. *caerulea* (Blue Elderberry)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny blue-black spheres (sometimes with a white powdery bloom) in heavy drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, forest edges, open slopes, and disturbed areas throughout the Pacific Northwest. Very common — an important part of the Pacific Northwest foraging calendar. Found from lowland riparian areas to montane slopes.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Berries: August–September.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems, steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Fry whole flower clusters in light batter for fritters. Berries: always cook. Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL REGIONAL NOTE:** *Red Elderberry (S. racemosa)* grows throughout the Pacific Northwest and has **red berries in a DOMED (not flat-topped) cluster** — the berries are toxic raw. **Blue/Black Elder = flat-topped flower/berry cluster + blue-black berries = edible when cooked. Red berries in a domed cluster = do not eat raw.** Always confirm the cluster shape. When in doubt, do not eat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pacific Crabapple
*Malus fusca*

![Pacific Crabapple (Malus fusca) — tiny apple-like fruit on thorny branches](images/native-plants/malus-fusca.jpg)

**Identification**
A small thorny tree (10–30 feet) with alternate, toothed, oval leaves and clusters of white five-petaled flowers in spring. The small fruit (½–¾ inch) ripens from green to yellow to red, resembling a tiny apple. Native to the Pacific Northwest coastal zone.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal forest edges, stream banks, wetland margins from Alaska to northern California.

**Season**
Fruit ripens August–September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not eat seeds.

**Preparation**
Very tart raw — best cooked. Jelly (excellent), sauce, cordial. Traditional food of coastal First Nations peoples.

**Safety**
Do not chew seeds — cyanogenic compounds like all *Prunus/Malus* relatives.

---

## FUNGI

The Pacific Northwest produces the most diverse and abundant wild edible mushroom harvest in North America.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pacific Golden Chanterelle
*Cantharellus formosus*

![Pacific Golden Chanterelle (Cantharellus formosus) — golden-yellow mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-formosus.jpg)

**Identification**
The premier edible mushroom of the Pacific Northwest. Egg-yolk yellow to deep gold cap with wavy irregular margin, blunt forking false gills (ridges, not true gills) running down the stem, solid white interior, fruity apricot smell. Grows from soil, not wood. Solitary or scattered.

**Where to Find It**
Conifer and mixed forests — particularly under western hemlock, Sitka spruce, Douglas fir, and western red cedar. In the same spots each year.

**Season**
Fall (September–December in the Pacific Northwest lowlands). Can be found through January in mild coastal areas. The Pacific Northwest is the world's largest chanterelle producing region.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously — absorbs water and becomes mushy. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. The flavor is fruity, slightly peppery, excellent in cream sauces and pasta. One of the most commercially valuable wild mushrooms of the Pacific Northwest.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus olivascens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at tree bases or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), gills glow faintly in the dark. **False Chanterelle (*Hygrophoropsis aurantiaca*)** has thin, crowded, forking gills (not blunt ridges). **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Matsutake
*Tricholoma magnivelare*

![American Matsutake (Tricholoma magnivelare) — white-capped mushroom with distinctive spicy cinnamon smell](images/native-plants/tricholoma-magnivelare.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, dense white mushroom with a cap (2–6 inches) that is white when young, developing brownish stains with age. True white gills (not running down stem). Thick, stout stem with a white ring. Most distinctive feature: the **smell is extraordinary** — a spicy, sweet cinnamon-and-pine aroma unlike any other mushroom. This smell is the primary identification feature.

**Where to Find It**
Under pine trees — particularly pine-dominated stands at low-to-mid elevations. In Oregon and Washington, especially under ponderosa pine and lodgepole pine. Often found in sandy soil.

**Season**
Fall (September–November). Emerges after the first fall rains.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh mushroom.

**Preparation**
Do not wash — the spicy aroma is volatile and water diminishes it. Slice and grill, wrap in foil with a drop of soy sauce, or sauté minimally. The flavor is extraordinary — one of the most prized mushrooms in Japanese cuisine (same species, different subspecies). The Pacific Northwest matsutake commands high prices when exported to Japan.

**Safety**
Some white *Amanita* species (potentially deadly) could be confused with young matsutake. Confirm the distinctive spicy smell — no Amanita smells like matsutake. Also check that the gills are free and white (not attached to a volva at the base — Amanitas have a cup at the base). Always dig up the full base to confirm no volva.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### King Bolete / Porcini
*Boletus edulis* and related species

![King Bolete / Porcini (Boletus edulis) — brown cap and thick white stem with pore surface](images/native-plants/boletus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, robust mushroom with a brown cap (2–8 inches across), creamy to brownish pore surface underneath (no gills — tiny tubes opening as pores), and a thick, club-shaped stem with a fine white net pattern near the top (reticulation). The flesh is white to pale yellow and does not change color when cut. Smells pleasant, earthy, nutty.

**Where to Find It**
Under Sitka spruce, Douglas fir, western hemlock, and mixed conifers from sea level to subalpine zones throughout the Pacific Northwest. The Pacific Northwest is one of the world's premier king bolete regions — the combination of old-growth conifer stands and wet fall conditions produces exceptional flushes. Peak season September–November.

**Season**
Fall (September–November). Most productive after the first heavy fall rains.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter, add to pasta, risotto, soups. Dry for storage — drying intensifies the flavor dramatically into the classic porcini aroma. One of the world's premier edible fungi.

**Safety**
**Avoid any bolete with a red or orange pore surface** — some are toxic. Avoid any bolete whose flesh turns blue rapidly when cut — some blue-staining boletes are toxic. The King Bolete has white to tan pores and white non-bluing flesh. When in doubt about any bolete, do not eat it.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hedgehog Mushroom
*Hydnum repandum*

![Hedgehog Mushroom (Hydnum repandum) — pale tan cap with distinctive spines (teeth) underneath](images/native-plants/hydnum-repandum.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the safest edible mushrooms for beginners because of its completely unique feature: instead of gills or pores underneath the cap, the hedgehog mushroom has **brittle, downward-pointing teeth (spines)** covering the underside. Cap is pale tan to cream, 1–4 inches, irregular and wavy. Stem central, same color. The teeth are the instant ID.

**Where to Find It**
Conifer and mixed forest throughout the Pacific Northwest. Very common under Douglas fir and western hemlock.

**Season**
Fall (October–December).

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter — mild, nutty flavor, excellent texture. The teeth may need to be rubbed off if very large. One of the best beginner mushrooms because the teeth make it unmistakable.

**Safety**
The teeth underneath are completely unique — there is no toxic mushroom with teeth instead of gills or pores in the Pacific Northwest. A beginner-safe choice.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lobster Mushroom
*Hypomyces lactifluorum*

![Lobster Mushroom (Hypomyces lactifluorum) — bright orange-red parasitized mushroom](images/native-plants/lobster-mushroom.jpg)

**Identification**
Not a single mushroom species but a parasitic fungus (*Hypomyces lactifluorum*) that transforms its host mushroom (typically *Russula* or *Lactarius* species) into a bright orange-to-red, dense, distorted mass with a lobster-colored exterior and white interior. The surface is rough-textured with tiny pimple-like pores. The bright orange color is unmistakable.

**Where to Find It**
Under mixed conifers — Douglas fir, western hemlock, and spruce — throughout the Pacific Northwest. Very common and commercially valued — Pacific Northwest lobster mushrooms are harvested commercially. Appears summer through fall, often in the same spots annually.

**Season**
Summer through fall (July–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire transformed fruiting body when fresh and firm.

**Preparation**
The flavor is outstanding — mild, dense, with a seafood-like quality resembling lobster bisque. Sauté in butter, add to cream sauces, soups. The firm flesh holds up well to cooking. One of the most commercially valuable wild mushrooms of the Pacific Northwest.

**Safety**
Once fully parasitized with the entire external surface bright orange, the host is no longer identifiable and the transformation is considered to eliminate any toxicity concern. Do not eat if the surface is not fully orange or if any white host mushroom gills remain visible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oregon White Truffle
*Tuber oregonense* and *T. gibbosum*

*[Photo needed: Tuber oregonense — pale white hypogeous truffle with marbled interior, found under Douglas fir. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
Hypogeous (underground) fungi, 1–3 inches across, with a white to pale gray exterior and white interior that becomes marbled with gray as spores mature. Found by probing soil or raking leaf litter near the base of Douglas fir trees. The smell when ripe is strong, garlicky-earthy — detecting the smell in the soil is how experienced hunters find them.

**Where to Find It**
Under Douglas fir in the Willamette Valley and coastal ranges of Oregon and Washington. 1–6 inches deep in the soil.

**Season**
Late fall through early spring (November–March). Must be fully mature (interior marbled, strong smell) to be worth eating.

**Preparation**
Shave thinly over pasta, eggs, or risotto. The garlic-earth aroma is volatile — use fresh and minimize cooking. Oregon White Truffles have a different, arguably more complex flavor than European Black Truffles.

**Safety**
No toxic underground fungi with the same appearance and smell in this habitat. However, harvesting truffles typically requires a trained dog or pig, or years of experience finding them by smell in the soil.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. True gills run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, stubby, off-center or absent. White spore print. Found in cool weather on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead alder, cottonwood, bigleaf maple, and other hardwoods throughout the Pacific Northwest. The mild, wet Pacific Northwest climate produces year-round oyster mushrooms in the coastal zone — found on dead hardwoods throughout winter when other mushrooms are absent. A very reliable winter-season find.

**Season**
Year-round in the Pacific Northwest coastal zone. Best in fall through spring. May be found on dead alder along any stream in winter.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well. One of the most widely cultivated mushrooms — the wild version has identical flavor and food value.

**Safety**
Angel wings (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) grows on conifer wood and is smaller and pure white — avoid. Oyster mushrooms on hardwoods with the described characteristics are generally reliable, but avoid specimens that are old, soft, or smell sour.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hen of the Woods / Maitake
*Grifola frondosa*

![Hen of the Woods (Grifola frondosa) — overlapping gray-brown fronds at tree base](images/native-plants/grifola-frondosa.jpg)

**Identification**
A large polypore mushroom growing at the base of oaks or from buried roots as a dense cluster of overlapping, fan-shaped gray-to-brown fronds with white to cream pore surfaces underneath. The fronds are thin and flexible, not shelf-like. The entire mass rises from a single central base. Smells pleasantly earthy and mushroom-like.

**Where to Find It**
Base of mature oaks in appropriate habitat throughout the Pacific Northwest lowlands — particularly in the Willamette Valley and other oak-dominated lowland areas of Oregon and Washington. Returns to the same spot annually. Less common than in the eastern US but present wherever mature oaks grow.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh fruiting body. Tender outer fronds are best.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or oil, roast in the oven, add to soups and stir-fries. One of the most flavorful and versatile wild mushrooms. Dries well for storage.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes for a large gray-brown overlapping frond cluster at an oak base. Berkeley's Polypore (*Bondarzewia berkeleyi*) is similar but much paler and is edible (though tougher).


---

---

# REGION 7: ROCKY MOUNTAINS AND INTERMOUNTAIN WEST
*Montana, Idaho, Wyoming, Colorado, Utah (mountains), Nevada (mountains)*

The Rockies are a vertical landscape. Moving up a mountain is like moving north — 1,000 feet of elevation equals approximately 300 miles of latitude in terms of plant communities. Spring moves upslope at about 100 feet per week after lower elevations thaw. The forager follows the harvest upward through summer.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Camas
*Camassia quamash*

![Common Camas (Camassia quamash) — blue-violet flower spikes coloring a Pacific Northwest meadow](images/native-plants/camassia-quamash.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows from a bulb (1–5 cm) with a black surface and white interior. Leaves are grass-like, basal, and linear. The flower stalk bears 6–20 **blue to blue-violet** flowers opening progressively from bottom to top.

**THE COLOR IS THE CRITICAL LIFE-OR-DEATH FEATURE:** Blue-violet flowers = Common Camas = edible. White or cream flowers = Death Camas (*Toxicoscordion venenosum*) = potentially fatal.

**Where to Find It**
Wet mountain meadows, vernal pools, and valley bottoms throughout the Rockies at 4,000–8,000 feet elevation. The same wetland meadow habitat as in the Pacific Northwest — look for blue meadows in late spring.

**Season**
Flowers May–June (timing varies with elevation). Bulbs harvestable fall through spring. **Only harvest where plants are in blue-violet bloom — never from unconfirmed sites.**

**Edible Parts**
Bulbs — traditionally one of the most important carbohydrate staples of Rocky Mountain tribes.

**Preparation**
Bulbs require slow cooking — the inulin transforms from bland to rich, sweet, and molasses-like. Traditional pit cooking: stone-lined earth pit, heated rocks, layered with camas, covered, cooked 24–48 hours. Modern: bake at 250°F for 4–6 hours or slow-cook in liquid.

**Safety**
**DEATH CAMAS (*Toxicoscordion venenosum*)** — POTENTIALLY FATAL — grows in the same habitat with the same grass-like leaves and bulb structure. The bulbs CANNOT BE RELIABLY DISTINGUISHED. **Never harvest camas bulbs unless standing next to the plant in active blue-violet bloom.** White or cream flowers = Death Camas. Any doubt = do not harvest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fireweed
*Epilobium angustifolium*

![Fireweed (Epilobium angustifolium) — tall spikes of bright magenta-pink flowers](images/native-plants/epilobium-angustifolium.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall (3–6 feet), striking perennial with lance-shaped leaves with pale green secondary veins. Dense spikes of bright magenta-to-pink four-petaled flowers from midsummer onward. After flowering, narrow elongated seed pods split open releasing silky-tailed seeds. One of the first plants to colonize burned and disturbed areas — often carpets entire mountainsides.

**Where to Find It**
One of the most abundant plants in disturbed mountain habitats throughout the Rockies — clear-cuts, burns, roadsides, and alpine meadow edges. Young spring shoots at lower elevations (May–June) and at high elevations through June into July.

**Season**
Young shoots: spring (May–June at lower elevations, later at altitude). Leaves: summer. Flowers: midsummer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots (before flowering), leaves (cooked or tea), flowers (fresh garnish), and the inner pith of older stems.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: steam or sauté like asparagus — mild, tender, excellent. Leaves: dry for tea or cook as greens. Flowers: garnish salads or infuse into syrups. Stem pith: scoop from older hollow stems, mix into foods as a thickener.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive magenta flower spikes and willow-like leaves.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — yellow flower, toothed leaves, and white seedhead](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with deeply toothed, lance-shaped basal leaves. Single bright yellow composite flower heads on hollow stems that bleed white latex when broken. Taproot is substantial, brown-skinned, white-fleshed.

**Where to Find It**
Found at all elevations from valley bottoms to treeline throughout the Rockies. At higher elevations (above 9,000 feet), the growing season is compressed — harvest in June–July. A reliable food source throughout the vertical Rockies landscape.

**Season**
June–July at high elevation. Earlier at lower elevations. Leaves are best in spring and fall when least bitter.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves (raw or cooked), flowers (raw or cooked), roots (cooked or roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Young spring leaves: raw in salads. Blanch briefly to reduce bitterness. Flowers: battered and fried, made into jelly or wine. Roots: roast at 350°F until very dark, grind, brew as coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the combination of toothed basal leaves, hollow latex-bearing stem, and yellow composite flower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis stricta* and related species

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped clover-like leaflets and tiny yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Low plants with distinctive three-part leaves, each leaflet heart-shaped (resembling shamrocks). Yellow five-petaled flowers. Leaves fold down at night. The stems and leaves taste distinctly tart and lemony.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, roadsides, woodland edges, and disturbed ground throughout the Rockies. Common as a lawn weed in mountain towns; also found in forest understory.

**Season**
Spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods (all tart and lemony).

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail nibble — the sour taste is pleasant in small quantities. Add to salads for tartness. Chew as a thirst quencher.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — do not eat in large quantities. Fine in small amounts as a flavoring or trail nibble.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria vesca* (Wood Strawberry) and *F. virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. *F. vesca* (Wood Strawberry) is the dominant Rockies species — same use as *F. virginiana*, equally flavorful. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored.

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, forest edges, and cleared areas throughout the Rockies. Common from valley bottoms to subalpine meadows. The growing season follows elevation — harvest at lower elevations in June, at higher elevations in July–August.

**Season**
Fruit ripens June–August (varies by elevation).

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, or dried. The flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries.

**Safety**
No dangerous lookalikes. Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*) has insipid but non-toxic berries — distinguished by yellow flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets). Small white four-petaled flowers. Stems hollow and float. Flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Cold mountain springs and streams throughout the Rockies. Very common — found in spring seeps, cold stream headwaters, and protected creek sections. Always harvest from confirmed clean sources above agricultural and livestock areas.

**Season**
Best in cool months (spring and fall). Available when springs don't freeze.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — serrated leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
An upright perennial (2–6 feet) with opposite, deeply toothed, heart-shaped leaves covered in hollow stinging hairs that inject formic acid on contact. The sting is immediate and distinctive. Stems are square. Small greenish flowers in drooping clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Moist disturbed areas, stream banks, forest edges, and nitrogen-rich soils throughout the Rockies. Common along stream corridors in valley bottoms and at mid-elevation; less common at high altitude.

**Season**
Young shoots and top leaves: spring (May–June at lower elevations, later at altitude).

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and leaves (cooked only). Cooking destroys the sting completely.

**Preparation**
Wear gloves to harvest. Blanch in boiling water 2–3 minutes (destroys the sting). Use like cooked spinach in soups, omelets, pasta, pesto. Nutritionally exceptional.

**Safety**
**Always cook before eating.** Use gloves when handling fresh plants.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Blue Violet
*Viola sororia* and related mountain species

![Common Blue Violet (Viola sororia) — heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers](images/native-plants/viola-sororia.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with heart-shaped, scallop-edged basal leaves 1–3 inches across. Flowers are deep violet-blue (sometimes white or yellow depending on species) with five petals, the lower three with darker veins. Multiple violet species grow in the Rockies — all edible.

**Where to Find It**
Moist meadows, forest edges, stream banks, and disturbed ground throughout the Rockies. Common from valley bottoms to subalpine meadows.

**Season**
Leaves: spring through early summer. Flowers: spring–early summer.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves and flowers both edible. Leaves are high in vitamin C and vitamin A.

**Preparation**
Leaves raw in salads or cooked as greens. Flowers as colorful garnish. Flowers candied as an elegant edible decoration.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for violets with the characteristic heart-shaped leaves and violet flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Ginger
*Asarum caudatum* (Western Wild Ginger)

![Wild Ginger (Asarum canadense) — heart-shaped leaves and single maroon flower at base](images/native-plants/asarum-canadense.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, slow-spreading ground cover with large, paired, heart-shaped, velvety leaves 3–6 inches across, growing close to the ground in colonies. A single small maroon-brown, three-lobed flower hides at ground level under the leaf pair in early spring. The roots (rhizomes) smell strongly of ginger when crushed — the most reliable ID feature. *A. caudatum* (Western Wild Ginger) has longer, more pointed leaf tips than the eastern species.

**Where to Find It**
Moist, shaded forest understory under conifers and mixed forest throughout the Rockies. Common in moist canyon bottoms and shaded riparian zones.

**Season**
Rhizomes: year-round. Leaves: spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Rhizomes (as a ginger substitute/flavoring). Leaves (small amounts as flavoring). Not eaten in large quantities.

**Preparation**
Rhizomes: dig, wash, chop fine, and use as a ginger substitute in teas, stir-fries, baked goods. Dry and grind as a spice. The flavor is distinctly ginger-like but with a more resinous, earthy quality.

**Safety**
Contains aristolochic acids — use as a flavoring in small amounts, not as a bulk food. No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive ginger smell.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Onion
*Allium cernuum* (Nodding Onion), *A. textile*, *A. geyeri* and related species

![Wild Garlic (Allium vineale) — grass-like leaves and round flower head with tiny bulblets](images/native-plants/allium-vineale.jpg)

**Identification**
Multiple *Allium* species grow throughout the Rockies — all are edible. The nodding onion (*A. cernuum*) has a distinctive drooping (nodding) flower cluster of pink to white flowers on a stem that curves downward at the tip. All species have grass-like to flat basal leaves growing from a bulb, with the essential onion smell. **The smell test is absolute: if it smells like onion when crushed, it is safe to eat.**

**Where to Find It**
Mountain meadows, forest edges, rocky slopes, and disturbed areas throughout the Rockies. Very common — found from valley bottoms to subalpine zones.

**Season**
Spring leaves and bulbs (May–July). Fall bulbs after tops die back.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: bulbs, leaves, stems, flowers. All edible.

**Preparation**
Leaves chopped fresh like chives. Bulbs cooked in any dish requiring onion or garlic. Pickled whole. Dried and stored.

**Safety**
The smell test is the safety protocol — if it smells like onion, it is safe. **Death Camas (*Anticlea/Zigadenus*)** is the dangerous lookalike — same grass-like leaves and similar mountain meadow habitat, but NO onion smell whatsoever. Always perform the smell test before harvesting any onion-like plant in the mountains.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mountain Huckleberry
*Vaccinium membranaceum*

![Mountain Huckleberry (Vaccinium membranaceum) — dark berries on shrub in subalpine forest](images/native-plants/vaccinium-membranaceum.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs (1–4 feet) with small, oval, alternately arranged leaves with fine serrations tipped with glandular teeth (visible with a hand lens). Urn-shaped pink flowers in spring. Berries ripen from red through dark purple to nearly black at peak ripeness — smaller and more intense in flavor than cultivated blueberries.

**Where to Find It**
Subalpine forests and open slopes, mixed conifer forests at mid-to-high elevations throughout the Rocky Mountains. Montana named the huckleberry its state fruit — berry hunting is a serious seasonal activity here, with entire families making annual trips to productive mountain slopes. Many tribal nations have treaty rights to gather huckleberries on public lands. Most abundant in the northern and central Rockies (Montana, Idaho, Wyoming).

**Season**
