Depends sharply on elevation. Lower (4,000–6,000 feet): mid-July. Upper (7,000–9,000 feet): August into September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries only.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent. Dried like raisins. Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial. Freezes exceptionally well.

**Safety**
The five-pointed crown (calyx) at the blossom end of the berry distinguishes all *Vaccinium* species from any toxic lookalike. No dangerous lookalikes once the crown is confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Serviceberry / Saskatoon
*Amelanchier alnifolia*

![Serviceberry (Amelanchier) — dark purple berries and finely toothed oval leaves](images/native-plants/amelanchier.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub to small tree (3–15 feet) with oval, finely serrated alternate leaves. One of the first shrubs to flower in spring — white star-shaped five-petaled flowers appear before or with the first leaves. Fruit ripens in early summer, turning from red to dark purple when fully ripe. *A. alnifolia* (Saskatoon) is the mountain-adapted species — slightly rounder leaves, very productive, sweeter than eastern species.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, forest edges, mountain slopes, and valley bottoms throughout the Rockies. The Saskatoon serviceberry is one of the most productive and sweet wild berries of the mountain West. Common throughout Montana, Idaho, Wyoming, Colorado, and Utah. Very important historically as a primary component of pemmican — an essential calorie-dense winter food for mountain tribes.

**Season**
Fruit ripe June–July, window of 2–3 weeks. Fully purple berries are sweet; red berries still tart. Higher elevations ripen later.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — excellent, sweet. Dried like raisins (traditional — dried serviceberries are a pemmican ingredient). Jam. Pie. Muffins. Cordial.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe purple serviceberries with their characteristic fine-toothed oval leaves. Confirm the five-petaled flower or the characteristic berry with a tiny five-pointed crown at the blossom end.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chokecherry
*Prunus virginiana*

![Chokecherry (Prunus virginiana) — elongated fruit clusters (racemes) of dark berries](images/native-plants/prunus-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with alternate, oval, finely toothed leaves (1–4 inches). Bark grayish-red with small pores (lenticels). White flowers in elongated cylindrical clusters (racemes) — distinctly cylindrical, not flat-topped like elder. Berries ripen dark red to nearly black in late summer, in the same elongated cluster configuration.

**Where to Find It**
Valley bottoms, stream banks, forest edges, rocky slopes, and roadsides throughout the Rocky Mountains. Extremely common — one of the most widespread shrubs of the mountain West. One of the most important traditional food plants of mountain tribes and Plains nations across many centuries. Common throughout Montana, Idaho, Wyoming, Colorado, Utah, and New Mexico.

**Season**
Flowers late May–June. Berries ripen August–September.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berry flesh and juice only. Do not chew seeds.

**Preparation**
Very astringent raw — the name "chokecherry" is accurate. Jelly (excellent — natural pectin). Cordial (simmer berries with water and sugar, strain). Syrup. Dried fruit leather (traditional Plains and mountain preparation). **Do not chew pits** — cyanogenic.

**Safety**
Pits, leaves, and bark contain cyanogenic glycosides. Strain seeds from all processed products. The astringency of raw berries deters overconsumption.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus racemosa* (Red Elderberry — **berries toxic raw**) and *S. nigra* ssp. *caerulea* (Blue Elderberry — **edible when cooked**)

**CRITICAL REGIONAL NOTE:** In the Rocky Mountains, both Red Elderberry (red berries, domed cluster — toxic raw) and Blue Elderberry (dark blue-black berries, flat-topped cluster — edible when cooked) occur. **Always identify berry color and cluster shape before processing:** flat-topped cluster + dark blue/black berries = edible when cooked. Domed cluster + red berries = **do not eat raw.** Never eat any elderberry raw.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Rose
*Rosa woodsii* (Wood's Rose) and related species

![Wild Rose (Rosa carolina) — pink five-petaled flowers and oval leaflets](images/native-plants/rosa-carolina.jpg)

**Identification**
Shrubs with thorny or prickly canes, alternate compound leaves with 5–9 oval toothed leaflets, and classic five-petaled pink (occasionally white) single rose flowers. The red to orange-red fruit (rose hip) is a swollen receptacle containing seeds surrounded by fleshy tissue. Hips develop in late summer and persist through winter.

**Where to Find It**
Open areas, roadsides, stream banks, forest edges, meadows, and thickets throughout the Rocky Mountains at all elevations. *Rosa woodsii* (Wood's Rose) is the dominant wild rose of the region — very common from foothills to subalpine zones. Abundant rose hips persist through winter and are an essential vitamin C source in a landscape with few winter fruits.

**Season**
Flowers: June–August (elevation dependent). Rose hips: August through winter (best after first frost sweetens them).

**Edible Parts**
Petals (fresh), rose hips (processed), and young shoots in spring (peeled).

**Preparation**
Petals: fresh in salads, infused in syrups, dried for tea. Rose hips: cut off the blossom end and stem, slice in half, scrape out the seeds and fine hairs (both irritating), then use the flesh. Rose hip tea: dry hips and steep in hot water — extremely high in vitamin C. Rose hip jam, syrup, cordial.

**Safety**
Thorns are a physical hazard. The seeds and fine hairs inside the hip are irritating — always remove before consuming. No toxic lookalikes for the classic rose flower-and-hip combination.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Gooseberry and Currant
*Ribes* spp. (*R. inerme*, *R. cereum*, *R. viscosissimum*, *R. aureum*)

![Wax Currant (Ribes cereum) — small red berries on low shrub](images/native-plants/ribes-cereum.jpg)

**Identification**
Small shrubs (2–5 feet) with alternately arranged, maple-like lobed leaves. Gooseberries have thorns at nodes; currants are typically thornless. Small, round berries (¼–½ inch) ripen red, black, yellow, or orange-red depending on species. All have a characteristic five-pointed star at the blossom end.

**Where to Find It**
Mountain slopes, aspen groves, stream banks, forest edges throughout the Rockies.

**Season**
Berries ripen July–August depending on elevation.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries.

**Preparation**
Fresh eating (quality varies by species). Jam (excellent — gooseberry and currant jam are traditional). Jelly. Cordial. Pie. Some species are more palatable than others.


**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the characteristic star at berry end and maple-like lobed leaves. All North American *Ribes* species are edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Kinnikinnick / Bearberry
*Arctostaphylos uva-ursi*

![Kinnikinnick (Arctostaphylos uva-ursi) — trailing evergreen with bright red berries](images/native-plants/arctostaphylos-uva-ursi.jpg)

**Identification**
A trailing, mat-forming evergreen shrub (2–6 inches tall) with small, thick, spatula-shaped glossy leaves. Small pink urn-shaped flowers in spring. Bright red berries (¼ inch) persist through winter. Grows in dense mats covering the ground in dry, open forest.

**Where to Find It**
Dry, acidic, rocky or sandy soil under conifers throughout the Rockies. Very common at all elevations.

**Season**
Berries: August through winter. Most palatable after frost.

**Edible Parts**
Berries — mealy, mild, dry.

**Preparation**
Fresh (mealy, starchy, not sweet — best raw in small amounts or dried). Dried and ground into flour for mixing into bread or mush (traditional). Cooked in soups. Not a highly palatable fresh berry but nutritionally valuable and widely available as a year-round food in the mountains.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive trailing mat habit, glossy spatula-shaped leaves, and red berries in dry conifer forest.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Buffalo Berry
*Shepherdia canadensis* (Canada Buffaloberry) and *S. argentea*

![Buffalo Berry (Shepherdia argentea) — silvery-scaled leaves and red-orange berries](images/native-plants/shepherdia-argentea.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub (2–8 feet) with **distinctive silver-gray color** — opposite leaves densely covered in silver-gray scales giving the plant a frosted, silvery appearance visible from a distance. *S. canadensis* (the Rockies species) is typically less thorny than the Plains *S. argentea*, but both have the unmistakable silver foliage. Small yellowish flowers in early spring. Small red to orange-red berries (¼ inch) in clusters in late summer.

**Where to Find It**
Mountain slopes, open conifer forest, stream banks, and rocky terrain throughout the Rocky Mountains. *S. canadensis* is the dominant species from the northern Rockies through the subalpine zone — it tolerates poor soil and exposed, windswept sites where few other shrubs fruit. Common throughout Montana, Idaho, Wyoming, and Colorado.

**Season**
Berries ripen late July–September. Best after first frost, which reduces astringency.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries.

**Preparation**
Fresh (tart — better after frost). Traditional "Indian ice cream": beat berries vigorously with a small amount of fat or water — saponins in the berries cause the mixture to foam into a frothy, mousse-like dessert (a traditional food across many Rocky Mountain and Plains nations). Jam. Cordial. High in vitamin C.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes — the silver leaf color is unmistakable. Do not eat the raw berries in very large quantities (saponins cause digestive upset in excess).

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mountain Ash
*Sorbus scopulina* (Western Mountain Ash)

![Mountain Ash (Sorbus scopulina) — large clusters of bright red-orange berries and pinnate leaves](images/native-plants/sorbus-scopulina.jpg)

**Identification**
A shrub or small tree (6–15 feet) with pinnately compound leaves (11–15 toothed leaflets) and flat-topped clusters of tiny white flowers in spring. The fruit clusters are stunning in fall — dense, flat-topped clusters of small round berries ripening from green to brilliant orange-red.

**Where to Find It**
Mountain slopes, forest edges, stream banks, subalpine zones throughout the Rockies.

**Season**
Berries ripen August–September. Best after first frost.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries — always cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw berries are very bitter and mildly toxic (contain parasorbic acid that converts to sorbic acid with cooking or frost). Jelly, jam, and sauce after cooking. Very high in vitamin C.

**Safety**
Do not eat raw in large quantities. Cooking or thorough drying eliminates the bitter compound. No dangerous lookalikes for the combination of pinnate leaves and orange-red berry clusters.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pinon Pine / Two-Needle Piñon
*Pinus edulis*

![Pinon Pine (Pinus edulis) — paired needles and small round cones with large edible seeds](images/native-plants/pinus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
Short, dense, multi-branched pine tree (10–30 feet) of the piñon-juniper woodlands. **Needles grow in bundles of exactly two**, 1–2 inches long, stiff and dark green. Cones are small (1–2 inches), nearly round, producing large oval seeds — the pine nuts.

**Where to Find It**
Piñon-juniper woodland at 4,000–8,000 feet elevation — the characteristic two-tree ecosystem of the Colorado Plateau and southern Rockies.

**Season**
Cones mature in fall (September–October). Harvest when cones are partially open and seeds release easily. Heavy cone years alternate with lean years.

**Edible Parts**
Seeds (pine nuts) — calorie-dense and highly nutritious.

**Preparation**
Collect cones from tree or ground when open. Shake or press to release seeds. Roast briefly in a dry pan or oven — intensifies flavor dramatically. Eat as trail food, add to salads, grind into paste.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes — all pine nuts are edible though size and flavor vary.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Gambel Oak
*Quercus gambelii*

![Gambel Oak (Quercus gambelii) — deeply lobed rounded leaves and round acorns](images/native-plants/quercus-gambelii.jpg)

**Identification**
The dominant oak of the southern Rocky Mountains — a small tree or large shrub (6–25 feet) with deeply lobed leaves, the lobes **rounded** (not pointed). Acorns oval, ¾–1 inch, in shallow caps. Bark gray. Often forms dense thickets in foothills.

**Where to Find It**
Foothills and lower mountain slopes, 5,000–8,500 feet elevation. Often forms dense thickets. Southern Rockies from Colorado to Arizona.

**Season**
Acorns ripen and fall September–October.

**Edible Parts**
Acorn nutmeat — after leaching tannins.

**Preparation**
Shell and chop acorns. Leach tannins with cold water (change water 2–3 times daily, 3–7 days) or hot water (boil, change water 3–4 times, 1–2 hours). Taste for bitterness — keep leaching until mildly nutty. Dry and grind into flour. Substitute 25–50% for wheat flour in bread and baked goods. Gambel Oak is relatively low-tannin — requires less leaching than most eastern oaks.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes once the acorn form is confirmed. Different tannin levels between species require taste-testing to determine completion of leaching.

---

## FUNGI

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chanterelle
*Cantharellus cibarius* and *C. subalbidus*

![Chanterelle (Cantharellus cibarius) — golden mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-cibarius.jpg)

**Identification**
Golden-yellow to egg-yolk orange mushrooms with a wavy, irregular cap margin, often funnel-shaped with age. The critical feature: **false gills** — beneath the cap are blunt, forking ridges (not thin knife-like gills) that run partway down the stem. These ridges are thick, widely spaced, and fork repeatedly. Flesh is solid white. Fruity, apricot-like smell.

**Where to Find It**
Mixed conifer and aspen forests throughout the Rocky Mountains. Grows from soil (not wood), solitary or scattered. Most common under Engelmann spruce, fir, and aspen at 7,000–10,000 feet elevation. Peak season August–October after monsoon or fall rains.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October). Most productive after rain followed by warm weather.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously — absorbs water and becomes mushy. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. The flavor is fruity, slightly peppery, excellent in cream sauces and pasta.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus illudens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at the base of trees or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), and gills glow faintly in the dark. Causes severe vomiting and diarrhea. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel
*Morchella* spp. (*M. esculenta*, *M. elata*, *M. importuna*)

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — this is the single most important identification criterion. Cut vertically: the cap and stem form one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base. Cap color: tan to gray to near-black depending on species.

**Where to Find It**
Rocky Mountain morels appear in two main contexts: spring (April–June) in valley-bottom riparian areas under cottonwood, willow, and aspen; and summer–fall in recently burned conifer forest (post-fire morels). Post-fire morels in the Rockies are legendary — a year after a significant wildfire, the burned area often produces massive morel flushes that attract commercial pickers from across the region.

**Season**
Spring valley morels: April–June (elevation dependent). Post-fire morels: summer through fall, the year following a wildfire.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. Slice vertically and rinse to clean the hollow cavity. Add to cream sauces, eggs, risotto, pasta. Dry for year-round storage.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* and *Verpa* species) — POISONOUS, some potentially FATAL. Contains gyromitrin, a compound metabolized to a toxic hydrazine derivative. False morels: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap attaches only at the top (not at the base), surface is wrinkled or brain-like (not regular honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### King Bolete / Porcini
*Boletus edulis* and related species

![King Bolete / Porcini (Boletus edulis) — brown cap and thick white stem with pore surface](images/native-plants/boletus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, robust mushroom with a brown cap (2–8 inches across), creamy to brownish pore surface underneath (no gills — tiny tubes opening as pores), and a thick, club-shaped stem with a fine white net pattern near the top (reticulation). The flesh is white to pale yellow and does not change color when cut. Smells pleasant, earthy, nutty.

**Where to Find It**
Under spruce, fir, and pine at 7,000–11,000 feet elevation throughout the Rocky Mountains. The combination of high-elevation conifer forests and late-summer monsoon rains makes the Rockies one of North America's premier king bolete regions. Most abundant in August–September following monsoon moisture. Can be found through October at appropriate elevations.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (August–October), peaking after monsoon rains.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter, add to pasta, risotto, soups. Dry for storage — drying intensifies the flavor dramatically into the classic porcini aroma. One of the world's premier edible fungi.

**Safety**
**Avoid any bolete with a red or orange pore surface** — some are toxic. Avoid any bolete whose flesh turns blue rapidly when cut — some blue-staining boletes are toxic. The King Bolete has white to tan pores and white non-bluing flesh. When in doubt about any bolete, do not eat it.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hedgehog Mushroom
*Hydnum repandum*

![Hedgehog Mushroom (Hydnum repandum) — pale tan cap with distinctive spines (teeth) underneath](images/native-plants/hydnum-repandum.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the safest edible mushrooms for beginners because of its completely unique feature: instead of gills or pores underneath the cap, the hedgehog mushroom has **brittle, downward-pointing teeth (spines)** covering the underside. Cap is pale tan to cream, 1–4 inches, irregular and wavy. Stem central, same color. The teeth are the instant ID.

**Where to Find It**
Conifer forest throughout the Rocky Mountains, typically under spruce, fir, and pine at mid-to-high elevations (6,000–10,000 feet). Appears in fall after moisture events, often in the same spots year after year.

**Season**
Fall (August–October).

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter — mild, nutty flavor, excellent texture. The teeth may need to be rubbed off if very large. One of the best beginner mushrooms because the teeth make it unmistakable.

**Safety**
The teeth underneath are completely unique — there is no toxic mushroom with teeth instead of gills or pores in the Rockies. A beginner-safe choice.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Mountain meadows, open parks, forest clearings, and disturbed ground throughout the Rocky Mountains. Some of the largest specimens in North America occur in high-elevation mountain meadows (8,000–10,000 feet). Appears in late summer following monsoon rains.

**Season**
Late summer through fall (July–October). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white. Once any yellow, purple, or green coloring develops inside, it is no longer safe to eat.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which means it might be a deadly *Amanita* button — also round and white but with developing structure inside), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating. Other edible puffballs including smaller species are also found in Rocky Mountain meadows — all require the same pure-white interior test.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Coral Fungi
*Ramaria* spp.

**Identification**
Coral-like fruiting bodies rising from the ground or from decaying wood, branching repeatedly into club-like tips. Colors range from white to cream to yellow to bright orange. The branching structure resembling a sea coral makes identification straightforward at the genus level.

**Where to Find It**
Under conifers in moist forest throughout the Rockies and beyond.

**Season**
Late summer through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Fresh, young, firm fruiting bodies.

**Preparation**
Sauté, add to soups. The flavor is mild and pleasant.

**Safety**
Several *Ramaria* species cause gastrointestinal upset. The safest approach: harvest only **white to cream-colored coral fungi** — yellow and orange species have a higher rate of causing reactions. Eat small amounts first. Avoid any that taste bitter raw. The yellow-tipped *R. formosa* is known to cause problems.

---

---

# REGION 8: CALIFORNIA
*Central Valley, Southern California, Sierra Nevada foothills, Central and North Coast*

California's botanical richness is unmatched in the continental United States — over 5,000 plant species, one-third of all US species, in a single state. The foraging calendar here runs counter to the rest of the country: the most productive seasons are late fall, winter, and spring, when Mediterranean-climate rains bring growth. Summer heat shuts down most annual plants.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Miner's Lettuce
*Claytonia perfoliata*

![Miner's Lettuce (Claytonia perfoliata) — distinctive round leaf with stem growing through center](images/native-plants/claytonia-perfoliata.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the most distinctive plants in this guide. Adult leaves are unmistakable: two stem leaves fuse together forming a single round or diamond-shaped platform with the flower stem passing directly through the center — like a stake through a coin. No other plant looks like this. Tiny white flowers emerge from the center of the round leaf.

**Where to Find It**
California is the heartland of miner's lettuce — abundant in coastal hills, oak woodland understory, stream banks, north-facing canyon slopes, and shaded disturbed areas throughout the state. Thrives in the wet season (November through April) and disappears entirely with summer heat. Used by California Gold Rush miners to prevent scurvy — hence the name.

**Season**
Winter through spring (November–May). Best in cool wet conditions. Peak December–March.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant above ground — raw or cooked.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — one of the mildest, most pleasant-tasting wild greens available. The round coin-shaped leaves are visually striking in presentations. Wilted briefly in olive oil. One of the most beginner-friendly wild greens: unmistakable identification, mild flavor, abundant availability.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes — the round leaf-around-stem appearance is impossible to confuse.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Mustard
*Brassica nigra* (Black Mustard) and *B. rapa* (Field Mustard)

![Black Mustard (Brassica nigra) — tall branching stems with yellow four-petaled flowers and small pods](images/native-plants/brassica-nigra.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall, branching, coarse-stemmed annuals (3–6 feet) covered in four-petaled, bright yellow flowers in spring. Leaves are deeply lobed and rough-textured at the base, becoming smaller and clasping (wrapping the stem) higher up. Seed pods are small, cylindrical, pressed against the stem. The characteristic mustard smell when leaves are crushed is diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Fields, roadsides, disturbed hillsides, burned areas, dry grassland. Common in coastal California and the Central Valley. Often colors entire hillsides yellow in spring.

**Season**
Young leaves: winter–early spring. Flowers: spring. Seeds: late spring–summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots (cooked greens), flowers (raw garnish), seeds (ground as mustard condiment).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: steam or sauté — pungent, spicy, excellent with garlic and olive oil. The flavor mellows with cooking. Flowers as garnish. Seeds: crush and mix with vinegar for a homemade mustard.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the four-petaled yellow mustard flower and the characteristic mustard smell. All *Brassica* species are edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cat's Ear
*Hypochaeris radicata*

![Cat's Ear (Hypochaeris radicata) — dandelion-like yellow flowers on branching stems](images/native-plants/hypochaeris-radicata.jpg)

**Identification**
Often confused with dandelion — a rosette plant with yellow composite flowers. Distinguish from dandelion by: branching flower stems (dandelion has single, unbranched stems per flower), leaves with rounded hairy lobes (dandelion leaves are more deeply and sharply cut), and slightly hairy leaf surfaces (dandelion leaves are smooth).

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, disturbed areas. A naturalized European plant now very common in California.

**Season**
Year-round in coastal California. Peak spring.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (raw or cooked), flowers (raw), roots (roasted coffee substitute — same as dandelion).

**Preparation / Safety**
Identical to dandelion. No toxic lookalikes — no dangerous plant resembles the dandelion/cat's ear type.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Sow Thistle
*Sonchus oleraceus* (Common Sow Thistle) and *S. asper* (Spiny Sow Thistle)

![Sow Thistle (Sonchus oleraceus) — clasping lobed leaves and yellow dandelion-like flowers](images/native-plants/sonchus-oleraceus.jpg)

**Identification**
Upright plants (1–4 feet) with lobed, toothed leaves that clasp the stem with ear-like bases (auricles). Yellow dandelion-like flower heads at the branch tips. Milky sap when broken. *S. oleraceus* has softer leaf spines; *S. asper* has sharper spines but both are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed areas, gardens, roadsides, agricultural fields. Common throughout California year-round.

**Season**
Young growth: winter–spring. Available year-round in mild coastal areas.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads (mild, slightly bitter). Cooked like spinach. Blanching reduces any bitterness. Traditional in Mediterranean and Pacific Island cuisines.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes among plants with milky sap and clasping auricle leaves bearing dandelion-like yellow flowers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Radish
*Raphanus sativus*

![Wild Radish (Raphanus sativus) — white to purple four-petaled flowers and elongated seed pods](images/native-plants/raphanus-sativus.jpg)

**Identification**
A coarse-stemmed annual (1–4 feet) with deeply pinnately lobed lower leaves (a large terminal lobe and smaller side lobes) and smaller clasping upper leaves. Four-petaled flowers ranging from white to pale yellow to pale purple with dark veining. Seed pods are distinctive: swollen between seeds, not splitting (unlike mustard), becoming corky and spongy when dry.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, fields, disturbed areas, coastal bluffs. Very common throughout California.

**Season**
Young growth: winter–spring. Young pods: spring–early summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked), flowers (raw garnish), young green seed pods (raw like radish pods), roots of first-year plants (small, peppery radish flavor).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: steam, sauté — pungent, radish-like. Young pods: raw, crunchy, spicy — eat like raw radish. Root: peel and eat raw or pickled if large enough and not too fibrous.

**Safety**
The four-petaled mustard-type flower and lobed radish-like leaves are diagnostic. No dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Mallow
*Malva neglecta* and *M. parviflora* (Small-flowered Mallow)

![Common Mallow (Malva neglecta) — round scalloped leaves and small pink-white flowers](images/native-plants/malva-neglecta.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing plants with round, shallowly lobed, scalloped-edged leaves on long petioles. Small five-petaled flowers (pale pink to white with darker veins). The fruit is a round, segmented disc ("cheese wheel" shape) that separates into wedge-shaped sections. The plant has a slightly mucilaginous quality.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed areas, gardens, roadsides, waste ground. Common throughout California.

**Season**
Winter–spring primarily. Available year-round in mild areas.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, flowers, young fruits ("cheeses"), shoots.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads (mild, slightly mucilaginous). Cooked as greens — used in many traditional cuisines as a pot herb. Young fruits (the round discs) eaten raw as a trail snack — mild, chewy, slightly nutty. The mucilaginous quality thickens soups.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the round scalloped leaves and cheese-wheel fruit.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Stinging Nettle
*Urtica dioica*

![Stinging Nettle (Urtica dioica) — serrated leaves with hollow stinging hairs](images/native-plants/urtica-dioica.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 2–5 feet tall with opposite, coarsely toothed, pointed leaves on a square stem. Identification is immediate and painful: hollow stinging hairs covering all surfaces inject formic acid and histamine on contact. Drooping clusters of inconspicuous green flowers appear from leaf axils.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, moist disturbed areas, forest edges, coastal canyons. Common throughout California, especially in riparian zones and shaded moist areas. Available from fall through spring in coastal California — opposite timing from the Northeast.

**Season**
Young shoots and top leaves: fall through spring (October–April in coastal California). Harvest before flowering.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and tender leaves only, before flowering.

**Preparation**
Wear thick gloves to harvest. Cooking for 5 minutes (boiling, steaming, or blanching) completely neutralizes the sting. Rich flavor, similar to spinach but earthier. Nettle soup, pasta, omelets, tea.

**Safety**
**Always cook before eating** — raw nettles sting the mouth and throat. Use gloves when handling fresh plants. No toxic lookalikes once stinging hairs are confirmed.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — basal rosette and flower](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Basal rosette of deeply lobed, toothed leaves arising from a central crown at ground level — no stem leaves. A single hollow leafless stem from the crown bears one yellow flower head, which matures into the spherical seed ball. White milky latex bleeds from any broken stem or leaf.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, fields, disturbed areas. Virtually everywhere in California year-round, especially in coastal areas and the Central Valley. Young spring leaves are least bitter.

**Season**
Year-round in coastal California. Peak fall–spring. Flowers spring through fall.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: leaves, flowers, roots, flower buds.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads with vinaigrette. Older leaves: blanch 2 minutes, sauté with garlic and olive oil. Flowers in cordial or wine. Roots roasted as coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest and most identifiable plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Forms dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small, opposite, oval, pointed, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 petals each so deeply notched they appear to be 10. The single most reliable feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, under shrubs, disturbed areas, coastal hills. Very abundant in California during the wet season — often forms large, lush mats from November through April in shaded, moist spots.

**Season**
Fall through spring (October–April). Dies back in summer heat. Most tender and abundant in winter–early spring.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy flavor. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green combined with more flavorful foraged greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. The flavor is distinctly peppery. Found growing directly in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Clear springs, spring-fed streams, cold running water throughout California. More widespread in the wet season. **Harvest only from verified clean water sources** — upstream of farms, roads, and septic systems.

**Season**
Year-round where springs flow. Best in cool months (fall through spring).

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, or as garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. The peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates the parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched and upright. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped with wavy margins. The diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides, compost piles. Very common throughout California. Most productive during the cooler months of the growing season.

**Season**
Spring through early summer in cooler areas. Year-round in mild coastal California. Young shoot tips are best before the plant becomes large.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves, shoot tips, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Substitute for cooked spinach in any recipe.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating, do not eat in very large quantities daily. Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, or kidney disease.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips. The entire plant has a slightly mucilaginous quality when cut.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, dry, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, driveways, fields. Very common throughout California, particularly in the Central Valley and warm inland areas. Thrives in summer heat.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Peaks in hot weather. Year-round in very mild areas.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (mucilaginous quality thickens dressings). Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. A mainstream salad ingredient in Mediterranean and Middle Eastern cuisines. Exceptional in omega-3 fatty acid content for a land plant.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants worldwide.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis californica* and *O. stricta*

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped leaflets and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Small, low-growing plant with three heart-shaped leaflets arranged like a clover, each with a distinct fold down the center. Immediately distinguished from clover by heart-shaped (not oval) leaflets. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers. The entire plant has a distinctive sharp, lemony-sour taste from oxalic acid — tasting a small leaf is a reliable identification aid.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, gardens, disturbed ground, woodland shade, roadsides. Very common throughout California year-round, especially in coastal areas and shaded spots.

**Season**
Year-round in California's mild climate. Most abundant and tender fall through spring.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods. Use as seasoning or snack, not as a primary food source.

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail snack or salad ingredient. The sour flavor functions as a lemon substitute — add to salads, grain dishes, cold drinks. Brew in water for a tart wild "lemonade."

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid. Safe in moderate amounts. **Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, arthritis, or kidney disease.** Do not eat large amounts daily.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria californica* (California Strawberry) and *F. virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners spreading along the ground, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated. Leaves have prominently toothed edges.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal bluffs, grasslands, forest edges, shaded slopes throughout California. *F. californica* is the native California species — slightly smaller berries but equally intense in flavor. Coastal populations may fruit as early as February–April.

**Season**
Flowers in late winter–spring (February–April in coastal California). Fruit ripens March–June depending on elevation and exposure.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, made into jam, baked goods, or dried. The flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
**Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*)** — the small red berry on a similar-leafed plant is insipid but NOT toxic. Distinguished by its upward-facing yellow flowers (wild strawberry has white flowers). No dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Fennel
*Foeniculum vulgare*

![Fennel (Foeniculum vulgare) — feathery green fronds and flat-topped yellow flower umbel](images/native-plants/foeniculum-vulgare.jpg)

**Identification**
A tall (4–8 feet), highly aromatic plant with feathery, finely divided leaves (resembling a cloud of fine green needles) and flat-topped yellow flower umbels. The smell when any part is crushed is unmistakably anise/licorice. Hollow stems. Widely naturalized throughout California.

**Where to Find It**
Roadsides, vacant lots, coastal bluffs, disturbed areas, creek banks. Very common throughout California, especially coastal areas.

**Season**
Young fronds: fall through spring. Pollen: summer. Seeds: late summer.

**Edible Parts**
Young fronds and shoot tips, flower pollen, seeds.

**Preparation**
Fronds: raw in salads (anise flavor), used as a culinary herb. Pollen: shake into a bag as a precious culinary spice — extraordinary floral anise flavor. Seeds: dried as a spice.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** Fennel is in the Apiaceae (carrot family), which contains deadly species including Poison Hemlock (*Conium maculatum*). The anise smell is the distinguishing feature — Poison Hemlock smells unpleasant and "mousy," not like anise. Young hemlock can superficially resemble young fennel. **Confirm the distinctive licorice/anise smell before using.** Poison hemlock stems typically have purple spots. When in doubt, do not eat.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### California Black Oak
*Quercus kelloggii*

![California Black Oak (Quercus kelloggii) — deeply lobed pointed leaves and elongated acorns](images/native-plants/quercus-kelloggii.jpg)

**Identification**
A large deciduous tree (30–80 feet) with deeply and sharply lobed leaves — the lobes have pointed tips with bristles, distinguishing it from white oaks with rounded lobes. Bark is dark gray to nearly black on older trees. Acorns oval to oblong, ¾–1¼ inches, in deep thick cups. The most prized acorn species in California for food.

**Where to Find It**
Mountain slopes, foothills, mixed woodland at 1,000–6,000 feet elevation throughout California.

**Season**
Acorns ripen and fall September–November.

**Edible Parts**
Acorn nutmeat after leaching.

**Preparation**
Same leaching process as Gambel Oak (see Rocky Mountains entry). California Black Oak has higher tannin content than Gambel Oak — may require longer leaching (5–10 days cold water). Taste frequently. The resulting flour makes excellent pancakes, bread, and porridge. **Historically the most important caloric staple for most California tribal groups.**

**Safety**
Do not eat unleached acorns — tannins are astringent and interfere with nutrient absorption.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Valley Oak
*Quercus lobata*

![Valley Oak (Quercus lobata) — massive spreading canopy and large elongated acorns](images/native-plants/quercus-lobata.jpg)

**Identification**
A massive, spreading oak (60–100+ feet) with deeply lobed leaves with **rounded lobes** (white oak group — lower tannin). Acorns are elongated and narrow, 1–2 inches, one of the largest acorns of any California oak. Bark is light gray, checkered. The quintessential oak of California's Central Valley and foothills.

**Season / Preparation / Safety:** Same as California Black Oak. Valley Oak acorns have lower tannin content and require less leaching (2–5 days cold water). The large acorns make processing more efficient.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### California Bay Laurel
*Umbellularia californica*

![California Bay Laurel (Umbellularia californica) — lance-shaped aromatic leaves and olive-like fruit](images/native-plants/umbellularia-californica.jpg)

**Identification**
An evergreen tree or large shrub (20–75 feet) with narrow lance-shaped leaves 2–5 inches long. The smell when any leaf is crushed is immediately striking — intensely aromatic, combining eucalyptus, camphor, and bay laurel, far stronger than culinary bay. This smell is unmistakable and diagnostic. Fruit: plum-like, 1 inch, ripening green to purple.

**Where to Find It**
Moist canyon bottoms, stream sides, mixed woodland, coastal hills. Northern and central California primarily.

**Season**
Leaves available year-round. Nuts ripen fall (September–November).

**Edible Parts**
Leaves (culinary seasoning), roasted nuts (seeds inside the fruit).

**Preparation**
Leaves: dry for 1–2 weeks before use (fresh leaves can be overwhelming). Use at half the quantity of commercial bay. Excellent in slow-cooked beans, stews, soups. Nuts: roast whole in coals or oven at 350°F until skin turns black (30–45 minutes). Cool, crack shell, eat roasted kernel — rich, slightly bitter, reminiscent of coffee.

**Safety**
Prolonged inhalation of crushed fresh leaves may cause headache or sinus irritation in some people — handle in ventilated areas. Safe as a cooked seasoning in normal amounts.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Toyon / Christmas Berry
*Heteromeles arbutifolia*

![Toyon (Heteromeles arbutifolia) — bright red berries in clusters and dark green toothed leaves](images/native-plants/heteromeles-arbutifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
A large native shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with thick, leathery, dark green leaves with sharply toothed edges. Flat-topped clusters of small white flowers in summer. The bright red berries (¼ inch) in dense clusters ripen in fall and winter — one of the most visually spectacular native plants when bearing fruit. Toyon is considered the inspiration for naming Hollywood ("holly wood").

**Where to Find It**
Coastal sage scrub, chaparral, oak woodland throughout California.

**Season**
Berries ripen November–January.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries — after cooking or extensive drying.

**Preparation**
Raw berries are very astringent and mildly toxic (contain cyanogenic compounds). Traditional preparation: roast, bake, or boil berries (cooking removes the cyanogenic compounds), or dry for several weeks until sweet. The cooked/dried berries have a mild, slightly bitter-sweet flavor.

**Safety**
Do not eat raw berries in quantity. Cooking or thorough drying is required.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pacific Madrone
*Arbutus menziesii*

![Pacific Madrone (Arbutus menziesii) — distinctive smooth orange-red peeling bark and red berries](images/native-plants/arbutus-menziesii.jpg)

**Identification**
A distinctive evergreen tree (20–80 feet) with smooth, peeling, orange-to-red bark that is perhaps the most visually striking bark of any Pacific Coast tree — it peels in thin layers revealing fresh orange-red underneath. Thick, oval, leathery, dark green leaves. Clusters of small urn-shaped white flowers in spring. Red to orange-red berries (¼–⅓ inch), bumpy-surfaced, in clusters in fall.

**Where to Find It**
Dry, rocky forest edges, coastal mountains, mixed evergreen forest. Pacific Coast from British Columbia to Baja.

**Season**
Berries ripen October–December.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries — mealy, mild.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw (mealy, mildly sweet, not intensely flavored). Dry for trail mix. Cook in preparations for flavor development. Not typically a high-volume food but notable as a nutritious winter fruit.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive orange-peeling bark and red berry clusters.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### California Wild Grape
*Vitis californica*

![California Wild Grape (Vitis californica) — round lobed leaves and blue-black grape clusters](images/native-plants/vitis-californica.jpg)

**Identification**
A native grape vine with heart-shaped, shallowly lobed, alternate leaves and the characteristic tendrils and bark of all *Vitis* species. Small, dark blue to nearly black berries in clusters in late summer. The berries are small (¼–½ inch) and tart — they lack the sweetness of eastern wild grapes.

**Where to Find It**
Stream banks, canyon bottoms, riparian woodland throughout California.

**Season**
Fruit ripens August–October.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit. Young spring leaves (edible as wraps). Tendrils (raw as trail nibble).

**Preparation**
Fresh eating (small, tart). Juice. Jelly. Wine. Young leaves: blanch 5 minutes, fill with seasoned rice or meat, roll, steam.

**Safety**
**Canada Moonseed (*Menispermum canadense*)** — TOXIC — has similar leaf shape and berry clusters but has a single crescent-shaped seed (grape seeds are round). Moonseed has no tendrils (grapes do). Always check for tendrils.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus nigra* ssp. *caerulea* (Blue Elderberry)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–15 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets per leaf, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). In early summer, large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark blue to nearly black spheres in heavy drooping flat-topped clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Common throughout California in riparian areas, stream banks, moist canyons, disturbed moist ground. The Blue Elderberry (*S. nigra* ssp. *caerulea*) is the California species — berries are dark blue to nearly black with a white powdery bloom, in flat-topped clusters. Found from coastal areas through the Sierra Nevada foothills.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Berries: August–October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems, steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Berries: **always cook.** Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** Never eat elderberries raw — all elderberries contain cyanogenic glycosides that cause nausea and vomiting raw; cooking destroys them. California's Blue Elder is edible when cooked. Distinguish from Red Elderberry (*S. racemosa*) — red berries in a domed (not flat-topped) cluster, toxic raw. **Blue/black + flat-topped cluster = edible when cooked. Red + domed cluster = do not eat raw.**

---

## FUNGI

California's Mediterranean climate produces a winter-spring mushroom season unlike anywhere else.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### California Chanterelle
*Cantharellus californicus*

![California Chanterelle (Cantharellus californicus) — large golden mushroom with blunt ridges under oak](images/native-plants/cantharellus-californicus.jpg)

**Identification**
The largest chanterelle in North America — caps can reach 12 inches across. Same identification features as other chanterelles: golden-yellow cap, wavy margin, blunt forking ridges underneath, solid white interior, fruity smell. Grows from soil under oaks, not from wood.

**Where to Find It**
Under coastal and foothill oaks (Valley Oak, Blue Oak, Coast Live Oak) in the Coast Ranges and Sierra Nevada foothills.

**Season**
Winter through early spring (November–March) following fall and winter rains.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. The flavor is fruity and slightly peppery — the California Chanterelle's large size makes it especially productive. Excellent in cream sauces, pasta, and risotto.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus olivascens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at the base of oaks or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), gills glow faintly in the dark. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### King Bolete / Porcini
*Boletus edulis* and *B. aereus*

![King Bolete / Porcini (Boletus edulis) — brown cap and thick white stem with pore surface](images/native-plants/boletus-edulis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, robust mushroom with a brown cap (2–8 inches across), creamy to brownish pore surface underneath (no gills — tiny tubes opening as pores), and a thick, club-shaped stem with a fine white net pattern near the top (reticulation). The flesh is white to pale yellow and does not change color when cut. Smells pleasant, earthy, nutty.

**Where to Find It**
Under pines and firs in the Sierra Nevada foothills and Coast Ranges throughout California. California's Mediterranean climate produces a fall-winter bolete season (October–December) triggered by the first fall rains after the dry summer — opposite timing from the monsoon-driven Rocky Mountain season. *B. aereus* (Bronze Bolete — darker brown, nearly black cap) is also found in California under oaks and is equally excellent.

**Season**
Fall through early winter (October–December), following the first rain events.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fresh mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter, add to pasta, risotto, soups. Dry for storage — drying intensifies the flavor dramatically into the classic porcini aroma.

**Safety**
**Avoid any bolete with a red or orange pore surface** — some are toxic. Avoid any bolete whose flesh turns blue rapidly when cut — some blue-staining boletes are toxic. The King Bolete has white to tan pores and white non-bluing flesh. When in doubt about any bolete, do not eat it.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Morel
*Morchella* spp. (*M. esculenta*, *M. elata*, *M. importuna*)

![Morel Mushroom (Morchella) — honeycomb-pitted cap, fully hollow interior](images/native-plants/morchella.jpg)

**Identification**
True morels are **hollow throughout** — this is the single most important identification criterion. Cut vertically: the cap and stem form one single continuous hollow chamber. The cap is covered in a network of **pits and ridges resembling a honeycomb** (not smooth, not wrinkled, not brain-like). The cap attaches to the stem at its base.

**Where to Find It**
California morels appear in spring (March–May) following winter rains, typically under oaks, cottonwood, and other hardwoods in the Coast Ranges and Sierra Nevada foothills. Post-fire morels in California are exceptionally productive — Sierra Nevada burns regularly produce dense morel flushes the spring following a wildfire. Some of the largest post-fire morel harvests in North America occur in California burns.

**Season**
Spring (March–May) following winter rains. Post-fire morels: the spring following a significant wildfire.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Must be cooked.

**Preparation**
**Must be cooked** — raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. Slice vertically and rinse to clean the hollow cavity. Dry for year-round storage.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE: FALSE MOREL** (*Gyromitra* and *Verpa* species) — POISONOUS, some potentially FATAL. False morels: interior is chambered or cottony (not fully hollow), cap attaches only at the top (not at the base), surface is wrinkled or brain-like (not regular honeycomb pits). **Rule: if not completely hollow with a true honeycomb-pitted cap attached at the base, do not eat it.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Hedgehog Mushroom
*Hydnum repandum*

![Hedgehog Mushroom (Hydnum repandum) — pale tan cap with distinctive spines (teeth) underneath](images/native-plants/hydnum-repandum.jpg)

**Identification**
One of the safest edible mushrooms for beginners because of its completely unique feature: instead of gills or pores underneath the cap, the hedgehog mushroom has **brittle, downward-pointing teeth (spines)** covering the underside. Cap is pale tan to cream, 1–4 inches, irregular and wavy. Stem central, same color. The teeth are the instant ID.

**Where to Find It**
Under oaks and conifers throughout California in fall and winter — common in the Coast Ranges and Sierra Nevada foothills. California's wet season (October–March) is the primary hedgehog season here, unlike the summer-fall timing in northern regions.

**Season**
Fall through winter (October–February).

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter — mild, nutty flavor, excellent texture. One of the best beginner mushrooms because the teeth make it unmistakable.

**Safety**
The teeth underneath are completely unique — there is no toxic mushroom with teeth instead of gills or pores in California. A beginner-safe choice.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Candy Cap Mushroom
*Lactarius rubidus*

**Identification**
A small to medium mushroom (1–3 inch cap) with a cinnamon-brown cap, white to pale gills, and a central stem. The defining feature: when dried, the mushroom produces an extraordinarily strong maple syrup or fenugreek smell — one of the most distinctive odors in the mushroom world. Fresh specimens have a less obvious smell; drying concentrates and transforms it dramatically.

**Where to Find It**
Under oaks and madrone in the Coast Ranges and Sierra Nevada foothills.

**Season**
Fall through early winter (October–January).

**Edible Parts / Preparation**
Primarily used dried, where the maple aroma is most pronounced. Dried candy caps ground into a powder are used to flavor ice cream, cookies, French toast, and desserts — the flavor resembles maple syrup.

**Safety**
Confirm under oak/madrone, the cinnamon-brown cap, and the maple smell when dried. Some *Lactarius* species are mildly toxic — this is a mushroom to learn in the field with an experienced guide before foraging solo.

---

---

# REGION 9: GULF COAST AND FLORIDA
*Coastal Texas, Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, Florida*

The Gulf Coast and Florida represent the subtropical fringe of the United States — the flora here transitions through Caribbean and tropical influences. The foraging calendar is inverted from the North: the most productive seasons are fall, winter, and early spring, when temperatures are mild. Summer heat and humidity challenge most foragers, but aquatic and coastal species remain available year-round.

---

## PLANTS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Sea Beans / Glasswort / Pickleweed
*Salicornia* spp.

![Sea Beans (Salicornia bigelovii) — jointed succulent stems growing in salt marsh](images/native-plants/salicornia-bigelovii.jpg)

**Identification**
Plants adapted to saltwater — appearing almost alien: succulent, jointed stems with no visible leaves (reduced to tiny scales at each joint), bright green in spring and summer, turning red-orange-purple in fall. The texture is completely succulent — each segment is water-filled. The taste is intensely salty from seawater concentration in tissues.

**Where to Find It**
Salt marshes, tidal flats, estuaries, coastal mudflats, mangrove edges. Exclusively in saline or brackish coastal habitats.

**Season**
Spring and early summer for most tender growth. Available year-round.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground stem.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (crisp, briny, salty — no added salt needed). Sautéed briefly in butter (do not cook long). Pickled in white wine vinegar. Blanched and added to seafood dishes. Paired classically with oysters, clams, and shrimp.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in salt marsh habitat. The specific saline coastal habitat is the distinguishing context.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Pickerelweed
*Pontederia cordata*

![Pickerelweed (Pontederia cordata) — arrow-shaped leaves and blue-violet flower spikes in wetland](images/native-plants/pontederia-cordata.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic perennial with large, arrow-shaped, glossy leaves (4–10 inches) rising on long petioles from water. Dense spikes of small violet-blue flowers. Grows in shallow water in dense colonies.

**Where to Find It**
Freshwater wetlands, ponds, slow streams, lake margins throughout the Gulf Coast and Florida.

**Season**
Young leaves and shoots: spring. Seeds: late summer–fall. Available year-round in Florida.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked greens), young shoots (cooked), seeds (raw or cooked — nutty flavor).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: steam or boil — mild, slightly mucilaginous greens. Young leaf stalks: eat like celery when very tender. Seeds: boil or roast as a grain substitute. Edible in a pinch as an aquatic survival food.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the arrow-shaped leaves, blue-violet flower spikes, and wetland habitat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Purslane
*Portulaca oleracea*

![Purslane (Portulaca oleracea) — succulent reddish stems and paddle-shaped leaves](images/native-plants/portulaca-oleracea.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, succulent annual that sprawls in flat mats. Stems thick, fleshy, often reddish-tinged. Leaves small, paddle-shaped, noticeably succulent. Tiny yellow five-petaled flowers at stem tips.

**Where to Find It**
Warm, sunny disturbed ground, gardens, roadsides, fields throughout the Gulf Coast. Thrives in heat and humidity — peaks in Gulf Coast summers when most other edible greens are unavailable. Abundant in Florida year-round.

**Season**
Summer through early fall. Year-round in Florida. One of the few plants that thrives in peak Gulf Coast summer heat.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — stems, leaves, and seeds.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads (mucilaginous). Cooked like spinach. Added to soups as a thickener. Exceptional omega-3 content for a land plant. A mainstream vegetable in Mediterranean and Middle Eastern cuisines.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Dandelion
*Taraxacum officinale*

![Dandelion (Taraxacum officinale) — basal rosette and flower](images/native-plants/taraxacum-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
Basal rosette of deeply lobed, toothed leaves from a central crown — no stem leaves. Single hollow leafless flower stem bearing one yellow head, maturing into the spherical seed ball. White milky latex from any broken stem or leaf.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, roadsides, fields, disturbed areas throughout the Gulf Coast. Most abundant and palatable in fall through spring when temperatures are cooler. Summer heat reduces quality.

**Season**
Fall through spring (best October–April). Available year-round but least palatable in peak summer heat.

**Edible Parts**
Entire plant: leaves, flowers, roots, flower buds.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads with vinaigrette (fat cuts bitterness). Older leaves: blanch 2 minutes, sauté with garlic and olive oil. Flowers in cordial or wine. Roots roasted as coffee substitute.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest and most identifiable plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chickweed
*Stellaria media*

![Chickweed (Stellaria media) — tiny white star flowers and single line of hairs on stem](images/native-plants/stellaria-media.jpg)

**Identification**
Forms dense, sprawling mats close to the ground. Leaves small, opposite, oval, smooth. Flowers tiny, white, 5 petals so deeply notched they appear to be 10. Diagnostic feature: **one line of fine hairs** running down one side of the stem, switching sides at each leaf node.

**Where to Find It**
Moist soil in gardens, under shrubs, disturbed areas throughout the Gulf Coast. Primarily a cool-season plant — most abundant fall through spring. May be found year-round in shaded, moist spots along the Gulf Coast.

**Season**
Fall through spring (October–April). Dies back in summer heat.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads — mild, slightly grassy. Wilted briefly in olive oil. Use as a mild base green.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes. One of the safest forage plants for beginners.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Lamb's Quarters / Wild Spinach
*Chenopodium album*

![Lamb's Quarters (Chenopodium album) — triangular leaves with white mealy coating](images/native-plants/chenopodium-album.jpg)

**Identification**
Grows 1–6 feet tall, well-branched. Leaves broadly triangular to diamond-shaped. Diagnostic feature: a **white, mealy coating** on the undersides of young leaves and growing tips — rub with a finger and it transfers as a dusty white powder.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, agricultural fields, gardens, roadsides throughout the Gulf Coast. Most productive in the cooler spring season; plants bolt quickly in summer heat.

**Season**
Spring (March–May) primarily. May persist year-round in mild coastal areas.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoot tips.

**Preparation**
Cook like spinach — steam, blanch, or sauté. Mild, slightly mineral flavor. Substitute for cooked spinach in any recipe.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid — cook before eating; do not eat in large quantities daily. Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, or kidney disease.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wood Sorrel
*Oxalis* spp.

![Wood Sorrel (Oxalis stricta) — heart-shaped leaflets and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/oxalis-stricta.jpg)

**Identification**
Small, low-growing plant with three heart-shaped leaflets arranged like a clover, each with a distinct fold. Distinguished from clover by heart-shaped (not oval) leaflets. Yellow five-petaled flowers. Lemony-sour taste from oxalic acid — tasting a small leaf is a reliable ID aid.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, gardens, sidewalk cracks, disturbed ground, woodland shade throughout the Gulf Coast year-round. Multiple native *Oxalis* species are common in Florida.

**Season**
Year-round in the Gulf Coast's mild climate.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, stems, flowers, and seed pods. Use as seasoning or snack, not as a primary food source.

**Preparation**
Raw as a trail snack or salad ingredient. The sour flavor functions as a lemon substitute.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid. **Avoid if you have kidney stones, gout, arthritis, or kidney disease.** Safe in moderate amounts.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Watercress
*Nasturtium officinale*

![Watercress (Nasturtium officinale) — compound leaves and white flowers in clear stream](images/native-plants/nasturtium-officinale.jpg)

**Identification**
An aquatic or semi-aquatic plant with dark green, pinnately compound leaves (3–9 rounded leaflets) floating or creeping on or near water. Small white four-petaled flowers. Distinctly peppery flavor. Found growing in or alongside cold, clear, moving water.

**Where to Find It**
Clear springs, spring-fed streams, cold running water throughout the Gulf Coast. Florida's extensive spring systems support year-round watercress growth. **Harvest only from verified clean water sources** — upstream of farms, roads, and septic systems.

**Season**
Year-round where springs flow. Best in cooler months.

**Edible Parts**
Entire above-ground plant — leaves, stems, and flowers.

**Preparation**
Raw in salads, sandwiches, garnish. Wilted briefly in hot dishes. Watercress soup. Peppery bite diminishes with cooking.

**Safety**
**Harvest only from clean water sources.** Watercress from contaminated water can harbor *Giardia* cysts and liver flukes (*Fasciola hepatica*). Never harvest downstream of farms with livestock. Cooking eliminates parasite risk.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cattail
*Typha latifolia* and *T. domingensis* (Southern Cattail)

![Cattail (Typha latifolia) — sword-like leaves and brown cylindrical seed spike in wetland](images/native-plants/typha-latifolia.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: flat, sword-like leaves up to 9 feet tall growing from wetland, topped by the distinctive brown cylindrical "hot dog on a stick" seed spike. *T. domingensis* (Southern Cattail) has a longer, more slender spike — common in Gulf Coast and Florida marshes. Both are fully edible.

**Where to Find It**
Freshwater wetlands, marshes, swamps, bayous, lake margins, pond edges throughout the Gulf Coast. *T. domingensis* is abundant in Florida and coastal marshes. Year-round presence makes cattail one of the most reliable wild foods in the region.

**Season**
Year-round harvest from Gulf Coast wetlands:
- **Winter–Spring:** Young shoots (peel to white core)
- **Spring–Early Summer:** Green female flower spikes
- **Summer:** Yellow pollen from male spike
- **Year-round:** Starchy rhizomes in mud

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots, green flower spikes, pollen, and rhizomes.

**Preparation**
Young shoots: peel to pale inner core; eat raw or steamed. Green flower spikes: boil 10 minutes; eat like corn on the cob. Pollen: shake into a bag; mix into pancakes or bread (25% flour replacement). Rhizomes: pound in water, settle starch, dry — produces high-starch flour.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes in wetland habitat. The "supermarket of the swamp."

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Lotus
*Nelumbo lutea*

![American Lotus (Nelumbo lutea) — large round floating leaves and yellow flowers](images/native-plants/nelumbo-lutea.jpg)

**Identification**
Large aquatic perennial with enormous round leaves (1–2 feet across) rising above the water surface on long petioles, shedding water with a remarkable surface coating. Large, pale yellow flowers 4–8 inches across in summer. Distinctive seed head: a flat-topped structure resembling a shower head with oval seeds embedded in holes. Rhizomes thick, chambered, buried in pond mud.

**Where to Find It**
Ponds, slow-moving rivers, lakes, bayous, and backwaters throughout the Gulf Coast. Very productive in Gulf Coast bayous and river backwaters — the warmth and shallow standing water provide ideal conditions. Common throughout Louisiana, Mississippi, Alabama, and Florida.

**Season**
Young leaves emerging: spring. Seeds in seed head: fall. Rhizomes: fall through winter. Available year-round in Florida.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves (cooked), seeds (raw or roasted), rhizomes (cooked), and young leaf stalks.

**Preparation**
Seeds: remove from seed head, shell, eat raw or roast. Rhizomes: dig from mud, wash, slice into rounds, sauté or add to soups — similar to lotus root used in Asian cuisine. Young leaves: steam as wraps or cooked greens.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive round leaves and yellow flowers. Wade carefully in pond mud.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Strawberry
*Fragaria virginiana*

![Wild Strawberry (Fragaria virginiana) — small red berries and three-leaflet leaves](images/native-plants/fragaria-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing perennial with three toothed leaflets per leaf, long runners, and white five-petaled flowers in spring. The fruit is a miniature strawberry — intensely flavored, smaller than cultivated.

**Where to Find It**
Open meadows, forest edges, disturbed areas throughout the northern Gulf Coast and Florida. Most common at higher elevations and in northern portions of the region. In the Deep South, found primarily in cooler months and at woodland edges.

**Season**
Flowers in spring (March–April). Fruit ripens April–May in the Gulf Coast — earlier than northern regions.

**Edible Parts**
Fruit (primary). Leaves (dried for tea).

**Preparation**
Berries eaten fresh, jam, baked goods, or dried. Flavor is far more intense than cultivated strawberries. Leaf tea: dry leaves and steep in hot water.

**Safety**
Mock Strawberry (*Potentilla indica*) is insipid but NOT toxic — distinguished by yellow flowers (wild strawberry has white). No dangerous lookalikes.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Spiderwort
*Tradescantia ohiensis* and related species

![Spiderwort (Tradescantia ohiensis) — blue-purple three-petaled flowers and strap-like leaves](images/native-plants/tradescantia-ohiensis.jpg)

**Identification**
An upright perennial (1–3 feet) with long, narrow, strap-like leaves and clusters of bright blue-to-violet three-petaled flowers at stem tips. The sap from broken stems is mucilaginous and forms strings — hence "spiderwort." Flowers open in the morning, close by midday.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, roadsides, open woodlands, meadows, disturbed areas throughout the Gulf Coast and Florida. Multiple native *Tradescantia* species are common in the region.

**Season**
Spring through summer. Most productive spring (March–May) and fall (October–November). May be found nearly year-round in mild Florida locations.

**Edible Parts**
Young shoots and leaves (cooked as greens). Flowers (raw as garnish).

**Preparation**
Young shoots and leaves: steam, sauté, or add to soups — mild, slightly mucilaginous texture. Flowers: raw in salads as a colorful, edible garnish.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive three-petaled blue-violet flower.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Amaranth
*Amaranthus* spp. (*A. hybridus*, *A. tuberculatus*, and related species)

![Amaranth (Amaranthus retroflexus) — dense seed heads and large dark green leaves on tall stem](images/native-plants/amaranthus-retroflexus.jpg)

**Identification**
Tall, coarse, upright plants (1–6 feet) with large, alternate, oval leaves. Dense, bristly seed heads at the top. Stems and leaf undersides often reddish-tinged. Young plants are soft and leafy; older plants coarser. All North American *Amaranthus* species are edible.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed soil, roadsides, agricultural fields, waste areas throughout the Gulf Coast. Thrives in the heat and humidity of Gulf Coast summers. Common throughout Texas, Louisiana, Mississippi, and Florida.

**Season**
Young leaves: spring through fall. Seeds: late summer through fall. Most productive in summer heat.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots (cooked greens), seeds (grain).

**Preparation**
Young leaves: boil, steam, or sauté — nutritious greens, mild and spinach-like. Seeds: shake seed heads into a bag, winnow chaff, cook as porridge, grind into flour, or pop like popcorn. One of the ancient grains of the Americas — highly nutritious.

**Safety**
Contains oxalic acid in leaves — cook before eating. All *Amaranthus* species are edible.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Common Mallow
*Malva neglecta* and *M. parviflora* (Small-flowered Mallow)

![Common Mallow (Malva neglecta) — round scalloped leaves and small pink-white flowers](images/native-plants/malva-neglecta.jpg)

**Identification**
Low-growing plants with round, shallowly lobed, scalloped-edged leaves on long petioles. Small five-petaled flowers (pale pink to white). The fruit is a round, segmented disc ("cheese wheel") that separates into wedge-shaped sections. Slightly mucilaginous quality.

**Where to Find It**
Disturbed areas, gardens, roadsides, waste ground throughout the Gulf Coast. Year-round in mild coastal areas; most abundant in spring and fall.

**Season**
Year-round in the Gulf Coast's mild climate. Best quality in cooler months.

**Edible Parts**
Leaves, flowers, young fruits ("cheeses"), shoots.

**Preparation**
Young leaves raw in salads (mild, slightly mucilaginous). Cooked as greens. Young fruits (the round discs) eaten raw as a trail snack. The mucilaginous quality thickens soups.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for the round scalloped leaves and cheese-wheel fruit.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Sea Rocket
*Cakile* spp. (*C. edentula*, *C. lanceolata*)

*[Photo needed: Cakile edentula or C. lanceolata — succulent branching coastal annual on sandy beach with four-petaled flowers. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A succulent, branching coastal annual (4–20 inches) with thick, slightly fleshy, pinnately lobed leaves and small four-petaled white to lavender flowers. Grows directly on coastal beaches and foredunes. Seed pods are distinctive: two-segmented, rocket-shaped.

**Where to Find It**
Ocean beaches, barrier islands, coastal dunes. Gulf Coast beaches.

**Season**
Spring through fall. Most tender in spring.

**Edible Parts**
Young leaves and shoots, young seed pods.

**Preparation**
Young leaves and pods raw in salads — flavor is mustard-like, spicy, slightly salty. Cooked as greens. The sea-salt environment concentrates flavor.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes on ocean beaches in this family. The characteristic mustard family flower and beach habitat are diagnostic.

---

## TREES AND SHRUBS

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Red Mulberry
*Morus rubra*

![Red Mulberry (Morus rubra) — variable-shaped leaves and dark red to black fruit](images/native-plants/morus-rubra.jpg)

**Identification**
Native tree (20–70 feet) with highly variable leaves — same tree simultaneously has simple oval, mitten-shaped (one-lobe), and multi-lobed leaves. Leaves alternate, toothed, and rough-textured on the upper surface. The fruit resembles a large elongated blackberry — 1–1½ inches, ripening from pink to deep red to nearly black when fully ripe.

**Where to Find It**
Bottomland forests, field edges, stream banks, fence lines, urban areas throughout the Gulf Coast. One of the most reliable and productive fruit trees of the region — a mature tree drops enormous quantities of fruit for several weeks. Common from Texas to Florida.

**Season**
April–June in the Gulf Coast — earlier than in northern regions (April–May in deep south Texas and Florida). A mature tree produces fruit abundantly.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit. Fully dark ripe berries are sweet and excellent; pink ones are tart.

**Preparation**
Fresh raw. Jam. Wine. Cordial. Pie. Freeze for year-round use. The flavor is rich, sweet, slightly wine-like.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for ripe dark mulberries on a tree. Unripe mulberries (white or pale pink) are mildly hallucinogenic/laxative — eat only when dark ripe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Elderberry
*Sambucus canadensis* (American Elder)

![Elderberry (Sambucus canadensis) — flat-topped white flower clusters in summer](images/native-plants/sambucus-canadensis.jpg)

**Identification**
A large shrub (6–12 feet) with opposite, pinnately compound leaves (5–7 leaflets, each lance-shaped with finely toothed edges). Large flat-topped flower clusters (corymbs) of tiny fragrant white flowers in early summer. Berries form in late summer — tiny dark purple-to-black spheres in heavy drooping flat-topped clusters.

**Where to Find It**
