Stream banks, forest edges, fence rows, disturbed moist areas, and floodplains throughout the Gulf Coast. Common in riparian corridors and moist lowland areas from Texas to Florida. Year-round foliage; berries ripen in fall.

**Season**
Flowers: May–July. Berries: August–October.

**Edible Parts**
Flowers and ripe berries. Do not eat unripe green berries. Do not eat leaves, bark, stems, or roots.

**Preparation**
Flowers: strip florets from stems, steep in sugar syrup with lemon for elderflower cordial. Berries: **always cook.** Elderberry syrup: simmer berries with water, ginger, cinnamon, cloves, strain, add honey off heat. Jelly, cordial, wine.

**Safety**
**Never eat elderberries raw** — cooking destroys the cyanogenic glycosides. Flat-topped cluster + dark purple-black berries = *S. canadensis* = edible when cooked. Distinguish from Red Elderberry (*S. racemosa*) — red berries in a domed cluster, toxic raw.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### American Persimmon
*Diospyros virginiana*

![American Persimmon (Diospyros virginiana) — orange persimmon fruit on bare branches](images/native-plants/diospyros-virginiana.jpg)

**Identification**
A medium tree (30–60 feet) with distinctive blocky, gray-to-black bark divided into small square or rectangular plates — one of the most recognizable bark patterns of any eastern tree. Alternate, oval, leathery leaves. The fruit is round, about 1–2 inches, turning from green to orange-yellow when ripe, often with a powdery bloom. Calyx (dried flower base) persistent at fruit top.

**Where to Find It**
Forest edges, old fields, roadsides, disturbed areas throughout the Gulf Coast and Florida. More widespread and productive here than in northern regions — common throughout all lowland and mid-elevation areas from the coast inland. Tolerates poor soil.

**Season**
Fruit ripens fall (September–November). Must be fully ripe — after the first cool spell is ideal. Unripe fruit is outrageously astringent.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Skin edible but thick. Seeds edible (roasted as coffee substitute).

**Preparation**
Fully ripe fruit: eat raw, pulp out seeds, use in pudding. Dry for persimmon candy. Pulp freezes well. Seeds: roast as coffee substitute. Unripe persimmon puckering is legendary — always taste a tiny amount first.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive blocky bark and the characteristic orange fruit. If it puckers your mouth, it is not ripe — wait for full ripening.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Saw Palmetto
*Serenoa repens*

![Saw Palmetto (Serenoa repens) — fan-shaped palm fronds and clusters of dark berries](images/native-plants/serenoa-repens.jpg)

**Identification**
A low, sprawling palm (3–7 feet) with fan-shaped, deeply divided fronds with sharply serrated petioles (the "saw" in saw palmetto — these petioles can cut deeply). Dense clusters of small, oval, dark berries (¾ inch) ripen in fall.

**Where to Find It**
Sandy coastal areas, pine flatwoods, scrublands throughout Florida and the Gulf Coast.

**Season**
Berries ripen September–November. Heart (terminal bud): year-round but harvesting kills the plant.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries (processed), palm heart (survival food — kills the plant to harvest).

**Preparation**
Berries: extremely bitter and astringent fresh — traditionally dried and ground as a nutritive food. Commercial saw palmetto extract from these berries is sold as a supplement. The raw berries are not pleasant eating. In survival situations, the sweet, starchy palm heart (cabbage) can be harvested from the growing tip of the stem — requires cutting and is destructive (kills plant).

**Safety**
The serrated petiole edges are a genuine hazard — approach with heavy gloves and care. The berries are not poisonous but are very unpleasant fresh.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Yaupon Holly
*Ilex vomitoria*

![Yaupon Holly (Ilex vomitoria) — small red berries and small dark green leathery leaves](images/native-plants/ilex-vomitoria.jpg)

**Identification**
A native shrub or small tree (6–20 feet) with small, alternate, dark evergreen leaves with shallow rounded teeth. Clusters of small red berries (¼ inch) in fall. The specific epithet "vomitoria" refers to high-dose ceremonial use — this is **not** a regular concern at normal tea-brewing quantities.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal forests, scrublands, and disturbed areas throughout the Gulf Coast and southeastern states. Very common.

**Season**
Leaves: year-round. Berries: fall.

**Edible Parts**
Dried and roasted leaves only (brewed as tea). The berries are NOT consumed.

**Preparation**
**Leaves only, as tea.** Yaupon is the only native North American plant known to contain significant caffeine. Dry and lightly roast leaves. Steep in hot water. The flavor resembles green tea or yerba maté. This was a major trade commodity among southeastern tribes and was used ceremonially throughout the Southeast.

**Safety**
Do not eat the red berries — they are a cathartic (cause vomiting). Do not over-brew the tea to extreme concentration. Normal tea brewing amounts are safe and have been used for thousands of years.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Sea Grape
*Coccoloba uvifera*

![Sea Grape (Coccoloba uvifera) — large round leaves and clusters of purple grapes](images/native-plants/coccoloba-uvifera.jpg)

**Identification**
A distinctive coastal tree or shrub (up to 30 feet) with enormous, round, leathery leaves (6–10 inches across) with red veins. Clusters of small round berries resemble a bunch of grapes — green ripening to purple. Grows directly on ocean beaches and coastal dunes. Leaves may be used as plates in a survival situation.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal beaches and dunes of southern Florida and the Florida Keys. Limited to tropical coastal environments.

**Season**
Berries ripen in summer–fall.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe purple berries (thin flesh around a large seed).

**Preparation**
Fresh raw — the thin flesh is sweet-tart with grape-like flavor. Jelly (traditional in south Florida). The large seed makes mass eating tedious.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes with the distinctive enormous round leaves and coastal beach habitat.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Cabbage Palm / Sabal Palm
*Sabal palmetto*

![Cabbage Palm (Sabal palmetto) — tall palm trunk with fan-shaped fronds](images/native-plants/sabal-palmetto.jpg)

**Identification**
The state tree of Florida and South Carolina — a tall (up to 65 feet), single-trunked palm with fan-shaped (costapalmate) fronds. The trunk develops a shaggy, boot-covered appearance from old frond bases. Small, round, dark berries in large hanging clusters.

**Where to Find It**
Coastal areas, wet flatwoods, river swamps, and disturbed areas throughout Florida and the Gulf Coast.

**Season**
Berries: fall. Palm heart (cabbage): year-round (harvesting kills the tree).

**Edible Parts**
Palm heart (the terminal bud — survival food, requires killing the tree), berries, young flower stalks.

**Preparation**
Palm heart (cabbage): harvest from the top of the trunk — the compact, white, layered bud. Eat raw or cooked. Mild, crisp, slightly sweet. This is the "heart of palm" sold commercially.

Berries: small, dark, slightly sweet — eat fresh or use for tea/beverage.

**Safety**
Harvesting the palm heart kills the tree — ethically, this is a survival food only. Do not harvest mature trees unless in a genuine survival situation.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Mayhaw
*Crataegus aestivalis* and *C. opaca*

*[Photo needed: Crataegus aestivalis — small red spring-ripening haws on thorny branches in Gulf Coast bottomland. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A hawthorn species native to the Gulf Coast swamps and stream banks. The fruit is smaller than most haws (¼–½ inch), bright red, and ripens unusually early — in April–May ("May haw"). Same leaf, thorn, and fruit structure as other hawthorns (small thorny tree with white flowers, lobed leaves, small apple-like fruit) but the early-spring ripening is diagnostic.

**Where to Find It**
Wet bottomlands, swamp margins, flooded forests of the Gulf Coastal Plain. Most productive in Louisiana and eastern Texas — the heartland of mayhaw country. Fruit often collected by spreading nets under trees or scooping from water (flooded haws float downstream and accumulate at bends).

**Season**
Fruit ripens April–May. This early timing makes Mayhaw unique — a spring fruit harvest when few other trees are fruiting. Collect by shaking or gathering from flood water.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe fruit flesh. Do not chew seeds.

**Preparation**
Mayhaw jelly is a celebrated regional specialty throughout Louisiana, Mississippi, and Georgia — the high natural pectin and tart flavor produce an outstanding jelly. Also used for preserves, cordial, and wine. The flavor is tart and apple-like.

**Safety**
Do not chew seeds (cyanogenic). All hawthorn fruit is edible when ripe.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Wild Blueberry and Sparkleberry
*Vaccinium* spp. (*V. arboreum*, *V. corymbosum*, *V. myrsinites*)

**Identification**
Multiple native *Vaccinium* species grow in the Gulf Coast and Florida. Sparkleberry (*V. arboreum*) is the most distinctive: a small tree or large shrub (up to 20 feet) with thick, semi-evergreen leaves and very small, dark blue-black berries (¼ inch) that are hard and dry rather than juicy. Florida blueberry (*V. myrsinites*) is a low, evergreen shrub with small sweet berries. All *Vaccinium* species have the five-pointed crown (calyx) at the berry end — the key distinguishing feature from any toxic lookalike.

**Where to Find It**
Sandy, acidic, well-drained soils — pine flatwoods, sandhills, scrub, oak woodland edges throughout the Gulf Coast and Florida. Sparkleberry is common throughout the entire region. Florida blueberry is widespread in the Florida peninsula.

**Season**
*V. arboreum* berries ripen October–December (a fall-winter crop). *V. myrsinites* ripens April–June.

**Edible Parts**
Ripe berries. Sparkleberry is edible but dry and less sweet — better cooked or dried than eaten raw. Florida blueberry berries are sweet and excellent.

**Preparation**
Sweet species: fresh raw, jam, pie, dried. Sparkleberry: better for jelly or cooked preparations due to dry texture.

**Safety**
The five-pointed crown (calyx) at the blossom end of the berry distinguishes all *Vaccinium* species from any toxic lookalike. No dangerous lookalikes once the crown is confirmed.

---

## FUNGI

The hot, humid Gulf Coast climate supports year-round fungal activity in appropriate conditions.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Oyster Mushroom
*Pleurotus ostreatus*

![Oyster Mushroom (Pleurotus ostreatus) — white to gray shelf-like mushrooms on dead wood](images/native-plants/pleurotus-ostreatus.jpg)

**Identification**
Shelf-like mushrooms growing in overlapping clusters on dead or dying hardwood trees. Cap is fan-to-oyster-shaped, white to gray to tan, 2–8 inches across. **True gills** run down the stem (decurrent), white to cream colored. Stem is short, off-center or absent. White spore print. Found on dead wood.

**Where to Find It**
Dead or dying hardwoods throughout the Gulf Coast — oak, willow, cypress, sweetgum, and pecan. Year-round production in the Gulf Coast — the heat and humidity that challenge other mushroom species actually suits oyster mushrooms. Most productive in fall and spring, but flushes occur after significant rain events at any time of year.

**Season**
Year-round in the Gulf Coast. Peak fall and spring.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body. Most tender when young.

**Preparation**
Sauté, roast, stir-fry. Mild, pleasant flavor. Dries well. One of the most widely cultivated mushrooms — the wild version has identical flavor and food value.

**Safety**
No toxic lookalikes for white to gray fan-shaped clusters on dead wood with white decurrent gills. The angel wings fungus (*Pleurocybella porrigens*) is white and on conifer wood — edible but best avoided by beginners. Gulf Oyster grows on hardwood, not conifers.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chanterelle
*Cantharellus cibarius* and *C. cinnabarinus* (Cinnabar Chanterelle)

![Chanterelle (Cantharellus cibarius) — golden mushroom with blunt forking ridges](images/native-plants/cantharellus-cibarius.jpg)

**Identification**
Golden-yellow to egg-yolk orange mushrooms with a wavy, irregular cap margin, often funnel-shaped with age. The critical feature: **false gills** — beneath the cap are blunt, forking ridges (not thin knife-like gills) that run partway down the stem. These ridges are thick, widely spaced, and fork repeatedly. Flesh is solid white. Fruity, apricot-like smell. The Cinnabar Chanterelle (*C. cinnabarinus*) is a small, distinctly red-orange chanterelle with the same blunt ridges — same edible quality.

**Where to Find It**
Under oaks and mixed forest throughout the Gulf Coast. Gulf Coast chanterelles appear in fall and winter (November–February), opposite timing from northern regions. Most productive after fall rain events under oaks and pines.

**Season**
Fall through winter (November–February). Most productive after rain.

**Edible Parts**
Entire fruiting body.

**Preparation**
Do not wash vigorously. Brush clean or use damp cloth. Sauté in butter 8–12 minutes until golden. Excellent in cream sauces and pasta.

**Safety**
**Jack-o-Lantern (*Omphalotus illudens*)** — POISONOUS — is orange-yellow and similar, but: grows in clusters at tree bases or from buried wood, has **true thin gills** (not blunt ridges), gills glow faintly in the dark. Causes severe vomiting and diarrhea. **Rule: verify the blunt forking ridges. Chanterelles do not cluster and do not grow from wood.**

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Chicken of the Woods
*Laetiporus sulphureus*

![Chicken of the Woods (Laetiporus sulphureus) — bright orange-yellow shelf fungus on tree trunk](images/native-plants/laetiporus-sulphureus.jpg)

**Identification**
Unmistakable: bright orange-to-yellow shelf-like brackets growing in overlapping tiers directly from tree trunks or stumps. Top surface is vibrant orange; underside is pale yellow to cream with tiny pores (no gills). When fresh, the flesh is tender, moist, and white.

**Where to Find It**
Very common on oaks throughout the Gulf Coast. Also found on cypress, willow, and other hardwoods. Can be found in fall and winter months during mild weather — the Gulf Coast's mild winters allow production outside of the typical fall window. A large find can yield 20–40 lbs of edible mushroom.

**Season**
Fall through winter (October–February) in the Gulf Coast. Most productive fall.

**Edible Parts**
Young, tender outer edges of fresh specimens. Avoid the tough inner portions near the wood.

**Preparation**
Sauté in butter or olive oil. The flavor and texture genuinely resemble chicken. Use in stir-fries, tacos, sandwiches, pasta. Must be fully cooked — some people experience gastrointestinal reactions to undercooked specimens.

**Safety**
Some people react to specimens growing on certain tree species (black locust, eucalyptus). Try a small amount first. No dangerous lookalikes — the color, shelf form, and pore surface (not gills) are distinctive.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Giant Puffball
*Calvatia gigantea*

![Giant Puffball (Calvatia gigantea) — smooth white globe-shaped fungus in field](images/native-plants/calvatia-gigantea.jpg)

**Identification**
A large, round to oval, white, featureless sphere growing from grassland or forest edges — ranging from softball to basketball size. No cap, no stem, no gills. When sliced vertically, the interior must be **pure, uniform white throughout** — like a marshmallow. Any other color inside means do not eat.

**Where to Find It**
Old fields, disturbed areas, open woodland edges, and pastures throughout the Gulf Coast, most commonly in fall. Appears after significant rain events in cooler months.

**Season**
Fall through early winter (October–January in the Gulf Coast). Must be harvested when completely white inside.

**Edible Parts**
Entire interior when pure white.

**Preparation**
Slice thickly, season with salt and pepper, pan-fry in butter until golden. Add to soups. Slice thin and use like a cracker.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL:** The pure white interior requirement is absolute. If you can see any outline of a developing gill structure inside (which means it might be a deadly *Amanita* button — also round and white but with developing structure inside), DO NOT EAT IT. Always slice before eating.

---

<div style="page-break-before: always"></div>

### Meadow Mushroom
*Agaricus campestris* and *A. xanthodermus* (TOXIC — see below)

*[Photo needed: Agaricus campestris — white-capped mushroom in lawn/meadow with pink gills. Source: iNaturalist CC0.]*

**Identification**
A classic white-capped mushroom of lawns and meadows: 2–4 inch smooth white cap, gills turning pink then dark brown as spores mature, ring on the stem (partial veil remnant), no volva (cup) at the base. **Key test:** cut the cap or base — flesh turns **slightly pink** or does not change color (not yellow). Smell is pleasantly mushroom-like, not chemical.

**Where to Find It**
Lawns, meadows, pastures, parks throughout the Gulf Coast, most commonly in fall and spring.

**Season**
Fall and spring (after rain). Gulf Coast warmth produces multiple flushes.

**Edible Parts**
Entire mushroom.

**Preparation**
Sauté, add to pasta, pizza, soups. The cultivated white button mushroom is a relative.

**Safety**
**CRITICAL LOOKALIKE — TWO DANGERS:**
1. **Destroying Angel / Death Cap (*Amanita* spp.)** — DEADLY. *Amanita* species have a cup (volva) at the base. Always dig up the entire mushroom to check. If the base has a cup, do not eat it.
2. **Yellow-staining Meadow Mushroom (*A. xanthodermus*)** — TOXIC — especially common in the Gulf Coast and Southeast. The base flesh turns **bright yellow** when cut and smells strongly phenolic (like carbolic acid, ink, or chemicals). **Rule: if it smells like chemicals or turns yellow when cut at the base, do not eat it.**

---

---

# APPENDIX: UNIVERSAL SAFETY RULES

## The Six Questions Before You Eat

Before consuming any wild plant or mushroom, ask:
1. Have I positively identified this specimen by at least three independent characteristics?
2. Have I confirmed there are no dangerous lookalikes in my region?
3. Have I cross-referenced this identification with a regional field guide?
4. If required, have I prepared it correctly (leached, cooked, removed seeds, etc.)?
5. Am I in a location where harvesting is legal?
6. Have I taken only a sustainable amount?

If any answer is "no" — don't eat it.

---

## Most Dangerous Lookalike Pairs

| Edible | Toxic Lookalike | Key Distinguishing Feature |
|---|---|---|
| Common Camas (blue flowers) | Death Camas (white/cream flowers) | Flower color: blue-violet = safe; white/cream = fatal |
| True Morel (fully hollow) | False Morel / Gyromitra (chambered inside) | Interior: cut vertically — fully hollow = safe |
| Chanterelle (blunt ridges) | Jack-o-Lantern (true thin gills, clusters, glows) | Ridges vs. gills; solitary vs. clustered; soil vs. wood |
| Ramps (onion smell) | Lily of the Valley (no smell, toxic) | Crush leaf: onion smell = safe; no smell = toxic |
| Wild Carrot (carrot smell) | Poison Hemlock (purple spots, no carrot smell) | Smell test + spotted stem; Apiaceae family = expert-confirm only |
| Meadow Mushroom (pink cut) | Destroying Angel / Death Cap (cup at base) | Cut base: pink = safe (potentially); dig up entire stem, no volva |
| Meadow Mushroom (pink cut) | Yellow-staining Agaricus (yellow cut, chemical smell) | Cut base: yellow = toxic; chemical/phenolic smell = toxic |
| Staghorn Sumac (red upright clusters, dry habitat) | Poison Sumac (white/green hanging clusters, swamp) | Habitat + berry color/orientation — opposite on both counts |
| Elderberry (flat-topped cluster, dark berries) | Red Elderberry (domed cluster, red berries) | Cluster shape + berry color; red elderberry = toxic raw |
| Wild Grape (tendrils present) | Canada Moonseed (no tendrils, crescent seed) | Tendrils = grape; no tendrils = potentially moonseed |
| Giant Puffball (pure white inside) | Amanita button (structured inside) | Always cut vertically: pure white solid = safe; any structure inside = do not eat |

---

## Plants Requiring Preparation Before Eating

| Plant | Required Preparation | Reason |
|---|---|---|
| Stinging Nettle | Cook before flowering; do not eat post-flower | Neutralizes sting; post-flower develops kidney toxins |
| Acorns | Leach tannins in water (days to weeks) | Astringent tannins block nutrient absorption |
| Elderberries | Always cook | Raw ripe berries mildly toxic to some; unripe = toxic |
| Morel Mushrooms | Always cook | Raw morels cause gastrointestinal upset |
| Wild Milkweed | Always cook | Raw plants mildly toxic; cooking neutralizes |
| Mayapple | Ripe yellow fruit only — no other parts | All other parts contain cytotoxic podophyllotoxin |
| Mountain Ash berries | Cook or thoroughly dry | Raw contain parasorbic acid |
| Toyon berries | Cook or thoroughly dry | Raw contain cyanogenic compounds |
| Chokecherry / Wild Cherry seeds | Never chew seeds; strain from juice | Cyanogenic glycosides in pits |
| Wild Carrot (Queen Anne's Lace) | Expert ID required — Apiaceae family is dangerous | Multiple deadly lookalikes in same family |
| Camas bulbs | Confirm blue-violet flowers on site before harvest | Death Camas in same habitat cannot be distinguished by bulb alone |
| Shaggy Mane | Do not combine with alcohol | Coprine reaction with alcohol |
| Jerusalem Artichoke | Start with small amounts; gas-causing | Inulin causes significant digestive discomfort in quantity |

---

## Quick Reference: Calorie-Dense Survival Foods by Region

| Region | High-Calorie Priorities |
|---|---|
| Northeast | Black Walnut, Hickory, Acorns, Jerusalem Artichoke, Groundnut |
| Southeast | Pecan, Black Walnut, Pawpaw, Groundnut, Acorns |
| Midwest | Acorns, Hickory, Black Walnut, Jerusalem Artichoke, Groundnut |
| Great Plains | Prairie Turnip, Pine Nuts, Hackberry (ground), Buffalo Berry |
| Southwest | Mesquite flour, Pinon nuts, Prickly Pear, Saguaro, Amaranth seeds |
| Pacific NW | Camas bulbs (cooked), Huckleberry, Salal, King Bolete, Matsutake |
| Rocky Mtns | Pinon nuts, Acorns (Gambel Oak), Serviceberry, Huckleberry, Porcini |
| California | Acorns (multiple oaks), California Bay nuts, Pine nuts, Chanterelles |
| Gulf Coast | Pawpaw, Pecan, Persimmon, Cattail, American Lotus, Sea Grape |

## Recommended Regional Field Guides

*Northeast:* Samuel Thayer, *The Forager's Harvest* and *Nature's Garden*. Lee Allen Peterson, *A Field Guide to Edible Wild Plants*

*Southeast/Appalachia:* Marc Williams, *Foraging the Southeast*. Samuel Thayer's general guides

*Midwest:* Peterson guide; Regional university extension publications

*Great Plains:* Kelly Kindscher, *Edible Wild Plants of the Prairie*

*Southwest:* John Slattery, *Southwest Foraging*; Wendy Hodgson, *Food Plants of the Sonoran Desert*

*Pacific Northwest:* Langdon Cook, *The Mushroom Hunters*; Robin Wall Kimmerer, *Braiding Sweetgrass*

*Rocky Mountains:* Thomas Elpel, *Botany in a Day*

*California:* Alyce Santoro, *California Foraging*; Pascal Baudar, *The New Wildcrafted Cuisine*

*For mushrooms in any region:* David Arora, *Mushrooms Demystified*
